Do the Akwan Belong to Touch?
Commentator presents a subsidiary disagreement on whether akwan are perceived by touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subsidiary scholarly disagreement about whether the akwan (accidents of motion and rest) are objects of the sense of touch, and the argument used by those who affirm it.

Core Idea
Some scholars argue that touch perceives the akwan because a moving object is sensed by touch.

After the main discussion, the commentator adds a related point: The akwan (motion, rest, and other accidents) are, by agreement, objects of sight. But is touch also able to perceive them? Some scholars said yes, giving the example of touching a moving object—the motion is felt. This brief passage shows how the principle of universal perception is applied to specific attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic text from 'وقد اختلف الأصحاب' to the end. Notice the agreement that akwan are seen, and the disagreement about touch.
The text ends abruptly; the argument is incomplete.
2
Understand the issue
The akwan are a category of accidents (motion, rest, etc.) that are seen by all. The question is whether touch also perceives them.
3
Evaluate the proof
The proof given: if you touch something that is moving, you perceive the motion (it quivers under your hand). This suggests touch perceives the akwan.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقاتُ السمع والبصر والإدراك ]
وقولُه : ( والسمع والبصر والإدراك ـ على القولِ بهِ ـ بكل موجود ) يعني : أنَّ هذهِ الصفاتِ الثلاثَ في حق الله تعالى تتعلَّقُ بكل موجود ، وإنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها في حقنا خاصاً ببعض الموجوداتِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلك الخصوص عادي لا عقلي .
أمَّا البصرُ : فاتَّفَقَ أَهلُ السَّنَّةِ على جواز تعلقِهِ بكل موجود ،
واختلفوا في جواز تعلَّقِ ما عدا الرؤيةَ مِنَ الإدراكات بكل موجود ؛ فذهب القدماء منهم ؛ كعبدِ الله بن سعيد الكلابي والقلانسي : إلى أنَّ هذا العموم مختص بالرؤيةِ ، وبقية الإدراكات لا يجوز أن تعم الموجوداتِ ، ونُقِلَ عن الشيخ أبي الحسنِ مخالفتهما في ذلك ، وصارَ إلى جواز عموم كلّ إدراك لكل موجود ، ومذهب الشيخ أبي الحسنِ إمامِ أهلِ السنَّةِ وإليهِ يُنسبون سلكتُ في هذه العقيدة
ونُقِلَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدٍ أَنَّهُ لَمَّا خص تعلق السمعِ بالأصواتِ ذهب إلى أنَّ الكلامَ الأزليَّ لا يصح أن يُسمَعَ ؛ يعني والله أعلمُ : بل يُدرَكُ بصفةِ العلم ، وفي قولِهِ ذلكَ مخالفة لقواطع السمعِ والشيخ أبو الحسن رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ لمَّا قالَ : ( إدراك السمع يعم كلَّ موجود ( جوّزَ تعلقه بكلام الله تعالى ، وقال بوقوع هذا الجائز على ما ورد السمع به في حقٌّ موسى عليه السلام ، وعمدة الشيخ في ذلك : ما يأتي تقريرُهُ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فصل الرؤيةِ ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ الوجود هو المصحح للرؤية .
وقد اختلف الأصحابُ في الأكوان التي هي متعلق للرؤية في وقتنا اتفاقاً : هل هي مُتعلَّق للمس أم لا ؟ فذهبَ بعضُهم إلى أنَّ إدراك اللمس يتعلَّق بها ، واحتج بأنَّ مَنْ لمس شيئاً واضطرب تحت يده أدرك
The attachments of hearing, sight, and perception. And his statement: 'And hearing, sight, and perception—on the view of it—[relate] to every existing thing.' He means: that these three attributes in the case of Allah the Exalted are attached to every existing thing, even if each one of them in our case is specific to some existing things, because that specificity is customary not rational. As for sight: the people of the Sunnah agreed upon the permissibility of its attachment to every existing thing. And they disagreed about the permissibility of attachment of perceptions other than sight to every existing thing. The ancients among them, like 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id al-Kulaybi and al-Qalanisi, held that this generality is specific to sight, and that the remaining perceptions are not permitted to encompass existing things. And it was transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan that he differed from them in that, and he held the permissibility of generality of every perception for every existing thing. And the madhhab of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, the Imam of the people of the Sunnah and to whom they are attributed, I followed in this creed. And it was transmitted from 'Abdallah ibn Sa'id that when he specified the attachment of hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech cannot be heard; meaning, and Allah knows best, that it is perceived by the attribute of knowledge. In that statement of his is an opposition to decisive proofs of revelation. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan, may Allah be pleased with him, when he said: 'The perception of hearing encompasses every existing thing,' permitted its attachment to the speech of Allah the Exalted, and he said that this permissible occurs according to what revelation has reported in the case of Musa, peace be upon him. And the foundation of the Shaykh in that is what will be established, Allah willing, in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence is the rectifier for vision. And the companions disagreed about the akwan which are the object of vision in our time by agreement: are they an object of touch or not? Some of them held that the perception of touch attaches to them, and he argued that whoever touches something and it quivers under his hand perceives...
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory and argumentative passage about the objects of the divine attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and perception (idrak). It begins by interpreting the author's statement that these three attributes, on the view that perception exists as an attribute in Allah, attach to every existing thing (mawjud). The commentator explains that this is true in the case of Allah, even though these attributes in humans are specific to certain types of existing things; that specificity is due to custom, not rationality. The passage then focuses on the disagreements among Sunni scholars. It notes unanimous agreement that sight attaches to every existing thing. However, there is disagreement regarding other perceptions. The earlier scholars like Ibn Kullab and al-Qalanisi held that only sight is universal; other perceptions are not universal. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari opposed them and held that every perception is universal. The commentator states that he follows al-Ash'ari's madhhab in this creed. The passage then reports a specific opinion from Ibn Kullab: because he restricted hearing to sounds, he held that eternal speech (kalam azali) cannot be heard but is perceived by knowledge. The commentator notes that this contradicts clear revelatory proofs. Al-Ash'ari, by contrast, allowed hearing to encompass all existing things, including the speech of Allah, and affirmed that this actually occurred in the case of Musa (AS). The foundation for al-Ash'ari is the coming discussion in the chapter on ru'ya: that existence (wujud) is what makes vision possible. Finally, the passage discusses a subsidiary issue: the akwan (accidents of motion/rest) which are seen by agreement—are they also objects of touch? Some argued yes, using the proof that a person who touches something in motion perceives it, implying that touch perceives that motion.

Key Terms
أكوان
لمس
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the view that the perception of touch attaches to the akwan?
A) Scriptural evidence.
B) Rational necessity.
C) Empirical observation of touching a moving object.
D) Consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
Some scholars argue that touch perceives the akwan, as evidenced by feeling motion when touching a moving object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel your heart beat or your hand move, do you consciously acknowledge that these motions are created by Allah each instant? How can you cultivate this awareness throughout your day?
?
The scholars debated whether touch perceives motion. Have you ever considered that your own senses are a gift from Allah that testify to His constant creation? What would it mean for you to live with that consciousness?
?
This passage shows that even the perception of motion is a matter of theological inquiry. How often do you pause to reflect on the ordinary experiences of your body as signs of Allah's power? Is that a habit you need to develop?

The First View on Seeing Vision and Refuting the Sleep Argument
Sanusi presents the first view and refutes a counterargument using sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first scholarly view on whether vision itself can be seen, the infinite regress objection, and why the answer invoking sleep fails.

Core Idea
The infinite regress of hindrances is simultaneous, not sequential, making the sleep answer invalid.

This passage dives into a specific theological debate about divine vision. Sanusi presents the first view—that vision is absolutely possible to be seen—and then tackles an objection involving infinite regress. He refutes a common answer that Allah cuts the regress by creating sleep, clarifying the true nature of the hindrance series.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first view
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states the first school: vision can be seen absolutely, and if it is not seen, it's due to a hindrance.
Note the reference to 'al-Shaykh' (al-Ash'ari) and the answer from al-Qadi.
2
Examine the infinite regress objection
Understand the objection: if vision is seen, that vision itself would need to be seen, leading to an infinite regress.
Sanusi mentions that al-Qadi's earlier answer resolves this, but the specific answer is not repeated here.
3
Study the alternative answer using sleep
Another scholar proposed that Allah cuts the regress by creating sleep, which prevents perception, thus stopping the chain.
Sleep is presented as a hindrance to perception, similar to death or fainting.
4
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi refutes this by distinguishing simultaneous hindrances from sequential ones. The regress here is of infinite hindrances existing together in one atomic moment, not one after another.
Sleep etc. cannot remove simultaneous hindrances; they would only work if the regress were sequential.
5
Grasp the deeper point
Even if the regress were sequential, it would not be impossible—it would be like the endless bliss or punishment in the afterlife.
This reinforces the idea that the objection is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : قد اختلف علماؤنا في هذه المسألة على مذاهب (۳) : الأول : مذهبُ الشيخ أنَّ الرؤية يجوز أن تُرى مطلقاً ، وحيث لم تُرَ فلمانع ، وما لزمَ مِنَ التسلسل فجوابه ما سبق عنِ القاضي . وأجابَ غيرُهُ : بأنَّ الله تعالى يقطعُ تلك السلسلة يخلق النوم ، وهو عندَهُ يُضادُّ الإدراك متى شاءَ ؛ بأنْ . قلتُ : وهو مردودٌ ; لأنَّ السلسلة التي لزمَتْ إِنَّما هي وجود موانع لا نهاية لها مجتمعةً لا مرتبةً (٤) ، فلم يجي النوم ونحوهُ مِنَ الموتِ والغشية وما في معناها حتى لزم المحالُ ; وهو اجتماع موانع لا نهاية لها في الزمان الفردِ ، وإِنَّما يصح الجواب بالنوم ونحوه لو كانَتِ السلسلة اللازمة هي سلسلة الترتيب (۱) ; بأنْ يُوجَدَ بعدَ كلِّ مانعِ مانع ، على أنه لو كانَتِ السلسلة اللازمة سلسلة الترتيب . . هي لزم محالٌ ; إذْ غايتُهُ لزوم عدم انقطاع الموانع في المستقبل ، وذلك لا استحالة فيه ; كنعيم أهل الجنة ، وعذابِ أهل النارِ .
I said: Our scholars differed on this question according to schools (3): The first: the school of al-Shaykh that vision may be seen absolutely, and where it is not seen, it is due to a hindrance, and what follows of infinite regress, its answer is what preceded from al-Qadi. And another answered: that Allah, exalted, cuts that series by creating sleep, which in His view contradicts perception whenever He wills; by that. I said: And it is refuted; because the series that follows is only the existence of infinite hindrances simultaneously not sequentially (4), so sleep and the like – death, fainting, and what is in their meaning – did not come such that the impossible follows; which is the conjunction of infinite hindrances in an atomic moment. And the answer by sleep and the like is only valid if the necessary series were a series of order (1); such that after every hindrance a hindrance exists, and even if the necessary series were a series of order, it would be impossible; for its end is the non-interruption of hindrances in the future, and there is no impossibility in that; like the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three scholarly views on whether vision (ru'yat Allah) itself can be seen. Sanusi states that the view of al-Shaykh (al-Ash'ari) is that vision is absolutely capable of being seen; if it is not seen, that is due to a hindrance. The objection of infinite regress (if vision is seen, then that vision is also seen, ad infinitum) is answered by al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) in a way previously mentioned. Another scholar (non al-Qadi) responds that Allah cuts the regress by creating sleep, which contradicts perception (thus the second vision is not perceived). Sanusi refutes this: the regress here is of simultaneous hindrances (all existing together), not sequential. Sleep etc. do not remove the simultaneous hindrances; rather, the impossibility is the coexistence of infinite hindrances in one atomic moment. The sleep argument would work if the regress were a temporal sequence of hindrances, but even then it would not be impossible, as it resembles the endless state of Paradise and Hell. Sanusi thus defends al-Ash'ari's view by clarifying the nature of the regress.

Key Terms
رؤية
تسلسل
مانع
نوم
إدراك
زمان فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress of hindrances is a temporal sequence (one after another), so a single hindrance like sleep can stop it.
Correction: Sanusi explains the regress is simultaneous – infinite hindrances all existing at once in an atomic moment – so sleep cannot break it because it only adds another hindrance.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's refutation, what is the nature of the series of hindrances that follows from the first view?
A) A temporal sequence of hindrances
B) A simultaneous collection of infinite hindrances
C) A causal chain of hindrances
D) A logical hierarchy of hindrances

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress of hindrances in the first view is simultaneous, not sequential, so invoking sleep does not resolve it; the correct resolution lies in distinguishing simultaneous from sequential infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been a time when you assumed that your spiritual obstacles came one after another, like a sequential regress? How does this lesson's insight that they are simultaneous change your understanding of your need for Allah's direct intervention? Commit to spending a few minutes tonight asking Allah to lift all your veils at once, trusting in His absolute power.
?
The sleep argument was refuted because it relied on a misunderstanding of the regress. Are there any 'sleeps' in your life — states of heedlessness or distraction — that you use to avoid facing the full weight of your spiritual responsibilities? When will you stop depending on such escapes and confront the simultaneous reality of your dependence on Allah?
?
If the infinite regress were sequential, the lesson says it would not be impossible (like eternal bliss or punishment). Reflect: Do you sometimes fear that your struggles in faith will never end? How does the knowledge that an endless sequence is not impossible (but rather a sign of Allah's mercy or justice) give you hope or fear? What action will you take today to prepare for an eternal reality?

The Second View: Is Vision Itself Seeable?
Sanusi states and refutes the second view that vision is absolutely unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view regarding the visibility of the vision of God, its proof from infinite regress, and Sanusi's refutation by noting that existence qualifies for being seen.

Core Idea
If existence qualifies something to be seen, then infinite regress is harmless.

Sanusi now introduces the second theological position: that the vision of God (ru'yah) is itself absolutely impossible to be seen. This view invokes the familiar infinite regress argument. However, Sanusi points out a key flaw: the argument only works if we deny that existence (al-wujud) is a sufficient qualifier for being seen. Since vision certainly exists, the regress does not prove impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the structure of view->proof->refutation become clear.
2
Understand the second view's claim
The second view states that the act of seeing (ru'yah) cannot itself be seen under any circumstances. This is the position being refuted.
The word 'mutlaqan' means absolutely, i.e., in no way whatsoever.
3
Understand the proof: infinite regress
The proponents argue that if vision were seeable, then the vision of that vision would also need to be seen, leading to an infinite chain. This is considered impossible.
This is a standard argument used against any form of self-reference.
4
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi responds by pointing out that the infinite regress only refutes the view if one does not accept that existence (al-wujud) qualifies a thing to be seen. Since vision exists, it can be seen without problematic regress.
The phrase 'in kana yusallamu' indicates a conditional: the refutation depends on conceding this premise.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi not simply assert that existence qualifies? He is showing that the opponent's argument fails if they accept this basic premise. This reveals the importance of foundational assumptions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : امتناع كون الرؤيةِ مطلقاً مرئيَّةً ، وحُجَّتُهُ : ما سبقَ مِنَ التسلسل . قلتُ : وهو مردودٌ إِنْ كانَ يُسلَّمُ أَنَّ الوجودَ مُصحح للرؤية .
The second: the impossibility of vision being seen absolutely, and its proof: what preceded of infinite regress. I said: And it is refuted if it is conceded that existence qualifies for vision.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage states the second view: that it is absolutely impossible for vision (the act of seeing God) itself to be seen. Their proof is the infinite regress argument (if vision is seen, that vision must also be seen, ad infinitum). Sanusi refutes this in one sentence: this proof is rejected if one concedes that existence (al-wujud) qualifies something to be seen. Since vision exists, it is qualified to be seen, so the regress does not prove impossibility; rather, it would be a non-vicious regress. Thus the second view is insufficient if the premise that existence makes something seeable is accepted.

Key Terms
امتناع
رؤية
تسلسل
وجود
مصحح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that infinite regress always proves impossibility in theological contexts.
Correction: Infinite regress is only problematic if there is no way to break the chain. Sanusi shows that if existence qualifies, then each step is grounded and the regress is harmless.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's objection to the second view's proof?
A) The proof is circular
B) The proof only works if one accepts that existence qualifies for vision
C) The proof is based on a false premise
D) The proof is too weak

Key Takeaway
The soundness of the infinite regress argument depends on the premise about what qualifies a thing to be seen; if existence is the qualifier, the regress is not vicious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the act of hoping to see Allah on the Day of Judgment, do you ever consider what that vision itself is? Does your heart accept that it is a real existent thing that Allah can make seeable? How does this affect your longing for that moment?
?
The infinite regress argument is often used to reject possibilities. In your own spiritual life, are there times when you use reasoning to dismiss the possibility of experiencing Allah's closeness or His response to your prayers? What premise are you assuming that might be false?
?
Sanusi says the regress is refuted if existence qualifies. Do you truly believe that existence alone makes something perceptible to Allah? Does your worship reflect that belief, or do you act as though some actions are too 'small' or 'insignificant' for Allah to see? What would change if you truly believed that nothing is hidden from His sight, not even your inner thoughts?

The Third View's Internal Inconsistency
Sanusi refutes the third view on seeing vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third view on seeing vision—that one can see another's vision but not one's own—is logically inconsistent.

Core Idea
The third view either leads to infinite regress or becomes redundant.

Sanusi now turns to the third view on the visibility of vision. This view attempts to split the problem: it says you cannot see your own vision, but you can see another's. The proponent thinks this avoids infinite regress through annihilation. Sanusi shows that this position is internally inconsistent—it either falls into the same regress or collapses into the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the conditional 'if' statements in the refutation.
The Arabic has a footnote reference (٢) which you can ignore.
2
Identify the third view's claim
The view asserts two things: impossibility of seeing one's own vision, permissibility of seeing another's. Note the proponent's reasoning about annihilation of the second locus.
3
Trace Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi presents two conditional scenarios: if the proponent allows seeing the hindrance, regress follows; if not, the distinction collapses. Follow each branch.
This is the key analytical step.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The refutation shows the view is either inconsistent or redundant. Understand why neither possibility saves the view.
Connect this to the overall argument about seeing vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : استحالةُ أنْ يرى الإنسانُ رؤية نفسه ، وتجويز أن يرى رؤية غيرِه ، وكأنَّهُ يرى قائلُ هذا عدم لزوم التسلسل في رؤية الغيرِ ; لجواز أن يدرك الإنسانُ إدراك غيره أو لا يدركَهُ لمانع ، ثم يُعدِمَ اللهُ ذلك المحل الثاني الذي هو محلُّ الرؤية المدرَكةِ ، فتنعدم هي والموانع ، فينقطع التسلسلُ عند ذلك . قلتُ : ولا يخفى ضَعْفُ هذا الثالث أيضاً ; لأنَّهُ إِنْ كانَ يُجوِّزُ رؤية المانع فقد لزمَهُ مِنَ التسلسلِ عند عدم كون رؤية الغيرِ حال وجودها مرئية (٢).. ما لزمَ عند عدم كونِ رؤية نفسِهِ مرئية له ، وإنْ كانَ لا يُجوِّزُ رؤية الموانعِ فذلك يقطعُ التسلسل في رؤية نفسِهِ ورؤية غيره، كما ذكرناه عن القاضي في تصحيح قول الشيخ الأشعري
The third: the impossibility that a person sees his own vision, and the permissibility that he sees another's vision, and it is as if the proponent of this sees no necessity of infinite regress in seeing another's, because it is possible that a person perceives another's perception or does not perceive it due to a hindrance, then Allah annihilates that second locus which is the locus of the perceived vision, so it and the hindrances cease to exist, and so the regress is cut off. I said: And the weakness of this third is not hidden; because if he permits seeing the hindrance, then the infinite regress follows when another's vision is not visible at the time of its existence (2) the same as follows when one's own vision is not visible to him, and if he does not permit seeing hindrances, then that cuts the regress in seeing one's own and another's, as we mentioned from al-Qadi in correcting the statement of al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents the third view: a person cannot see his own vision (the act of seeing God in himself), but can see another person's vision. The proponent argues that infinite regress does not apply to seeing another's vision because the second locus (the vision being perceived) can be annihilated by Allah, stopping the regress. Sanusi refutes this: if the proponent allows seeing the hindrance (i.e., the vision that is being perceived itself may be seen), then the same regress follows for another's vision as for one's own. If the proponent does not allow seeing hindrances (i.e., the vision is not seeable at all), then that cuts the regress in both cases (own and another's), which is actually what al-Qadi said in correcting al-Ash'ari. Thus the third view is inconsistent.

Key Terms
استحالة
رؤية
تسلسل
مانع
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the third view avoids regress by appealing to annihilation, making it a viable alternative.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this leads to inconsistency: either it allows seeing hindrances (then regress applies) or not (then it is no different from the corrected Ash'ari view).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's refutation, what happens if the proponent of the third view allows seeing the hindrance (the vision itself)?
A) Infinite regress is avoided
B) Infinite regress follows for another's vision as well
C) The regress is cut off completely
D) The view becomes identical to the first view

Key Takeaway
The third view is internally inconsistent because it either leads to the same regress as one's own vision or collapses into the corrected Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems to offer an easy escape from a difficult implication, do you embrace it without examining its consistency? This lesson shows that such escapes often collapse or lead to other problems. Examine your own beliefs: are there any positions you hold because they feel comfortable rather than because they are logically coherent? Commit to studying the full implications of your views, even if it requires abandoning cherished ideas.
?
The third view attempted to avoid infinite regress by appealing to divine annihilation. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes hope that Allah will simply 'annihilate' the consequences of your inconsistencies rather than addressing them directly? How does relying on divine intervention to patch over intellectual or moral contradictions affect your sincerity in seeking truth? Resolve to confront contradictions in your faith with honesty, trusting that the truth is consistent.
?
Sanusi shows that the third view is either inconsistent or redundant—it adds nothing new. Are there areas in your own understanding of Islamic theology where you cling to a minority opinion or a novel interpretation not because it is stronger, but because it makes you feel distinct or independent? How does pride in your own views prevent you from fully submitting to the truth as established by the scholars? Ask Allah to purify your intention in seeking knowledge.

The Infinity of the Objects of Attributes
Proof that attributes' objects are infinite via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objects of God's attributes must be infinite in scope, based on a reductio ad absurdum argument that prevents arbitrary limitation.

Core Idea
God's attributes are unlimited in scope; limitation would require an impossible specifier.

This passage tackles a key question about divine attributes: are they limited in what they apply to? The author, Sanusi, presents a clever proof that they cannot be restricted. He shows that if an attribute were only related to some of its possible objects, that would turn a possibility into an impossibility, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step, clarifying why the objects of attributes must be infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the source Arabic and the literal English translation. Note the structure: it begins by stating the conclusion (infinity), then gives a brief argument, then explains the proof in more detail.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'متعلقات الصفات' and 'عموم التعلق'.
2
Identify the two 'demands' (matlab)
The passage mentions a 'second demand' (general relation of attributes) and a 'first demand' (unity of attributes). Understand that the second is proven here because some proofs of the first depend on it.
The author presents the second demand before the first due to logical dependence.
3
Analyze the main proof: conditional reasoning
The proof is a conditional: 'if an attribute were restricted to some of what it is suitable for, then the possible would become impossible.' Then it denies the consequent (impossible), so the antecedent (restriction) is also false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the entailment explanation
The author explains why the conditional holds: the part not related is equally suitable for relation as the part that is related. So restricting it would be arbitrary and unjustified.
This shows that restriction would not be a necessary outcome of the attribute's nature, but a contingent limitation.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for divine attributes: they are not limited in scope, but apply to everything they are suitable for. This supports the idea of God's perfection and omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عدم النهاية في متعلقات الصفات المتعلقة: فلأنها لو اختصت ببعض ما تصلح له لاستحال ما علم جوازه، وافتقرت إلى مخصص. هذا برهان على المطلب الثاني؛ وهو عموم التعلق للصفات وقدمه على المطلب الأول - وهو وحدة الصفات - لتوقف بعض أدلته عليه. وبيان ما أشار إليه من الدليل أن نقول: لو اختصت صفة - من صفاته تعالى المتعلقة - ببعض ما تصلح له لانقلب الجائز مستحيلا، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله. وبيان الملازمة: أن البعض الذي لم تتعلق به تلك الصفة مع صلاحية تعلقها به هي في صحة تعلقها به مثل البعض الذي تعلقت به، فقصر الصفة في التعلق على غيره منع لما علمت صحته.
As for the infinity in the objects of the attributes that are related: because if they were restricted to some of what they are suitable for, then what is known to be possible would become impossible, and they would need a specifier. This is a proof for the second demand; which is the generality of relation for the attributes, and he presented it before the first demand—which is the unity of the attributes—because some of its proofs depend on it. And the explanation of what he indicated of the proof is that we say: if an attribute—from among God's attributes that are related—were restricted to some of what it is suitable for, then the possible would turn impossible; the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. And the explanation of the entailment: that the part which that attribute did not relate to, despite its suitability for relation to it, is in the soundness of its relation to it like the part which it did relate to, so restricting the attribute in relation to something else is a prevention of what you know to be sound.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the infinity of the objects of God's attributes. It is a second demand (al-maṭlab al-thānī) concerning the generality of relation (ʿumūm al-taʿalluq) of the attributes. The author first states that if an attribute were restricted to only some of its possible objects, then what is known to be possible (i.e., relation to other objects) would become impossible, and this would require an arbitrary specifier. This is a reductio ad absurdum. He then explains the proof in more detail: if a particular attribute, which is capable of relating to multiple objects, only relates to some and not others, then those others are equally suitable for relation; so restricting it would be arbitrary and impossible. This shows that the attribute must be able to relate to all objects it is suitable for, implying infinity of objects (or at least unlimited scope).

Key Terms
متعلقات الصفات
عموم التعلق
وحدة الصفات
الاختصاص
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's attributes are limited in what they can relate to, e.g., that His knowledge only covers certain things.
Correction: This proof shows that attributes must relate to all objects they are suitable for, otherwise it would be an arbitrary limitation, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an attribute were restricted to only some of its possible objects, what would follow?
A) The attribute would be eternal.
B) The possible would become impossible.
C) The attribute would be simple.
D) The attribute would be one.

Key Takeaway
The objects of God's attributes must be infinite in scope because any limitation would require an arbitrary specifier and turn what is possible into an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are infinite in scope, then His knowledge encompasses every thought you have, even those you are ashamed of. How does that change the way you view your private moments? Will you live today as if nothing is hidden from Him?
?
The proof shows that any limitation on God's attributes would require an arbitrary specifier. Where in your heart have you placed limits on God's mercy, thinking some sins are beyond His forgiveness? What would it take for you to truly believe that His mercy is suitable for every sin without exception?
?
If the objects of God's attributes are infinite, then His justice applies to every wrong, no matter how small, but also His patience endures. Are you living as if God's justice is limited to 'big' sins while neglecting the 'small' ones? How will you adjust your repentance to reflect the infinite scope of His attributes?

First Proof: Restriction Makes the Possible Impossible
Sanusi presents the first proof for unlimited scope of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that divine attributes cannot be restricted to some objects, because such restriction would turn what is possible into something impossible.

Core Idea
Restricting a divine attribute to some objects makes the possible impossible.

Sanusi begins proving that the objects of divine attributes are unlimited with a powerful argument from impossibility. He shows that if an attribute were limited to only some of its suitable objects, it would arbitrarily prevent what is known to be possible. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the meaning of 'possible' as used here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key claim: restriction would make the possible impossible.
The proof is concise; the commentary expands on the logical step.
2
Understand the logical implication
Focus on the explanation of the implication: the part not attached to is equally suitable, so restricting is arbitrary prevention of what is known to be possible.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the gloss on 'possible'
Read the al-Manjur clarification: 'possible' here means absolute possibility (validity of existence), not special possibility (neither necessary nor impossible).
This prevents confusion with other uses of 'possible'.
4
Connect to the broader proof
Reflect on how this argument establishes that divine attributes must be universal in scope, not limited.
This is the first of two proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عدم النهاية في متعلقاتها: فلأنها لو اختصت ببعض ما تصلح له لاستحال ما علم جوازه، وافتقرت إلى مخصص. هذا برهان على المطلب الثاني؛ وهو عموم التعلق للصفات، وقدمه على المطلب الأول - وهو وحدة الصفات - لتوقف بعض أدلته عليه. وبيان ما أشار إليه من الدليل أن نقول: لو اختصت صفة - من صفاته تعالى المتعلقة - ببعض ما تصلح له لانقلب الجائز مستحيلاً، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله. وبيان الملازمة: أن البعض الذي لم تتعلق به تلك الصفة مع صلاحية تعلقها به هي في صحة تعلقها به مثل البعض الذي تعلقت به، فقصر الصفة في التعلق على غيره منع لما علمت صحته. قال العلامة المنجور في (حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الكبرى) (ص 162): أشار بقوله: «صحته» إلى الجواز في قوله: (لاستحال ما علم جوازه)، بمعنى الإمكان العام؛ وهو صحة وجود الشيء وجب وجوده أو لا، وليس المراد الإمكان الخاص؛ إذ تعلق القدرة بكل ممكن واجب، فالجواز إذاً فيه بمعنى صحة ثبوته، لا بمعنى أنه يصح ثبوته ونفيه. وأيضاً: فتخصيص الصفات ببعض ما جاز أن تتعلق به يوجب افتقارها إلى مخصص مختار؛ لاستواء الجميع بالنسبة إليها، وذلك يوجب حدوثها، وقد سبق البرهان على وجوب القدم والبقاء لذاته تعالى ولجميع صفاته.
As for the absence of limit in their objects: it is because if a (divine) attribute were restricted to some of what it is suitable for, then what is known to be possible would become impossible, and it would need a specifier. This is a proof for the second claim, which is the universality of attachment for the attributes, and he presented it before the first claim—which is the unity of the attributes—because some of its proofs depend on it. The explanation of what he indicated from the proof is that we say: if an attribute—from His exalted attributes that are attached—were restricted to some of what it is suitable for, then the possible would become impossible, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. The explanation of the implication is that the part which that attribute does not attach to, despite the suitability of its attachment to it, is in the validity of its attachment to it like the part to which it is attached. So restricting the attribute in attachment to something other than it is a prevention of what you know to be valid. The scholar al-Manjur said in his gloss on Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra (p. 162): He indicated by his saying 'its validity' the permissibility in his saying 'what is known to be possible would become impossible' in the sense of absolute possibility, which is the validity of existence of a thing whether its existence is necessary or not; and what is meant is not special possibility, because the attachment of power to every possible is necessary. So permissibility here is in the sense of the validity of its affirmation, not in the sense that its affirmation and negation are both valid. And also: specifying the attributes to some of what it is possible for them to attach to necessitates that they need a voluntary specifier, because all (objects) are equal with respect to them, and that necessitates their temporality, and the proof has already preceded for the necessity of pre-eternity and everlastingness for His essence—exalted is He—and for all His attributes.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the claim that the objects of divine attributes are unlimited (no finality). The author first states the thesis: there is no limit in the objects of the attributes. The first proof argues by impossibility: if an attribute were restricted to some of what it is suitable for, then what is known to be possible (i.e., the attribute attaching to other objects) would become impossible, and it would require a specifier. This is explained: if an attribute is restricted to some objects while others are equally suitable, then restricting it to only one set is an arbitrary prevention of what is known to be possible—hence contradictory. The author then cites a gloss by al-Manjur clarifying that 'validity' here means absolute possibility (validity of existence regardless of necessity), not special possibility. The second proof argues: if attributes were restricted to some of what they can attach to, they would need a voluntary specifier, because all objects are equally possible; this implies temporality, contradicting the pre-eternality proven earlier. Thus both proofs establish that divine attributes must have unrestricted scope.

Key Terms
عدم النهاية
متعلقات الصفات
الجائز
المستحيل
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'possible' here means logically possible in the sense of neither necessary nor impossible.
Correction: Sanusi uses 'possible' in the sense of absolute possibility, which includes necessary existence as well; it means the validity of existence, not the middle state.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what does restricting a divine attribute to some objects entail?
A) The attribute becomes temporal.
B) The possible becomes impossible.
C) The attribute becomes unnecessary.
D) The objects become limited.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that restriction of a divine attribute is impossible because it would arbitrarily prevent what is validly possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof demonstrates that it is logically impossible for Allah to restrict His attributes. Yet, how often do you act as if His mercy, power, or forgiveness has limits? Identify one specific situation where you have doubted the reach of His attribute. What step can you take today to remove that mental barrier from your heart?
?
The proof argues that restriction would turn the possible into the impossible, which is false. Reflect on a time when you thought a certain sin was too great for Allah to forgive, or a certain prayer was too small for Him to answer. Did you inadvertently impose a restriction on His attributes? How does this lesson refute that assumption?
?
If Allah's attributes are unlimited in scope, then His justice applies equally to all, and His mercy encompasses everything. Is there someone you have excluded from your own mercy or forgiveness, thinking they do not deserve it? How does the logical impossibility of restricting Allah's attributes challenge you to reflect His unrestricted mercy in your own actions?

Second Proof: Restriction Requires a Specifier
Sanusi gives the second proof for unlimited scope: restriction implies need for specifier, hence temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that divine attributes cannot be restricted, because restriction would necessitate a specifier and thus imply temporality, contradicting pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Restricting attributes to some objects implies temporality, contradicting pre-eternity.

Sanusi presents a second argument: if divine attributes were restricted to some possible objects, they would need a voluntary specifier because all objects are equally possible. This would imply that the attributes are temporal, which contradicts the established proof that Allah and His attributes are pre-eternal. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second proof in Arabic and translation. Notice the reasoning: equality of all objects leads to need for a specifier.
This proof builds on the first but adds the consequence of temporality.
2
Understand the need for a specifier
Because all objects are equally possible, any restriction without a reason requires a voluntary specifier.
This is a common kalam argument against arbitrary limitation.
3
Connect to pre-eternity
If attributes need a specifier, they must be temporal (originated), but we already proved that Allah's attributes are pre-eternal.
This contradiction shows restriction is impossible.
4
See the two proofs together
Reflect on how both proofs lead to the same conclusion: divine attributes have unlimited scope.
The second proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Refuting a Preventing Factor on Divine Attributes
Sanusi refutes the objection of a preventing factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no preventing factor can legitimately restrict the attachment of a divine attribute to possible objects.

Core Idea
A preventing factor either destroys the attribute or is irrelevant.

Sanusi has established that divine attributes attach to all possible objects. But what if a preventing factor blocks that attachment? In this passage, he considers and refutes that objection by analyzing the nature of such a factor. The refutation is elegantly simple: either the factor contradicts the attribute, which is impossible, or it does not, in which case it has no effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core argument. Note the structure: a hypothetical objection followed by the author's rebuttal.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author splits the analysis into two cases: the preventing factor either contradicts the attribute or it does not. Identify both and their implications.
This is a classic kalam method of exhaustive division.
3
Understand the first case
If the factor contradicts the attribute, the attribute would cease to exist. But the attribute is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible. Hence this case is invalid.
The term 'eternal' here refers to the attribute's everlastingness.
4
Understand the second case
If the factor does not contradict the attribute, then it has no effect on the attachment. The attribute remains fully operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ثبوت التعلق لا يمنع منه مانع ] لا يقال: جاز التعلق بالجميع، لكن منع منه مانع. لأنَّا نقول: المانع إِنْ ضادَّ الصفة لزمَ عدمُها، وعدمُ القديم محال، وإلا فلا أثرَ لهُ.
[The affirmation of attachment is not prevented by a preventing factor.] It cannot be said: attachment to all is possible, but a preventing factor prevents it. Because we say: the preventing factor, if it contradicts the attribute, then its non-existence follows, and the non-existence of the eternal is impossible; otherwise, it has no effect.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the proof that attributes attach to all possible objects. The objector suggests that while attachment to all is possible, a preventing factor (manā') could restrict it. The author refutes this by analyzing the nature of the preventing factor. If the factor contradicts the attribute (i.e., is incompatible with its existence), then the attribute would cease to exist, but that is impossible because the attribute is eternal. If the factor does not contradict the attribute, then it has no effect on its attachment. Therefore, no preventing factor can legitimately restrict the scope of divine attributes.

Key Terms
المانع preventing factor
ضادَّ contradicts
القديم the eternal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a preventing factor could limit God's power or attributes without affecting the attribute's existence.
Correction: The passage shows that any preventing factor either contradicts the attribute (impossible) or is irrelevant, so it cannot limit the attribute's attachment.

Checkpoint
What happens if a preventing factor contradicts the divine attribute?
A) The attribute restricts itself.
B) The attribute becomes temporal.
C) The attribute would cease to exist, which is impossible.
D) The preventing factor remains ineffective.

Key Takeaway
No preventing factor can limit a divine attribute's attachment because it either destroys the attribute (impossible) or has no effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt that something was preventing God from answering your prayer or helping you. According to Sanusi's argument, what must that 'something' be—either it contradicts God's attribute (impossible) or it has no effect. How does this reframe the obstacle you faced? Will you now treat that obstacle differently?
?
You claim to believe that God is all-powerful and all-knowing. But when a door closes in your life, do you secretly think there is a 'preventing factor' that limits Him? Examine your heart: do you truly live as if nothing can stop God's mercy and power from reaching you? What specific situation makes this hardest for you to believe?

Objection and Answer on Psychological Attachment
Sanusi answers an objection about psychological attachment and preventing factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that psychological attachment can be prevented and Sanusi's answer that prevention in humans is due to multiplicity, not applicable to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Human forgetfulness does not imply God's attributes can be prevented.

This lesson examines a subtle objection: if God's knowledge is attached to objects, could a preventing factor block that attachment? Sanusi clarifies that mental attachment is intrinsic and cannot be prevented; the apparent prevention in humans is only due to the multiplicity of their attributes, not applicable to the simplicity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that psychological attachment can be prevented by a factor.
This challenges the necessity of God's knowledge.
3
Understand Sanusi's answer
Sanusi argues that mental attachment cannot be prevented; human prevention is due to multiplicity, as shown by forgetting one known thing while retaining another.
The key is the distinction between the attachment itself and the existence of the attribute.
4
Connect to divine attributes
Since God's attributes are simple and not multiple, the preventing factor cannot apply to Him.
This preserves God's perfect knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً: فالتعلق نفسي يستحيل أن يمنع منه مانع، والمانعُ في حقنا إنَّما منع وجود الصفة لتعدُّدها بالنسبة إلينا بدليل صحة ذهولنا عن أحد المعلومين مع بقاء الآخر، لا تعلُّقها. هذا اعتراض على الملازمة، وجوابه.
And also: attachment is mental (intrinsic), it is impossible for a preventing factor to prevent it. The preventing factor in our case only prevents the existence of the attribute due to its multiplicity in relation to us, as evidenced by the validity of our forgetting one of the two known things while the other remains, not its attachment. This is an objection to the implication and its answer.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and answer. The objection argues that attachment (tā'alluq) is psychological/mental (nafsī) and therefore can be prevented by a factor. The author responds that psychological attachment is intrinsic and cannot be prevented; any apparent prevention in humans is due to the multiplicity of the attribute in relation to us—for example, we can forget one known thing while retaining another, but this does not affect the intrinsic attachment of knowledge to its object. Thus, the possibility of prevention in humans does not apply to God, whose attributes are simple and not multiple in relation to Him. This objection targets the implication (mulāzama) that if restriction were possible, then attributes would need a specifier, and the answer refutes it.

Key Terms

الذهول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human forgetting proves that God's knowledge could be prevented.
Correction: Human forgetting is due to multiplicity of attributes, not applicable to God's simple attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the preventing factor in humans not apply to divine attributes?
A) Because human attributes are multiple.
B) Because divine attributes are psychological.
C) Because preventing factor contradicts God's essence.
D) Because divine attributes are not subject to prevention at all.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that psychological attachment is intrinsic and cannot be prevented; any apparent prevention in humans is due to multiplicity, not applicable to God.

The Necessity of a Specificr for Divine Attributes
Sanusi argues that restricting divine attributes requires a specificr, refuting the objection of a preventer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why restricting divine attributes to only some possible relations necessitates a specificr and how the objection of a preventer is refuted.

Core Idea
Attributes need a specificr; a preventer cannot block them without absurdity.

Sanusi presents a tight argument linking specification of attributes to temporality. Before we can accept that some attributes are related to some things and not others, we must consider the rational implications. This passage explores why a preventer cannot be invoked as an excuse, and strengthens the case for the attributes' pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Sanusi argues that specification of attributes requires a specificr, leading to temporality.
Notice the logical flow: equality of possibilities → need for specificr → temporality.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection: perhaps a preventer could block the relation to all possibilities, making specification unnecessary.
The objection is introduced with 'لا يقال'.
3
Examine the refutation
Study Sanusi's two-part refutation: (1) if the preventer opposes the attribute, it leads to impossibility; (2) if not, it has no effect. Then the argument about the relational attribute.
The refutation uses the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal.
4
Reflect on the footnote
Read the parenthetical note clarifying the term 'validity' as absolute possibility, not specific possibility.
This helps avoid a misunderstanding about the scope of possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فتخصيص الصفاتِ ببعض ما جازَ أَنْ تتعلَّقَ بهِ يُوجِبُ افتقارها إلى مُخصص مختارٍ ؛ لاستواء الجميع بالنسبة إليها ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثها ، وقد سبق البرهان على وجوب القدمِ والبقاء لذاتِهِ تعالى ولجميع صفاته (۱)
[ ثبوت التعلقِ لا يمنعُ منه مانع ]
لا يُقالُ : جازَ التعلق بالجميع ، لكن منع منه مانع . لأنَّا نقولُ : المانعُ إِنْ ضادَّ الصفةَ لزمَ عدمُها ، وعدمُ القديم محال ، وإلا فلا أثرَ لهُ
وأيضاً : فالتعلق نفسي يستحيل أن يمنع منه مانع ، والمانعُ في حقنا إنَّما منع وجود الصفة لتعدُّدها بالنسبة إلينا
بدليل صحة ذهولنا عن أحد المعلومين مع بقاء الآخر ، لا تعلُّقها
هذا اعتراض على الملازمة ، وجوابه
.
=
( أشار بقوله : « صحته » إلى الجواز في قوله : ( لاستحال ما عُلِمَ جوازه » ، بمعنى الإمكان العام ؛ وهو صحة وجود الشيء وجب وجوده أو لا ، وليس المراد الإمكان الخاص ؛ إذ تعلُّق القدرة بكل ممكن واجب ، فالجواز إذاً فيه بمعنى صحة ثبوته ، لا بمعنى أنه يصح ثبوته ونفيه )
And also: specifying the attributes to some of what they may relate to necessitates their need for a specific chooser [mukhtaṣṣ]; because all [possibilities] are equal in relation to them, and that necessitates their temporality [ḥudūth]. The proof has already preceded for the necessity of pre-eternity [qidam] and everlastingness [baqā'] for His Essence, exalted is He, and for all His attributes. [The affirmation of relation is not prevented by a preventer.] It is not said: Relation to all is possible, but a preventer prevented it. Because we say: if the preventer opposes the attribute, its non-existence is necessary, and non-existence of the eternal [qadīm] is impossible; otherwise, it has no effect. And also: the relational attribute [taʿalluq nafsī] is impossible to be prevented by a preventer. The preventer in our case only prevented the existence of the attribute due to its multiplicity in relation to us, as evidenced by the validity of our being heedless of one of two known things while the other remains, not its relation. This is an objection to the implication and its answer. = (He indicated by his saying 'its validity' to permissibility in his saying: 'for what is known to be permissible becomes impossible,' meaning absolute possibility [al-imkān al-ʿāmm], which is the validity of existence of a thing whether its existence is necessary or not. He does not mean specific possibility; for the relation of power to every possible is necessary. So permissibility here means the validity of its affirmation, not the validity of its affirmation and negation.)
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by an objection-and-answer. Sanusi begins by arguing that if the divine attributes were related to only some of what they could relate to, they would require a specificr [mukhaṣṣiṣ] because all possibilities are equally possible in themselves. That would entail the attributes' temporality [ḥudūth], contradicting their pre-eternity [qidam]. He then anticipates an objection: why not say that relation to all is possible but a preventer [māniʿ] prevented it? He refutes this: if the preventer opposes the attribute (i.e., contradicts it), the attribute would cease to exist, and non-existence of the eternal is impossible. If it does not oppose it, the preventer has no effect. He adds a second argument: the relational attribute [taʿalluq nafsī] (like knowledge or power as a relation) cannot be prevented by an external factor. In our case, the preventer only prevents the existence of the attribute due to multiplicity, not the relation itself. He illustrates: we can be forgetful of one known thing while knowing another, which shows the relation can persist. Then he notes that this objection is against the implication [mulāzama] and gives its answer. The parenthetical note clarifies the term 'validity' (ṣiḥḥa) as absolute possibility (imkān ʿāmm), not specific possibility, because the relation of power to every possible is necessary. So permissibility here means the validity of affirmation, not the possibility of both affirmation and negation.

Key Terms
تخصيص
مخصص
الصفات
التعلق
المانع
الاستحالة
الجواز
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A preventer can block a divine attribute's relation to some objects without affecting the attribute's pre-eternity.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if the preventer opposes the attribute, the attribute ceases to exist (impossible), and if it does not oppose, it has no effect. Thus, a preventer cannot be invoked to explain limited relation.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence Sanusi draws from restricting divine attributes to only some of their possible relations?
A) It shows the attributes are necessary.
B) It proves the attributes need a specificr and become temporal.
C) It demonstrates the attributes are independent.
D) It confirms the attributes are eternal without need of a specificr.

Key Takeaway
The argument that specification implies a specificr and temporality, and the refutation of the preventer objection, together reinforce the pre-eternity of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage proves that no preventer can limit God's attributes. Yet in your life, when you face trials or unanswered prayers, do you ever doubt God's absolute knowledge or power? What would it mean for your faith to truly accept that nothing can prevent His attributes from being perfect?
?
Sanusi argues that specification requires a specificr, implying temporality. Are there aspects of God's attributes that you, consciously or unconsciously, 'specify'—focusing only on mercy while ignoring justice, or trusting His power only in certain areas? Essential vs. Accidental Impossibility in Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents a detailed objection and refutes it by distinguishing essential and accidental impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes an objection about divine attribute specification by distinguishing essential impossibility from impossibility due to an external preventer.

Core Idea
A thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to another—no contradiction.

Sanusi now presents a more detailed version of the objection and his refutation. The objector argues that restricting an attribute's relation to only some of its suitable objects should not entail impossibility. Sanusi's reply hinges on a crucial distinction: is the impossibility essential or from an external preventer? This distinction preserves divine perfection while answering the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: objection then refutation.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage first presents the objector's argument, then Sanusi's refined formulation via a question about 'essential' vs 'absolute' impossibility.
The refinement is framed as 'it is better to formulate... by way of inquiry'.
3
Focus on the key distinction
Sanusi distinguishes between essential impossibility (from the thing's own essence) and accidental impossibility (due to an external preventer). This is the core of the refutation.
The objector's error is conflating these two types.
4
Understand why it resolves the contradiction
If impossibility is by essence, then a thing cannot be both permissible and impossible. But if impossibility is by another, the same thing can be permissible in essence yet impossible due to a preventer.
This avoids any logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرير الاعتراضِ أنْ يُقالَ : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ اختصاص الصفةِ المتعلقة ببعض ما تصلحُ لهُ يلزمُ منهُ استحالةُ ما عُلِمَ جوازه ، لأنَّهُ إِنَّما يلزمُ ذلك لو كانَ امتناع تعلُّقها بالبعض مِنْ ذاتِها ؛ إِذِ الفَرْضُ حِينَئِذٍ أَنَّ ذلكَ البعض مما يصلح أن تتعلَّقَ بهِ ، فامتناع تعلقها بهِ لا لموجبِ جمع بينَ جواز التعلقِ واستحالتِهِ . أَمَّا إذا كانَ امتناع تعلُّقها بذلكَ البعض لا مِنْ ذاتها ، بل لمانع . . لم يلزم الجمع بينَ الجواز والاستحالة (۱) ؛ لاختلافهما حينئذ بالإضافة ؛ إذ الجوازُ إِنَّما هو باعتبار الذات ، والاستحالةُ إنَّما هي باعتبار الغير والأولى أن يُقرَّرَ هذا الاعتراض بطريق الاستفسار ؛ وذلكَ أنْ يقال : ما تريدون بالاستحالة والجواز اللذين لزم اجتماعهما على تقدير عدمِ العموم في تعلقِ الصفاتِ : الاستحالة والجواز الذاتيين ، أو ما هو أعم ؟ فإن أردتُمُ الأوَّلَ منعنا الملازمة ؛ إذ الاستحالة هنا نقول : إنَّها ليسَتْ بذاتيّة ، بل مِنَ الغيرِ ؛ وهو المانع . وإن أردتُمُ الثاني - وهو مطلقُ الاستحالة والجواز ـ منعنا الاستثنائية؛
And the formulation of the objection is to say: We do not concede that the specification of the relational attribute to some of what it is suitable for necessitates the impossibility of what is known to be permissible, because that would only follow if the prevention of its relation to that part were from its own essence; for then the assumption is that that part is something suitable for it to relate to, so the prevention of its relation to it without a cause brings together the permissibility of its relation and its impossibility. But if the prevention of its relation to that part is not from its own essence, but due to a preventer, then the conjunction of permissibility and impossibility does not follow, because they differ then in relation: permissibility is considered in relation to the essence, while impossibility is considered in relation to something other [than the essence]. And it is better to formulate this objection by way of inquiry: that is, to say: What do you mean by impossibility and permissibility, the conjunction of which would follow on the assumption of non-generality in the relation of attributes? Are they essential impossibility and permissibility, or something more general? If you mean the first, we deny the implication, for here the impossibility, we say, is not essential but from another, namely the preventer. And if you mean the second—absolute impossibility and permissibility—we deny the conditional statement [al-istithnā'iyya].
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents a more detailed objection and Sanusi's refutation. The objector argues: we do not accept that restricting the relational attribute to only some of its suitable objects necessitates the impossibility of what is known to be permissible. This consequence would only follow if the prevention of relation were from the attribute's own essence (i.e., essential impossibility). But if the prevention is due to an external preventer, then the same thing can be permissible in itself (by essence) and impossible due to another. There is no contradiction because the two judgments are relative: permissibility is by essence, impossibility by another. Sanusi then offers a better formulation: ask the opponent what kind of impossibility and permissibility they mean. If they mean essential, then the implication is denied because the impossibility here is not essential but from the preventer. If they mean absolute (any impossibility and any permissibility), then the conditional statement (the 'if-then' link) is denied because absolute permissibility does not entail absolute impossibility when the impossibility is by another.

Key Terms
تقرير الاعتراض
الاستحالة الذاتية
الاستحالة بالغير
الجواز الذاتي
المطلق
الاستثنائية
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing is possible in itself and impossible due to another, it entails a logical contradiction.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the two judgments are relative: permissibility by essence and impossibility by another do not conflict.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what distinguishes essential impossibility from impossibility due to another?
A) Essential impossibility is stronger.
B) Essential impossibility pertains to the essence of the thing, while other-imposed impossibility relates to an external preventer.
C) Both are identical in effect.
D) Essential impossibility can be removed by a preventer.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essential impossibility and impossibility due to another allows for a thing to be possible in itself but prevented by an external cause, resolving the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection and Answer on Universality of Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents an objection and two arguments answering it regarding the universality of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the universality of divine attributes and the two arguments that answer it, demonstrating that the relation of an attribute is essential and cannot be removed by an external impediment.

Core Idea
An attribute's relation is essential; any impediment either destroys it or has no effect.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer regarding whether divine attributes must apply universally. The objection argues that non-universality does not imply contradiction if due to an external cause, but the answer shows that such a cause is impossible. We will see how the author defends the necessity of universal relation through two key arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two parts: the objection (first half) and the answer (second half).
2
Identify the objection's main claim
The objector denies that non-universal relation implies contradiction. Find the two ways he formulates this claim.
The objection distinguishes between impossibility from essence versus from an external impediment.
3
Understand the first argument of the answer
The answer argues that any impediment must subsist in the essence of the attribute. Then it either contradicts the attribute (annihilating it) or does not (having no effect). Both are impossible for divine attributes.
This refutes the objection by showing an impediment cannot be coherently assumed.
4
Understand the second argument of the answer
The answer states that the relation of a relational attribute is essential (نفسي), meaning it cannot be removed while the attribute remains. Since the attribute is necessary, its relation must remain.
This establishes that the relation is intrinsic and universal.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is important for the doctrine of divine attributes. How does it preserve their universality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرير الاعتراضِ أنْ يُقالَ : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ اختصاص الصفةِ المتعلقة ببعض ما تصلحُ لهُ يلزمُ منهُ استحالةُ ما عُلِمَ جوازه ، لأنَّهُ إِنَّما يلزمُ ذلك لو كانَ امتناع تعلُّقها بالبعض مِنْ ذاتِها ؛ إِذِ الفَرْضُ حِينَئِذٍ أَنَّ ذلكَ البعض مما يصلح أن تتعلَّقَ بهِ ، فامتناع تعلقها بهِ لا لموجبِ جمع بينَ جواز التعلقِ واستحالتِهِ . أَمَّا إذا كانَ امتناع تعلُّقها بذلكَ البعض لا مِنْ ذاتها ، بل لمانع . . لم يلزم الجمع بينَ الجواز والاستحالة (۱) ؛ لاختلافهما حينئذ بالإضافة ؛ إذ الجوازُ إِنَّما هو باعتبار الذات ، والاستحالةُ إنَّما هي باعتبار الغير والأولى أن يُقرَّرَ هذا الاعتراض بطريق الاستفسار ؛ وذلكَ أنْ يقال : ما تريدون بالاستحالة والجواز اللذين لزم اجتماعهما على تقدير عدمِ العموم في تعلقِ الصفاتِ : الاستحالة والجواز الذاتيين ، أو ما هو أعم ؟ فإن أردتُمُ الأوَّلَ منعنا الملازمة ؛ إذ الاستحالة هنا نقول : إنَّها ليسَتْ بذاتيّة ، بل مِنَ الغيرِ؛ وهو المانع . وإن أردتُمُ الثاني - وهو مطلقُ الاستحالة والجواز ـ منعنا الاستثنائية؛ إذ لا تنافي بإجماع بين كون الشيء جائزاً بحسَبِ ذاتِهِ ، وبين كونه ممتنعاً بحسب غيره، ألا ترى أنَّ إيمان أبي لهب ونحوه جائز بالنظر إلى ذاته ، مستحيل باعتبار غيره ؛ وهو تعلُّق علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعِهِ ؟! أجابَ في العقيدة عن هذا الاعتراض : بأنَّ تقدير المانع هنا حتى تكونَ الاستحالةُ بالغيرِ لا بالذاتِ .. لا يصح ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ المانع لا بدَّ وأَنْ يكون معنى قائماً بالذاتِ التي أوجب لها المنع لاستحالة إيجاب المعنى حكماً لما لم يقم بهِ ، وحينئذٍ نقولُ : هذا المانعُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يُضادَّ الصفةَ المتعلقة أو لا ؛ فإنْ ضادَّها لزمَ عدم الصفة أصلاً ؛ لاستحالة الجمع بينَ الضدين ، وقد سبق استحالة العدم مطلقاً في صفاته تعالى ، وإن لم يُضادَّها لم يكن له أثر ، فتبقى الصفة على عمومها . وأيضاً : فالتعلُّق عموماً أو خصوصاً للصفة المتعلّقة نفسي لها (۱) وإلا لزمَ قيامُ المعنى بالمعنى ، ولزم تعقُلُ الصفة المتعلقة بدونِ أصل التعلُّقِ ، وهو محال ، وإذا كانَ التعلَّق مطلقاً نفسياً للصفة المتعلقة استحال رفعه عموماً أو خصوصاً مع بقاء الصفة ، فمانعه إذاً مانع مِنْ وجود الصفة . لكنَّ الصفة واجبة الوجودِ لا تقبلُ عدماً، فتقديرُ مانع يرفعُ وجودها مستحيل ، وهذا معنى قولي : ( وأيضاً : فالتعلُّقُ نفسي يستحيلُ أنْ يمنعَ منهُ مانع ( أي : معَ بقاء الصفةِ المتعلقة كما قرَّرَ (۱) قوله : ( عموماً ) كالعلم والكلام ، وقوله : ( خصوصاً ) كسائر الصفات المتعلقة ، ومعنى كون التعلّق نفسيّاً للصفة : أنه ذاتي لها ، لا تعقل بدونه ؛ إذ الصفة المتعلقة تطلب الزائد في نفسها ، ولا تعقل بغير هذا الطلب
And the formulation of the objection is to say: We do not concede that the specificity of a relational attribute to some of what it is suitable for necessitates the impossibility of what is known to be possible, because that would only be necessary if the prevention of its relation to some [objects] is from its essence; for then the assumption is that that part is something suitable for it to relate to, so the prevention of its relation to it is without cause, [which would] combine possibility and impossibility. But if the prevention of its relation to that part is not from its essence, but due to an impediment, then the combination of possibility and impossibility does not follow (1); because they differ then in relation: for possibility is only with respect to essence, and impossibility is only with respect to something else. And it is better to formulate this objection by way of inquiry: that is, to say: What do you mean by the impossibility and possibility whose conjunction is necessitated on the assumption of the absence of universality in the relation of attributes: essential impossibility and possibility, or what is more general? If you mean the first, we deny the implication; for we say: the impossibility here is not essential, but from something else; that is, the impediment. And if you mean the second—that is, absolute impossibility and possibility—we deny the conditional; for there is no contradiction by consensus between a thing being possible with respect to its essence and being impossible with respect to something else. Do you not see that the faith of Abu Lahab and the like is possible with respect to its essence, but impossible with respect to something else—namely, the relation of the knowledge of Allah Most High to its non-occurrence? The Creed answered this objection: that the assumption of an impediment here so that the impossibility is by something else and not by essence is not valid; for that impediment must be a meaning subsisting in the essence that necessitates the prohibition, because it is impossible for a meaning to necessitate a ruling for what it does not subsist in. And then we say: This impediment either contradicts the relational attribute or not. If it contradicts it, then the non-existence of the attribute necessarily follows, because it is impossible to combine two opposites, and the absolute non-existence of His attributes has already been shown to be impossible. And if it does not contradict it, then it has no effect, so the attribute remains on its universality. And also: the relation, whether universal or particular, of the relational attribute is essential to it (1); otherwise, the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning would be necessary, and the conception of the relational attribute without the basis of relation would be necessary, which is impossible. And if the relation absolutely is essential to the relational attribute, its removal universally or particularly while the attribute remains is impossible. So any impediment to it is therefore an impediment to the existence of the attribute. But the attribute is necessarily existent and does not admit non-existence, so assuming an impediment that removes its existence is impossible. And this is the meaning of my statement: (And also: the relation is essential, so it is impossible for any impediment to prevent it) (i.e., while the relational attribute remains, as he explained (1) His statement: (universally) like knowledge and speech, and his statement: (particularly) like the rest of the relational attributes, and the meaning of the relation being essential to the attribute is that it is intrinsic to it, not conceivable without it; for the relational attribute demands the additional in itself, and is not conceivable without this demand.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection-and-answer regarding the universality of divine attributes. The objection begins by denying the claim that if a relational attribute (like knowledge or power) does not apply universally to all objects it can relate to, then that would combine possibility and impossibility. The objector argues that such a combination only occurs if the impossibility of relation is inherent to the attribute's essence, but if it is due to an external impediment (مانع), then the attribute's possibility with respect to its essence is not contradicted by its impossibility in a particular case. The objector elaborates by asking whether the impossibility and possibility in question are essential or absolute. If essential, he denies the implication because the impossibility here is from another. If absolute, he denies the conditional because a thing can be possible in essence and impossible due to another, like the faith of Abu Lahab being possible in itself but impossible due to Allah's prior knowledge. The answer from the Creed refutes this by first arguing that any impediment must subsist in the essence of the attribute, otherwise it cannot affect it. That impediment either contradicts the attribute (which would annihilate it, impossible for God) or does not contradict it (hence has no effect). Second, the answer argues that the relation of a relational attribute is essential (نفسي) to it, meaning it is intrinsic and cannot be removed while the attribute remains. Since the attribute is necessary in existence, removing its relation is impossible. The footnotes clarify the examples of universal (knowledge, speech) and particular (other relational attributes) relations, and explain that 'essential' means the attribute cannot be conceived without that relational demand.

الجواز الذاتي
المانع
التعلق النفسي
الصفة المتعلقة
المعنى القائم بالذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an attribute's non-universal application could be due to an external cause, while the attribute itself remains unchanged.
Correction: The author shows that such an external cause would either annihilate the attribute (contradiction) or have no effect, so the relation must be essential and universal.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the passage, what is the status of the relation (التعلق) for a relational attribute?
A) It is accidental and can be removed by an external impediment.
B) It is essential (نفسي) and cannot be removed while the attribute remains.
C) It depends on the attribute's object.
D) It is only universal for some attributes.

Key Takeaway
The relation of a divine attribute is essential to it; any impediment to its universality is impossible.

?

?
