The Intrinsic Relation of Divine Attributes Cannot Be Prevented
Sanusi argues that the relation of a divine attribute is intrinsic and cannot be blocked.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relation of a divine attribute is intrinsic and cannot be prevented while the attribute remains.

Core Idea
A divine attribute's relation is essential to it; preventing the relation would remove the attribute.

Sanusi continues his discussion of divine attributes by addressing a subtle objection. Could an attribute like knowledge be blocked from relating to a particular object without ceasing to exist? This might seem plausible, but Sanusi shows it is impossible. In this passage, he provides two interconnected arguments to demonstrate that the relation is intrinsic to the attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the flow from objection to two refutations.
The passage uses technical terms like 'mani' (preventer) and 'nafsi' (intrinsic).
2
Identify the initial principle
Sanusi begins by acknowledging that a thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to something else, using Abu Lahab's faith as an example.
This sets up the objection that the obstacle might be external.
3
Analyze the first refutation
The obstacle must either oppose the attribute or not. If it opposes, the attribute is removed (impossible). If not, it has no effect.
This shows that the obstacle cannot block the relation without removing the attribute.
4
Understand the second refutation
The relation is intrinsic to the attribute, so removing the relation would remove the attribute itself. Since divine attributes are necessary, prevention is impossible.
This is the key argument: the relation cannot be separated from the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ لا تنافي بإجماع بين كون الشيء جائزاً بحسب ذاته، وبين كونه ممتنعاً بحسب غيره، ألا ترى أن إيمان أبي لهب ونحوه جائز بالنظر إلى ذاته، مستحيل باعتبار غيره؛ وهو تعلق علم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه؟! أجاب في العقيدة عن هذا الاعتراض: بأن تقدير المانع هنا حتى تكون الاستحالة بالغير لا بالذات .. لا يصح؛ لأن ذلك المانع لا بد وأن يكون معنى قائماً بالذات التي أوجب لها المنع لاستحالة إيجاب المعنى حكماً لما لم يقم به، وحينئذ نقول: هذا المانع: إما أن يضاد الصفة المتعلقة أو لا؛ فإن ضادها لزم عدم الصفة أصلاً؛ لاستحالة الجمع بين الضدين، وقد سبق استحالة العدم مطلقاً في صفاته تعالى، وإن لم يضادها لم يكن له أثر، فتبقى الصفة على عمومها. وأيضاً: فالتعلق عموماً أو خصوصاً للصفة المتعلقة نفسي لها (١) وإلا لزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى، ولزم تعقل الصفة المتعلقة بدون أصل التعلق، وهو محال، وإذا كان التعلق مطلقاً نفسياً للصفة المتعلقة استحال رفعه عموماً أو خصوصاً مع بقاء الصفة، فمانعه إذاً مانع من وجود الصفة. لكن الصفة واجبة الوجود لا تقبل عدماً، فتقدير مانع يرفع وجودها مستحيل، وهذا معنى قولي: ( وأيضاً: فالتعلق نفسي يستحيل أن يمنع منه مانع ( أي: مع بقاء الصفة المتعلقة كما قرر ) . (١) قوله: ( عموماً ) كالعلم والكلام، وقوله: ( خصوصاً ) كسائر الصفات المتعلقة، ومعنى كون التعلق نفسياً للصفة: أنه ذاتي لها، لا تعقل بدونه؛ إذ الصفة المتعلقة تطلب الزائد في نفسها، ولا تعقل بغير هذا الطلب.
Since there is no contradiction by consensus between a thing being possible with respect to its essence and being impossible with respect to something else; do you not see that the faith of Abu Lahab and the like is possible with respect to its essence, impossible with respect to something else, namely the attachment of Allah's knowledge to its non-occurrence?! He answered in al-'Aqida regarding this objection: that the assumption of an obstacle here so that the impossibility is due to something else, not due to the essence, is not valid; because that obstacle must necessarily be a meaning subsisting in the essence which necessitates the prohibition, due to the impossibility of a meaning bringing about a ruling for that in which it does not subsist. Then we say: this obstacle either opposes the related attribute or not; if it opposes it, then the non-existence of the attribute necessarily follows, because of the impossibility of combining opposites, and the absolute non-existence of His attributes has already been shown to be impossible; and if it does not oppose it, then it has no effect, so the attribute remains in its generality. Also: the relation, whether general or specific, is intrinsic to the related attribute (1), otherwise a meaning subsisting in a meaning would occur, and it would be necessary to conceive of the related attribute without the basis of relation, which is impossible; and if the relation is absolutely intrinsic to the related attribute, its removal, whether general or specific, is impossible while the attribute remains, so its preventer is thus a preventer of the existence of the attribute. But the attribute is necessarily existent and does not accept non-existence, so assuming a preventer that removes its existence is impossible; and this is the meaning of my statement: 'Also: the relation is intrinsic; it is impossible for any preventer to prevent it (i.e., while the related attribute remains, as stated).' (1) His statement: 'general' like knowledge and speech; 'specific' like other related attributes. The meaning of the relation being intrinsic to the attribute is that it is essential to it, not conceivable without it; for the related attribute demands the extra (object) in itself, and is not conceivable without this demand.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a polemical clarification within a kalam argument about divine attributes. The author (likely Sanusi) is answering an objection about the distinction between essential impossibility and impossibility due to an external factor. He begins by acknowledging that a thing can be possible in itself but impossible due to something else (e.g., Abu Lahab's faith is possible in itself but impossible because God has eternally known it will not occur). The objector attempted to argue that a divine attribute (like knowledge) might be prevented from relating to some object without being removed. The author refutes this by analyzing the nature of a 'preventer' (mani'). He argues that any such preventer must be a meaning subsisting in the essence; otherwise it cannot impose a ruling. The preventer either contradicts the attribute (in which case the attribute itself would be removed, impossible for God) or does not contradict it (in which case it has no effect, and the attribute remains universal). Then he advances a second argument: the relation (talluq) is intrinsic (nafsi) to the related attribute – i.e., essential and inseparable. If it could be removed while the attribute remains, that would entail the impossible situation of an attribute existing without its essential characteristic. Hence, preventing the relation would be preventing the attribute itself. Since divine attributes are necessary and cannot cease, such prevention is impossible. The footnote clarifies that 'general' relation applies to attributes like knowledge and speech (which can relate to multiple objects), while 'specific' applies to others (like will, which relates to a specific object).

Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that the relation is intrinsic (essential) to the attribute, so blocking it would remove the attribute itself. Since divine attributes are necessary, such prevention is impossible.

B) The relation is intrinsic (nafsi) and essential to the attribute, so removing it would remove the attribute.
C) The relation is a separate entity that can be prevented by God's will.
D) The relation is merely a conceptual distinction with no real existence.

Key Takeaway
The relation of a divine attribute is intrinsic to it, so any attempt to prevent its relation would prevent the attribute itself, which is impossible for divine attributes.

The Intrinsic Relation Objection and Its Refutation
Sanusi refutes an objection about intrinsic relations by distinguishing human and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that human attributes would necessitate multiple divine attributes by distinguishing the multiplicity of human attributes from the unity of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Human attributes are multiple and perishable; divine attributes are singular and eternal.

Sanusi responds to a logical objection that attempts to draw a parallel between human and divine attributes. The objector argues that if divine attributes have intrinsic relations that cannot be removed, then human attributes should behave similarly. Sanusi refutes this by showing that human attributes are fundamentally different: each human action corresponds to a new, particular attribute, whereas divine attributes are singular and necessary. This passage sharpens the distinction between the Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the questioner's assumption that human and divine attributes follow the same rules regarding intrinsic relations.
Notice the 'if-then' structure of the objection.
2
Locate Sanusi's answer
He denies the implication (man' al-mulāzama); find why he says the relation and attribute vanish together in humans.
Contrast this with the divine case where the attribute remains even if the relation is not actual.
3
Understand the proof by heedlessness
Examine the example of knowledge and heedlessness to see why each known thing requires its own knowledge.
Heedlessness of one known thing while knowing another shows that knowledge is multiple.
4
Apply the distinction
Note that the key difference is whether attributes are multiple and temporal (humans) or single and eternal (Allah).
This safeguards divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ( والمانع في حقنا إنما منع وجود الصفة لتعددها ... ) إلى آخره : جواب عن سؤال مقدر، وتقريره أن يقال: لو كان التعلق للصفات المتعلقة نفسياً بحيث لا يمكن نفيه عموماً أو خصوصاً مع بقاء الصفة كما قررتم .. لزم ألا يرتفع تعلق صفاتنا المتعلقة عن بعض ما يصلح له، والتالي باطل قطعاً؛ بدليل أن علمنا إنما يتعلق ببعض المعلومات، وما لم يتعلق به مع إمكان أن يتعلق به.. فكثير لا يأخذه الحصر، وكذلك قدرتنا وكلامنا وسائر صفاتنا المتعلقة إنما تعلقت بالنزر اليسير مما تصلح له. أجاب في العقيدة بمنع الملازمة؛ وذلك أن المنعدم في حقنا الصفة وتعلقها النفسي معاً، لا تعلقها النفسي مع بقائها، فكل ما جهلناه من المعلومات مثلاً فقد انعدم في حقنا من أفراد العلوم بقدره. ومثار الغلط في كلام السائل: توهُمه أن علمنا مثلاً وسائر صفاتنا المتعلقة يصلح أن تتعلق بمتعدد، والذي عند أئمتنا: أن الصفة المتعلقة بالنسبة إلينا إنما تصلح أن تتعلق بمتعلق واحد فقط، فحيث تعدد المتعلق في حقنا فقد تعددت الصفة بحسبه، وقد استدلوا على ذلك: بأنه لو كان لنا علم واحد مثلاً يتعلق بمعلومين فأكثر .. لما صح أن نذهل عن بعضها مع حضور الآخر؛ لما فيه من اجتماع الضدين؛ وهما العلم والذهول، لكن ذهولنا عن بعض معلوماتنا معلوم لنا بالضرورة، فكل معلوم لنا إذاً فله علم يخصه
And His statement: 'And the preventer in our case only prevents the existence of the attribute due to its multiplicity...' to the end: a response to a presumed question; and its explanation is that it may be said: If the relation of the related attributes were intrinsic, so that it cannot be negated, generally or specifically, while the attribute remains as you have stated, then it would be necessary that the relation of our related attributes would not be removed from some of what is suitable for it; but the consequent is absolutely false; evidence for it: our knowledge is only related to some known things, and that which it does not relate to despite the possibility of relating to it is much that cannot be counted; and similarly our power, speech, and all our related attributes are only related to a small portion of what they are suitable for. He answered in al-'Aqida by denying the implication; that is because what ceases to exist in our case is the attribute and its intrinsic relation together, not the intrinsic relation while the attribute remains. So everything that we are ignorant of, for example, from among known things, has ceased to exist in our case from the individual knowledges to its extent. And the source of error in the questioner's statement is his assumption that our knowledge, for example, and all our related attributes are suitable for relating to multiple objects, whereas the view of our imams is that the related attribute with respect to us is only suitable for relating to a single object. So when the object is multiple in our case, the attribute becomes multiple accordingly. And they have argued for that by saying: If we had a single knowledge, for example, that relates to two or more known things, then it would not be correct for us to be heedless of some of them while the others are present, because that would involve the combination of two opposites: knowledge and heedlessness. But our heedlessness of some of our known things is known to us necessarily. So every known thing that we have, therefore, has a knowledge that is specific to it.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer structure typical of kalam. The objector (sā'il) argues: if the relation of a divine attribute is intrinsic and cannot be removed while the attribute remains, then the same should apply to human attributes. But human attributes are limited: our knowledge only covers some things, and does not relate to many things it could relate to. Therefore, either human attributes lack intrinsic relations (contradicting the premise) or the premise is false for divine attributes as well. The author answers by denying the implication (man' al-mulāzama). He explains that in humans, when a relation is not present, the attribute itself is absent. For example, if we do not know a certain thing, it is not that the same knowledge fails to relate; rather, that particular knowledge does not exist at all. Each instance of knowledge is a distinct attribute. The questioner's mistake is assuming a single attribute could relate to multiple objects. The author's school holds that human attributes are particular: each attribute relates to exactly one object. Evidence: if one knowledge related to multiple objects, we could not be heedless of some while aware of others, because that would require both knowledge and heedlessness of the same thing simultaneously. But we certainly experience such heedlessness, so each known thing has its own distinct knowledge. Thus, the case of human attributes does not undermine the claim about divine attributes, because human attributes are multiple and perishable, while divine attributes are single and necessary.

علم واحد
صفة متعلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human attributes, like divine attributes, have a single intrinsic relation that covers all possible objects.
Correction: Human attributes are multiple: each relation corresponds to a distinct attribute, so when a relation is absent, the attribute itself is absent.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi explain that human attributes do not have the same intrinsic relation as divine attributes?
A) Human attributes are the same as divine attributes but weaker.
B) In humans, when a relation is absent, the attribute itself is absent; each relation corresponds to a distinct attribute.
C) Human attributes can be prevented by external factors.
D) Human attributes do not have intrinsic relations at all.

Key Takeaway
Human attributes are multiple and each relates to a single object, unlike divine attributes which are singular and relate to all objects eternally.

Why Human Attributes Are Not Like Divine Attributes: An Objection and Answer
Sanusi answers an objection about the multiplicity of attributes by distinguishing human and divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the doctrine of divine attributes by distinguishing their intrinsic relation from the limited relation of human attributes, using the example of knowledge and forgetfulness.

Core Idea
Human attributes relate to only one object at a time; divine attributes are not so limited.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection: if God's attributes have an intrinsic relation (ta'alluq nafsī) that cannot be removed, then surely human attributes would also have permanent relations—but they clearly do not. Sanusi responds by denying the assumed parallel and showing that human attributes are fundamentally different: each human attribute is tied to a specific object and disappears when the object changes. This lesson walks through the objection, the response, and the proof from forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that if attributes have intrinsic relations, then human attributes would never lose their relation to an object, but they do. Understand the premise and conclusion.
The objector assumes a necessary connection between divine and human attributes.
2
Examine Sanusi's response
Sanusi denies the necessary connection (mulāzama). He explains that in humans, when the relation ceases, the attribute itself ceases. The attribute and its relation are inseparable.
He distinguishes the case: in God, attributes are eternal; in humans, they come and go.
3
Understand the root error
The objector mistakenly thought human attributes can relate to multiple objects. Sanusi proves that each human attribute relates to only one object by using the example of forgetfulness.
If one knowledge could cover two objects, we couldn't forget one while remembering the other.
4
Reflect on the grammatical note
Sanusi clarifies a grammatical point about the pronoun reference, ensuring the argument is correctly understood.
This shows attention to precise wording in the original objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعترضُ ، بل لا يرتفع إلا مع ارتفاع الصفة ، للكنَّ ارتفاع صفاتِهِ تعالى محال
وقوله : ( والمانع في حقنا إنَّما منع وجود الصفة لتعددها ... ) إلى آخره : جواب عن سؤالٍ مُقدَّر ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقال : لو كان التعلُّق للصفاتِ المتعلّقة نفسيّاً ؛ بحيثُ لا يمكن نفيه عموماً أو خصوصاً معَ بقاءِ الصفةِ كما قرَّرتُم .. لزمَ ألا يرتفع تعلُّق صفاتِنا المتعلقة عن بعض ما يصلح له ، والتالي باطل قطعاً ؛ بدليل أنَّ علمنا إِنَّما يتعلَّق ببعض المعلومات ، وما لم يتعلَّق بهِ معَ إمكان أن يتعلَّق به.. فكثير لا يأخذه الحصر ، وكذلك قدرتنا وكلامنا وسائر صفاتِنا المتعلقةِ إنَّما تعلَّقَتْ بالنزر اليسير مما تصلح له
أجابَ في العقيدة بمنع الملازمةِ ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ المنعدم في حقنا الصفةُ وتعلُّقها النفسي معاً ، لا تعلُّقها النفسي مع بقائها ، فكلُّ ما جهلناهُ مِنَ المعلوماتِ مثلاً فقد انعدم في حقنا مِنْ أَحادِ العلومِ بقدرِهِ .
ومثارُ الغلط في كلام السائلِ : توهُمُهُ أَنَّ علمنا مثلاً وسائرَ صفاتِنا المتعلّقة يصلحُ أن تتعلَّق بمتعدّدٍ ، والذي عند أئمتنا : أَنَّ الصفةَ المتعلقة بالنسبة إلينا إنَّما تصلح أن تتعلَّق بمتعلَّق واحدٍ فقط ، فحيثُ تعدد المتعلَّقُ في حقنا فقد تعدَّدَتِ الصفةُ بحسَبِهِ ، وقد استدلُّوا على ذلك : بأنَّهُ لو كانَ لنا علم واحدٌ مثلاً يتعلَّق بمعلومين فأكثر .. لَمَا صح أن نذهَلَ عن بعضها معَ حضورِ الآخرِ ؛ لما فيه من اجتماع الضدينِ ؛ وهما العلم والنُّهولُ ، لكنَّ ذهولنا عن بعض معلوماتِنا معلوم لنا بالضرورة ، فكلُّ معلوم لنا إذاً فله علم يخصه
والضمير في قولي : ( لتعدُّدِها ) يعود على الصفة ، وقولي : ( لا تعلَّقها ) منصوبٌ بالعطف على مفعول ( منع ) .
The objector: it is not removed except with the removal of the attribute, but the removal of His attributes is impossible. And his saying: 'The preventer in our case only prevented the existence of the attribute due to its multiplicity...' to the end: a response to an implicit question, and its exposition is that it is said: If the relation (ta'alluq) of the related attributes were intrinsic (nafsī), such that it is impossible to negate it generally or specifically while the attribute remains as you have established, then it would follow that the relation of our related attributes would not be removed from some of what it is suitable for. And the consequent is absolutely false, by the evidence that our knowledge relates only to some known things, and what it does not relate to despite the possibility of relating to it... many are not encompassed by limitation, and similarly our power, speech, and all our related attributes only relate to a small portion of what they are suitable for. He answered in al-'Aqīda by denying the necessary connection (mulāzama); and that is because what is absent in our case is both the attribute and its intrinsic relation together, not its intrinsic relation while it remains. For everything we are ignorant of from the known things, for example, there has become absent from us an amount of the individual knowledges in proportion to it. And the source of error in the speech of the questioner is his assumption that our knowledge, for example, and all our related attributes are suitable for relating to multiple objects, whereas according to our imams: the related attribute with respect to us is only suitable for relating to a single object. So when the object is multiple in our case, the attribute becomes multiplied accordingly. And they adduced for that: that if we had a single knowledge relating to two or more known objects, it would not be valid for us to forget some of them while the other is present, because that would involve the concurrence of two opposites: knowledge and forgetfulness. But our forgetfulness of some of our known things is known to us necessarily, so every known thing for us therefore has its own knowledge. And the pronoun in my saying: 'due to its multiplicity' refers to the attribute, and my saying: 'not its relation' is in the accusative as a conjunct to the object of 'prevented'.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the relation (ta'alluq) of attributes. It begins with the objector's premise: an attribute's relation cannot be removed unless the attribute itself is removed, but God's attributes cannot be removed. The author then quotes an objection: 'The preventer in our case...' which argues that if the relation of attributes were intrinsic (nafsī) as claimed, then human attributes would never cease relating to any possible object, but they clearly do. The author's response is to deny the necessary connection (mulāzama). He explains that in human experience, when we lack knowledge of something, both the attribute (knowledge) and its intrinsic relation are absent together—not the relation alone while the attribute persists. The root of the objector's error is the assumption that our attributes can relate to multiple objects simultaneously. According to the Ash'arī imams, each human attribute relates to only one object at a time. For instance, if one piece of knowledge could relate to two objects, we could not forget one while remembering the other, but we do forget. Hence multiplicity of objects requires multiplicity of attributes. The passage concludes with a grammatical note clarifying that 'its multiplicity' refers to the attribute, and 'not its relation' is accusative as a conjunct.

Key Terms
تعلق (ta'alluq)
نفسي (nafsī)
ملازمة (mulāzama)
علم (knowledge)
ذهول (forgetfulness)
صفة متعلقة (related attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human attributes are like divine attributes in being able to relate to multiple objects simultaneously.
Correction: The author clarifies that human attributes are limited to a single object at a time, as evidenced by forgetfulness.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the source of the questioner's error in the objection about the relation of attributes?
A) Assuming that divine attributes are multiple
B) Assuming that human attributes can relate to multiple objects simultaneously
C) Denying that forgetfulness exists
D) Claiming that God's attributes are removable

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the doctrine of divine attributes by showing that the objection falsely assumes a parallel between human and divine attributes; human attributes are limited to a single object, unlike God's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often forget things about Allah—His names, His favors, His commands. This very forgetfulness proves your knowledge is limited and dependent. How does this humbling truth drive you to seek His constant remembrance and rely on Him to preserve what you know?
?
If each of your knowledges is a separate gift from Allah, and you forget one when you turn to another, how does that make you cherish every moment of knowing Him? What will you do today to guard against forgetting His oneness and greatness?

Proof of the Unity of Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents and explains the proof for unity of attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof (dalīl) that demonstrates the unity of God's attributes, based on the impossibility of both infinite multiplicity and finite multiplicity requiring a specific.

Core Idea
God's attributes must be one or else impossible consequences follow.

In this passage, Sanusi provides a formal proof for the oneness of God's attributes. He builds on the earlier premise that divine attributes relate to infinite objects. Using a dilemma, he shows that neither an infinite nor a finite multiplicity of attributes is possible, forcing the conclusion that each attribute is one. This is a classic argument in Islamic theology to safeguard divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the primary proof
Start by reading the first paragraph (دليلُ وَحدتِها ... تعيَّنَ إذا وجوبُ وَحدتِها). This gives the concise argument. Note the two branches of the dilemma.
The proof is presented twice: first succinctly, then in more detail.
2
Understand the dilemma's first branch
If attributes were multiple according to the infinite objects, infinite attributes would exist. That is impossible (محال). See why this is a problem.
Infinite regress or actual infinite in existence is rejected in kalam.
3
Understand the dilemma's second branch
If attributes were limited to a finite number, then some finite number would need to be chosen. Without a preferencer, it would require a specific (مخصص), which implies the attributes are temporal (حادث), contradicting their proven eternity (قديم).
The need for a mukhassis is a key argument against contingency.
4
Conclude the necessity of unity
Since both options lead to impossibility, the only remaining possibility is that each divine attribute is one (واحدة). This is the proof of unity.
This does not mean there is only one attribute; each attribute is one in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليلُ وَحدة الصفاتِ
وأما دليلُ وَحدتِها : فلأنَّها لو تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ مُتعلقاتِها لزم دخولُ ما لا نهاية له عدداً في الوجودِ ، وهو محال وإلا لم يكن لبعض الأعدادِ ترجيح على بعض ، فتفتقرُ في تعيين بعضها إلى مُخصص ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثها ، وقد تبيَّنَ وجوب قدمها ، فتعيَّنَ إذا وجوبُ وَحدتِها
هذا برهان المطلب الأوَّلِ ؛ وهو وجوب الوحدة لصفاتِهِ جل وعلا. وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لو كانَتْ صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى مُتعدّدةً ، وقد قامَ البرهان قبل قريباً على تعلُّقها بما لا يتناهى .. لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تتعدد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ المتعلقاتِ التي عرفت أنَّها لا تتناهى ، وإِمَّا أَنْ تختص بعددٍ متناه، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُه ، والملازمة ظاهرة. وأما بطلانُ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنَ التالي : فلأنَّه يؤدي إلى وجودِ صفاتِ لا نهاية لها عدداً ، وهو محال
Proof of the Unity of Attributes. And as for the proof of their unity: because if they were multiple according to the number of their objects, it would entail the entry of what has no end in number into existence, which is impossible. And if not (i.e., if they are not multiple according to the infinite objects), then no number would have preference over another, so specifying some numbers would require a specific (mukhassis), and that necessitates their temporality, while their pre-eternity has been established. So the necessity of their unity is determined. This is the demonstration of the first point, which is the necessity of unity for His attributes, glory be to Him. And its exposition is that it is said: If an attribute of His, exalted, were multiple, and the proof has already been established previously that it relates to what is infinite, it would not be free from either being multiple according to the multiplicity of objects which you know are infinite, or being restricted to a finite number. The consequent, in both its divisions, is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise, and the connection is apparent. And as for the invalidity of the first division of the consequent: because it leads to the existence of attributes without end in number, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the proof (dalīl) for the unity (wahda) of God's attributes. It begins by stating the proof directly: if the attributes were multiple in number corresponding to their objects (which are infinite), then an infinite number of things would exist, which is impossible. If, on the other hand, they were limited to a finite number, then no finite number would be inherently preferable to another, so a specific (mukhassis) would be needed to choose that number. The need for a specific implies that the attributes are temporally originated (hādith), but it has already been established that they are eternal (qadīm). Therefore, they must be one. The author then restates the argument more formally: an attribute of God relates to infinite objects (as proven earlier). So if it were multiple, it would either be multiple according to the infinite objects (leading to infinite attributes, impossible), or limited to a finite number (requiring a specific, implying temporality, contradicting eternity). Both alternatives are impossible, so the attribute must be one. This is a classic kalam proof for the oneness of divine attributes.

Key Terms
وحدة (wahda)
مخصص (mukhassis)
حادث (hādith)
قديم (qadīm)
برهان (proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that having multiple attributes (e.g., knowledge, power) means they are many in number like a collection of separate entities.
Correction: The proof shows that multiplicity in number leads to absurdities. Each attribute is one in its essence, though they are distinct conceptually. The oneness of each attribute does not mean there is only one attribute overall.

Checkpoint
What is the second impossibility argued in the proof for the unity of attributes?
A) That attributes would be infinite in number
B) That attributes would require a specific and thus be temporal
C) That attributes would not exist
D) That attributes would be multiple in number

Key Takeaway
The unity of divine attributes is proven because multiplicity would lead either to an actual infinite or to contingency, both of which are impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are one and not multiple, what does that mean for how you approach Him in supplication? Do you sometimes separate His mercy from His justice in your heart, relying on one to the neglect of the other?
?
The proof shows that multiplicity would require a mukhassiṣ, implying dependence. Have you ever treated God's attributes as if they are independent of His essence? How can you deepen your reliance on God as a single, unified being worthy of all worship?
?
Consider your daily life: do you ever ascribe 'parts' to God's essence by focusing on one attribute while ignoring another? How can you train your heart to see all of God's attributes as one?

Divine Attributes

The Proof of Oneness of Divine Attributes
Sanusi enumerates the proof for oneness of attributes and refutes two counter-possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the oneness of God's attributes, including why both infinite and finite multiplicity are impossible.

Core Idea
God's attributes cannot be multiple, as any multiplicity leads to absurdity.

Sanusi now provides the formal proof for the unity of God's attributes. This proof builds on the earlier argument that each attribute relates to infinite objects. He shows that if attributes were multiple, they would either be infinite in number (impossible) or limited to a finite number (requiring a determiner, implying temporality). The passage first states the proof concisely, then expands it into a detailed argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the concise proof
Start with the opening lines which state the proof in a nutshell: if attributes were multiple by the number of objects, infinite attributes would exist; if not, a determiner would lead to contingency.
Notice the immediate conclusion: therefore oneness is necessary.
2
Understand the dilemma
The detailed explanation presents two possibilities: either attributes multiply with the infinite objects (division 1) or are restricted to a finite number (division 2). Both are impossible.
3
Examine why each division fails
Read the refutation of each division: infinite attributes cannot be distinguished; finite attributes require a chooser (contingency) and impose an impossible distribution.
Focus on the concepts of 'distinguishing the infinite' and 'specific chooser'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that attributes are one. Ask yourself: How does this proof confirm the oneness of each divine attribute?
This proof is foundational in Kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دليلُ وَحدتِها : فلأنَّها لو تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ مُتعلقاتِها لزم دخولُ ما لا نهاية له عدداً في الوجودِ ، وهو محال وإلا لم يكن لبعض الأعدادِ ترجيح على بعض ، فتفتقرُ في تعيين بعضها إلى مُخصص ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثها ، وقد تبيَّنَ وجوب قدمها ، فتعيَّنَ إذا وجوبُ وَحدتِها
هذا برهان المطلب الأوَّلِ ؛ وهو وجوب الوحدة لصفاتِهِ جل وعلا. وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لو كانَتْ صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى مُتعدّدةً ، وقد قامَ البرهان قبل قريباً على تعلُّقها بما لا يتناهى .. لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تتعدد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ المتعلقاتِ التي عرفت أنَّها لا تتناهى ، وإِمَّا أَنْ تختص بعددٍ متناه، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُه ، والملازمة ظاهرة. وأما بطلانُ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنَ التالي : فلأنَّه يؤدي إلى وجودِ صفاتِ لا نهاية لها عدداً ، وهو محال ؛ إذ كلُّ ما يدخل تحتَ الوجودِ فلا بدَّ مِنْ صحة تميزه ، وتمييز ما لا يتناهى محال ، فوجود ما لا يتناهى محال .
وأمَّا بطلانُ القسم الثاني منه ـ وهو اختصاصها بعددٍ متناه ـ : فلأنَّهُ يقتضي اختصاصها بذلك العددِ المتناهي بدلاً عن غيرِهِ مُخصصاً مختاراً ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها ، وأيضاً يلزم توزيع ما لا يتناهى مِنَ المتعلقاتِ على ما يتناهى مِنَ الصفاتِ ، وهو محال ضرورة .
فإِنْ قلت : العلمُ مثلاً في حقنا مُتعدِّد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقِهِ، وكذا غيرُه ، فلو قامَ العلمُ مثلاً في حقِّهِ تعالى مَقامَ علوم [؟] .. لجازَ أنْ يقومَ في حقِّهِ تعالى مقام القدرة وسائر الصفاتِ ؛ بجامع قيامِهِ مَقامَ صفاتٍ متغايرةٍ ، بل ويلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يجوز قيامُ ذاتِهِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ ممَّا يأباه كلُّ مسلم . قلنا : الفرقُ أنَّ التغاير في العلوم لأجل التغاير في المتعلَّقِ مع الاتحادِ في النوع ، فحيثُ فُرِضَتِ الوَحدةُ في العلم مثلاً زال التغايرُ ، أمَّا العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفاتِ فمتغايرة في حقائقها جنساً ، فلو قام بعضُها مَقامَ بعضٍ للزمَ قلْبُ الحقائقِ ، ولزمَ ما تقدَّمَ في مسألة ( سوادِ حلاوة )
As for the proof of their oneness: it is that if they were multiple according to the number of their objects, then the entry of what has no end in number into existence would be necessary, and that is impossible. Otherwise, some numbers would have no preference over others, so they would need a specific in determining some of them, and that necessitates their temporality, and the necessity of their eternity has been established, so the necessity of their oneness is determined. This is the proof of the first demand; which is the necessity of oneness for His attributes, may He be exalted and glorified. And its detailed explanation is to say: if an attribute of His attributes, may He be exalted, were multiple, and the proof has been established shortly before that it is related to what is infinite, then it is not free: either it becomes multiple according to the multiplicity of the objects that you know are infinite, or it is restricted to a finite number. The consequent with its two divisions is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise, and the entailment is apparent. As for the falsity of the first division of the consequent: it is that it leads to the existence of attributes with no end in number, and that is impossible; for everything that enters into existence must be validly distinguished, and distinguishing what is infinite is impossible, so the existence of what is infinite is impossible. As for the falsity of the second division—which is its restriction to a finite number—it is that it necessitates its restriction to that finite number instead of others by a specific choosing agent, and that entails its temporality; and also it necessitates distributing what is infinite of objects among what is finite of attributes, and that is impossible by necessity. If you say: knowledge, for example, in our case is multiple according to the multiplicity of its object, and likewise other [attributes]. So if knowledge, for example, in His case, may He be exalted, were to take the place of various knowledges... then it would be possible for it, in His case, to take the place of power and the rest of the attributes, due to the common factor of it taking the place of distinct attributes; rather, it would also entail that His essence could take the place of all attributes, and that is rejected by every Muslim. We say: the difference is that the diversity in knowledges is due to the diversity of the object while being united in type; so when unity is assumed in knowledge, for example, the diversity disappears. As for knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes, they are diverse in their realities by genus; so if some of them were to take the place of others, it would necessitate the inversion of realities, and it would necessitate what was previously discussed in the issue of 'the blackness of sweetness'.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the proof (برهان) for the oneness of God's attributes. The author first states the proof concisely, then expands it into a detailed argument, refutes two possibilities, and finally answers an objection.

The concise proof: If attributes were multiple according to the number of their objects, that would lead to an infinite number of attributes existing, which is impossible. If they were not multiple according to the number of objects, then there would be no reason for a specific number, so they would need a determiner, which would imply they are contingent (حادث), but they are known to be eternal (قديم).

The detailed explanation: Given that each attribute relates to infinite objects (as proven earlier), the attribute is either (1) multiplied according to the infinite objects, leading to infinite attributes—impossible because anything existing must be distinguishable, and the infinite cannot be distinguished; or (2) restricted to a finite number, which would require a specific chooser and implies contingency, and also implies distributing infinite objects among finite attributes—impossible.

The objection: In humans, knowledge is multiple according to its objects, so if God's single knowledge could stand for many instances of knowledge, why couldn't it also stand for power and other attributes? That would lead to absurdity like the essence standing for all attributes.
  The answer: The diversity in human knowledges is due to diverse objects while they are of the same type (نوع); unity in the type removes diversity. But the diversity between knowledge and power is by genus (جنس), so one cannot substitute for another without distorting realities, as previously explained in the 'blackness of sweetness' analogy (a thing cannot be both black and sweet in the same respect).

Key Terms
وحدت الصفات
متعلقات
لا يتناهى
حدوث
قدم
مخصص
برهان
توزيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's attributes are multiple in the same way human attributes are multiple (i.e., each attribute has many instances).
Correction: Sanusi shows that if attributes were multiple, they would be either infinite (impossible) or finite (requiring a determiner, implying contingency). Therefore, each attribute must be one in essence.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the consequence if attributes were multiple corresponding to the number of objects (infinite)?
A) It would lead to infinite attributes existing, which is impossible.
B) It would lead to finite attributes, which is possible.
C) It would lead to the attributes being contingent.
D) It would lead to the attributes being abolished.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are necessarily one in essence, because any multiplicity would lead to either an impossible infinity or an impossible contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God's attributes are one in essence, or do you sometimes mentally separate them, acting as if God's power could override His mercy or His will could conflict with His knowledge? What would change in your relationship with Him if you internalized this unity?
?
The proof shows that multiplicity in attributes would require a determiner, implying contingency. When you ascribe multiple 'parts' to God in your mind, are you inadvertently limiting His absolute simplicity and eternity? How can you guard your heart against this subtle form of shirk?
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge, power, and will are a single, indivisible reality, how would that affect your trust in His decree? Are there areas in your life where you doubt that the same God who knows all also wills and is able to act perfectly?

Divine Attributes

Objection and Answer on Attribute Substitution
Sanusi answers an objection about attribute substitution by distinguishing type from genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's response to the objection that if God's knowledge can cover multiple objects, it could also substitute for power and other attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes of different genera cannot replace each other without inverting realities.

After establishing the proof for oneness, Sanusi anticipates an objection. The objector draws an analogy from human knowledge, which is multiple by its objects. If God's single knowledge covers multiple instances, why can't it also cover power and other attributes? Sanusi's answer distinguishes between diversity within the same type (نوع) and diversity across different genera (جنس), citing the principle that crossing genera would invert realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objector's statement: in humans, knowledge is multiple by its objects. So if God's knowledge takes the place of many knowledges, why not also power and other attributes?
The objector pushes the analogy to an absurd conclusion: the essence could replace all attributes.
2
Understand Sanusi's distinction
Sanusi says diversity in knowledges is due to objects while the type (نوع) is the same. If unity is assumed (as in God's knowledge), the diversity disappears.
Focus on the phrase 'with union in type' (مع الاتحاد في النوع).
3
Apply to different genera
Knowledge and power differ in genus (جنس). Their realities are different. Substituting one for the other would invert realities, just like saying something is both black and sweet (invalid).
The 'blackness of sweetness' example refers to a previous argument about impossibility of combining distinct properties.
4
Conclusion
Thus, the analogy fails because diversity across genera is essential, not just due to objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دليلُ وَحدتِها : فلأنَّها لو تعدَّدَتْ بعددِ مُتعلقاتِها لزم دخولُ ما لا نهاية له عدداً في الوجودِ ، وهو محال وإلا لم يكن لبعض الأعدادِ ترجيح على بعض ، فتفتقرُ في تعيين بعضها إلى مُخصص ، وذلك يُوجِبُ حدوثها ، وقد تبيَّنَ وجوب قدمها ، فتعيَّنَ إذا وجوبُ وَحدتِها
هذا برهان المطلب الأوَّلِ ؛ وهو وجوب الوحدة لصفاتِهِ جل وعلا. وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لو كانَتْ صفةٌ مِنْ صفاتِهِ تعالى مُتعدّدةً ، وقد قامَ البرهان قبل قريباً على تعلُّقها بما لا يتناهى .. لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ تتعدد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ المتعلقاتِ التي عرفت أنَّها لا تتناهى ، وإِمَّا أَنْ تختص بعددٍ متناه، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُه ، والملازمة ظاهرة. وأما بطلانُ القسم الأوَّلِ مِنَ التالي : فلأنَّه يؤدي إلى وجودِ صفاتِ لا نهاية لها عدداً ، وهو محال ؛ إذ كلُّ ما يدخل تحتَ الوجودِ فلا بدَّ مِنْ صحة تميزه ، وتمييز ما لا يتناهى محال ، فوجود ما لا يتناهى محال .
وأمَّا بطلانُ القسم الثاني منه ـ وهو اختصاصها بعددٍ متناه ـ : فلأنَّهُ يقتضي اختصاصها بذلك العددِ المتناهي بدلاً عن غيرِهِ مُخصصاً مختاراً ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها ، وأيضاً يلزم توزيع ما لا يتناهى مِنَ المتعلقاتِ على ما يتناهى مِنَ الصفاتِ ، وهو محال ضرورة .
فإِنْ قلت : العلمُ مثلاً في حقنا مُتعدِّد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقِهِ، وكذا غيرُه ، فلو قامَ العلمُ مثلاً في حقِّهِ تعالى مَقامَ علوم [؟] .. لجازَ أنْ يقومَ في حقِّهِ تعالى مقام القدرة وسائر الصفاتِ ؛ بجامع قيامِهِ مَقامَ صفاتٍ متغايرةٍ ، بل ويلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يجوز قيامُ ذاتِهِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ ممَّا يأباه كلُّ مسلم . قلنا : الفرقُ أنَّ التغاير في العلوم لأجل التغاير في المتعلَّقِ مع الاتحادِ في النوع ، فحيثُ فُرِضَتِ الوَحدةُ في العلم مثلاً زال التغايرُ ، أمَّا العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفاتِ فمتغايرة في حقائقها جنساً ، فلو قام بعضُها مَقامَ بعضٍ للزمَ قلْبُ الحقائقِ ، ولزمَ ما تقدَّمَ في مسألة ( سوادِ حلاوة )
As for the proof of their oneness: it is that if they were multiple according to the number of their objects, then the entry of what has no end in number into existence would be necessary, and that is impossible. Otherwise, some numbers would have no preference over others, so they would need a specific in determining some of them, and that necessitates their temporality, and the necessity of their eternity has been established, so the necessity of their oneness is determined. This is the proof of the first demand; which is the necessity of oneness for His attributes, may He be exalted and glorified. And its detailed explanation is to say: if an attribute of His attributes, may He be exalted, were multiple, and the proof has been established shortly before that it is related to what is infinite, then it is not free: either it becomes multiple according to the multiplicity of the objects that you know are infinite, or it is restricted to a finite number. The consequent with its two divisions is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise, and the entailment is apparent. As for the falsity of the first division of the consequent: it is that it leads to the existence of attributes with no end in number, and that is impossible; for everything that enters into existence must be validly distinguished, and distinguishing what is infinite is impossible, so the existence of what is infinite is impossible. As for the falsity of the second division—which is its restriction to a finite number—it is that it necessitates its restriction to that finite number instead of others by a specific choosing agent, and that entails its temporality; and also it necessitates distributing what is infinite of objects among what is finite of attributes, and that is impossible by necessity. If you say: knowledge, for example, in our case is multiple according to the multiplicity of its object, and likewise other [attributes]. So if knowledge, for example, in His case, may He be exalted, were to take the place of various knowledges... then it would be possible for it, in His case, to take the place of power and the rest of the attributes, due to the common factor of it taking the place of distinct attributes; rather, it would also entail that His essence could take the place of all attributes, and that is rejected by every Muslim. We say: the difference is that the diversity in knowledges is due to the diversity of the object while being united in type; so when unity is assumed in knowledge, for example, the diversity disappears. As for knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes, they are diverse in their realities by genus; so if some of them were to take the place of others, it would necessitate the inversion of realities, and it would necessitate what was previously discussed in the issue of 'the blackness of sweetness'.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents the proof (برهان) for the oneness of God's attributes. The author first states the proof concisely, then expands it into a detailed argument, refutes two possibilities, and finally answers an objection.

The concise proof: If attributes were multiple according to the number of their objects, that would lead to an infinite number of attributes existing, which is impossible. If they were not multiple according to the number of objects, then there would be no reason for a specific number, so they would need a determiner, which would imply they are contingent (حادث), but they are known to be eternal (قديم).

The detailed explanation: Given that each attribute relates to infinite objects (as proven earlier), the attribute is either (1) multiplied according to the infinite objects, leading to infinite attributes—impossible because anything existing must be distinguishable, and the infinite cannot be distinguished; or (2) restricted to a finite number, which would require a specific chooser and implies contingency, and also implies distributing infinite objects among finite attributes—impossible.

The objection: In humans, knowledge is multiple according to its objects, so if God's single knowledge could stand for many instances of knowledge, why couldn't it also stand for power and other attributes? That would lead to absurdity like the essence standing for all attributes.
  The answer: The diversity in human knowledges is due to diverse objects while they are of the same type (نوع); unity in the type removes diversity. But the diversity between knowledge and power is by genus (جنس), so one cannot substitute for another without distorting realities, as previously explained in the 'blackness of sweetness' analogy (a thing cannot be both black and sweet in the same respect).

Key Terms
تغاير
نوع
جنس
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all attributes are interchangeable because they all belong to God and are unlimited.
Correction: Attributes differ in their essential natures (genera). Knowledge and power are different in genus, so one cannot replace the other without distorting their realities.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the idea that God's knowledge could take the place of power?
A) Because knowledge is not eternal.
B) Because they differ in genus (جنس).
C) Because power has no objects.
D) Because it would lead to infinite attributes.

Key Takeaway
Attributes belonging to different genera cannot substitute for one another without altering their essential natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes like knowledge and power, do you ever treat them as interchangeable in your mind? How does this passage challenge you to see each attribute as distinct in its reality?
?
The objector's argument leads to an absurdity that no Muslim accepts. Have you ever accepted a theological claim without fully tracing its implications? What can you do to guard against hidden contradictions in your beliefs?
?
Sanusi uses the example of 'blackness of sweetness' to show that two different realities cannot combine. In your own life, are there areas where you try to mix incompatible concepts (e.g., expecting mercy without justice)? How will you realign your understanding based on this lesson?

Proof That Divine Attributes Cannot Be Finite
Sanusi presents two proofs against the finiteness of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why restricting God's attributes to a finite number leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
Finiteness of attributes would require a selector and leave infinite objects unaccounted.

Sanusi continues his refutation by tackling the second possibility—that attributes are limited to a finite number. He provides two compelling rational proofs: first, finiteness would necessitate a choosing agent, implying temporal origination; second, it would create an impossible mismatch between infinite objects and finite attributes. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the two separate argument phrases.
The passage starts with 'وأما بطلان القسم الثاني' and ends with 'وهو محال ضرورة'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Look for the two reasons Sanusi gives: (1) finiteness requires a specifying agent leading to temporality, (2) distributing infinite objects among finite attributes is impossible.
Each argument is introduced by 'فلأنه' and 'وأيضاً'.
3
Understand the first argument
Why does finiteness imply a choice? If attributes were limited to a specific finite number, there must be a selecting cause—otherwise any number would be possible. That selector indicates temporal origination (ḥudūth).
This ties into the broader proof of God's eternity.
4
Understand the second argument
God's attributes have infinitely many objects (e.g., all knowable things). If attributes were finite, they couldn't cover all objects, which is absurd.
This is a quantitative impossibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: attributes cannot be finite. This sets the stage for the objection about knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلان القسم الثاني منه ـ وهو اختصاصها بعددٍ متناه ـ : فلأنَّهُ يقتضي اختصاصها بذلك العددِ المتناهي بدلاً عن غيرِهِ مُخصصاً مختاراً ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها ، وأيضاً يلزم توزيع ما لا يتناهى مِنَ المتعلقاتِ على ما يتناهى مِنَ الصفاتِ ، وهو محال ضرورة . فإِنْ قلت : العلمُ مثلاً في حقنا مُتعدِّد بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقِهِ، وكذا غيرُه ، فلو قامَ العلمُ مثلاً في حقِّهِ تعالى مَقامَ علوم . . لجازَ أنْ يقومَ في حقِّهِ تعالى مقام القدرة وسائر الصفاتِ ؛ بجامع قيامِهِ مَقامَ صفاتٍ متغايرةٍ ، بل ويلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يجوز قيامُ ذاتِهِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ ممَّا يأباه كلُّ مسلم . قلنا : الفرقُ أنَّ التغاير في العلوم لأجل التغاير في المتعلَّقِ مع الاتحادِ في النوع ، فحيثُ فُرِضَتِ الوحدةُ في مثلاً زال التغايرُ ، أمَّا العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفاتِ فمتغايرة في حقائقها جنساً ، فلو قام بعضُها مَقامَ بعضٍ للزمَ قلْبُ الحقائقِ ، ولزمَ ما تقدَّمَ في مسألة ( سوادِ حلاوة ).
As for the invalidity of the second division — which is that they (the attributes) are restricted to a finite number — it is because it would necessitate their restriction to that finite number instead of other than it by a specifying agent who chooses, and that entails their temporal origination. Also, it would necessitate distributing what is infinite of the objects (of attributes) among what is finite of the attributes, and that is impossible necessarily. If you say: Knowledge, for example, in our case is multiple according to the multiplicity of its object, and likewise other attributes. So if knowledge, for example, in His, exalted, right stood in place of different sciences, it would be possible for it to stand in His, exalted, right in place of power and the rest of the attributes, by the commonality of its standing in place of differing attributes. Rather, it would even be necessary to allow that His Essence stand in place of all attributes, and that is rejected by every Muslim. We say: The difference is that the differentiation among the sciences is due to the differentiation in the object while they are united in type. So where unity is assumed in, for example, the differentiation disappears. As for knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes, they differ in their realities generically. So if some of them stood in place of others, it would necessitate the inversion of realities, and what was previously mentioned in the issue of 'the blackness of sweetness' would follow.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the second division of the argument concerning divine attributes — the division that claims attributes are limited to a finite number. Sanusi first presents the proof: if the attributes were limited to a finite number, that would require a specific choice by a determining agent, which implies that the attributes are temporally originated (since choice among equals indicates temporality). Additionally, it would require distributing an infinite number of objects (e.g., all knowable things) among a finite number of attributes, which is impossible. Then Sanusi presents an objection from a hypothetical questioner. The objector argues: In us, knowledge is multiple because it has multiple objects (e.g., knowledge of Zayd and knowledge of Amr are two knowledges). If God's knowledge is one, it could stand in place of many sciences; by analogy, it could also stand in place of power and other attributes, since the same reasoning (multiplicity in us) applies to all attributes. Indeed, the objector pushes further, claiming that if this objection is accepted, one could even argue that God's Essence could stand in place of all attributes, which no Muslim accepts. Sanusi answers by distinguishing between the way attributes differ in the created realm and the way they differ in God. Among created sciences, the difference is only due to different objects, but the essence (type) of knowledge is the same. If one knowledge were to encompass all objects, the differentiation would vanish. However, between knowledge and power, the difference is not just in objects but in their very essence (different genera). So if knowledge were to substitute for power, it would invert the realities of the attributes. Thus the analogy fails. Sanusi concludes by referencing a previous discussion about 'the blackness of sweetness' (sawād ḥalāwah) which is a traditional example of impossible inversion of realities.

Key Terms
بطلان القسم الثاني
اختصاص الصفات بعدد متناه
حدوث
توزيع ما لا يتناهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi give as the first reason why attributes cannot be finite?
A) It would require a specifying agent, implying temporality.
B) It would contradict God's omnipotence.
C) The Qur'an says attributes are infinite.
D) It would make attributes created.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes cannot be finite because finiteness would require a temporal selector and cannot accommodate infinite objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously limit God's power or knowledge by thinking some situation is beyond His reach? How does today's lesson challenge that unspoken assumption in your heart?
?
The proof shows that finite attributes would require a temporal selector. Have you ever secretly thought that God's attributes are somehow limited by your own understanding of them? What step will you take this week to actively remind yourself of His infinite attributes?
?
If God's attributes are truly infinite, then His mercy, knowledge, and power have no bounds. Is there a sin or failure you have been carrying that you have essentially considered too great for His mercy? How will you let this lesson change that today?

Answering the Objection About Knowledge and Attribute Substitution
Sanusi answers an objection about knowledge and the substitution of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the multiplicity of human knowledge does not imply multiplicity in God's attributes, and why one attribute cannot substitute for another.

Core Idea
Human knowledge differs by object only, but attributes differ in essence; substitution would invert realities.

After proving that attributes cannot be finite, Sanusi anticipates an objection: if human knowledge is multiple (e.g., knowledge of Zayd and of Amr), why can't God's knowledge be multiple? And if one knowledge could cover all objects, could it also cover power? The answer lies in the distinction between accidental multiplicity due to objects and essential generical difference between attributes like knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues: In us, knowledge is multiple because of multiple objects. But if God's knowledge is one, it could stand for many sciences. By analogy, it could stand for power too—even God's Essence could replace attributes, which is absurd.
The objection pushes the argument to a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand Sanusi's reply
Sanusi distinguishes: Sciences differ only in object but share the same type. So a single comprehensive knowledge can cover all objects. But attributes like knowledge and power differ in essence (genus). Substitution would invert their realities.
The key is 'الاتحاد في النوع' vs 'التغاير في الجنس'.
3
Grasp the principle of inversion of realities
If knowledge could act as power, that would change what knowledge is—like saying blackness is sweetness. That is impossible.
Sanusi references a known example: 'sawād ḥalāwah' (blackness of sweetness).
4
Apply the distinction
Thus the objection fails: multiplicity in human knowledge is accidental, not essential. God's attributes are distinct in essence, so they cannot substitute for each other.
This upholds the earlier proof that attributes are not finite and are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the invalidity of the second division — which is that they (the attributes) are restricted to a finite number — it is because it would necessitate their restriction to that finite number instead of other than it by a specifying agent who chooses, and that entails their temporal origination. Also, it would necessitate distributing what is infinite of the objects (of attributes) among what is finite of the attributes, and that is impossible necessarily. If you say: Knowledge, for example, in our case is multiple according to the multiplicity of its object, and likewise other attributes. So if knowledge, for example, in His, exalted, right stood in place of different sciences, it would be possible for it to stand in His, exalted, right in place of power and the rest of the attributes, by the commonality of its standing in place of differing attributes. Rather, it would even be necessary to allow that His Essence stand in place of all attributes, and that is rejected by every Muslim. We say: The difference is that the differentiation among the sciences is due to the differentiation in the object while they are united in type. So where unity is assumed in, for example, the differentiation disappears. As for knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes, they differ in their realities generically. So if some of them stood in place of others, it would necessitate the inversion of realities, and what was previously mentioned in the issue of 'the blackness of sweetness' would follow.

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the second division of the argument concerning divine attributes — the division that claims attributes are limited to a finite number. Sanusi first presents the proof: if the attributes were limited to a finite number, that would require a specific choice by a determining agent, which implies that the attributes are temporally originated (since choice among equals indicates temporality). Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The multiplicity of human knowledge (e.g., knowledge of different things) proves that God's knowledge must also be multiple.
Correction: Human knowledges are multiple only because of different objects, but the essence of knowledge is one. A single divine knowledge can encompass all objects without multiplicity. This does not apply to different attributes like knowledge and power, which differ in essence.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say that knowledge cannot substitute for power?
A) Because power is stronger than knowledge.
B) Because knowledge and power differ in their essential nature (genus).
C) Because God's knowledge is infinite.
D) Because power is not needed for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Human knowledge is multiple due to objects, but God's knowledge is one in type; attributes differ in essence, so substitution is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names (al-'Alim, al-Qadir, etc.), do you truly feel the distinctness of each attribute, or do you unconsciously blur them together? How would your du'a change if you honored each attribute as a unique and perfect reality?
?
The objector in this passage tried to fit God into a human logical framework. Is there an area of your faith where you have reduced God's majesty to fit your own reasoning, rather than submitting to His revealed description? What would it take to let go of that framework and accept the distinctness of His attributes as taught by the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
If the inversion of realities is impossible for God, how much more should we be careful not to distort His attributes in our speech and belief? Take a moment to repent for any time you described God in a way that diminished His perfection or conflated His attributes.

Objection Against the Unity of Knowledge and Its Answer
Sanusi presents and refutes a shubha against the unity of God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the objection against the unity of God's knowledge and Sanusi's refutation based on the distinction between essential and accidental differences in knowledge.

Core Idea
The unity of God's knowledge does not collapse into other attributes because multiplicity in created sciences is accidental, not essential.

Sanusi presents a powerful objection that some thinkers raised against the proof of the unity of God's knowledge, followed by a precise answer. Understanding this exchange clarifies how kalam defends divine simplicity while maintaining meaningful distinctions among the attributes, without falling into anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage, which presents the shubha. Note how the objector uses analogy from the witnessed world to argue that knowledge must be multiple if its objects are multiple.
The objector extends the argument to other attributes, claiming that if knowledge can be one despite multiple objects, then by parity one attribute could replace all others.
2
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
The objector pushes the logic to an extreme: if we accept that a single knowledge stands in place of many sciences, then why not allow it to stand for power, will, and even the divine Essence itself? This is presented as an absurd conclusion.
The reductio relies on the assumption that all attributes are similar in their multiplicity in the witnessed world.
3
Analyze Sanusi's answer
Read Sanusi's answer. He points out that the multiplicity of created sciences is not in the essence of knowledge, but only in their objects. A single comprehensive knowledge would remove that multiplicity.
This shows that the analogy between knowledge and other attributes fails because those other attributes differ in essence, not merely in objects.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this answer preserves the unity of God's knowledge while affirming that other attributes like power and will are distinct in essence. The objection is thus refuted without compromising tawhid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه شبهة على سبيل المعارضة لدليل الوحدة . وتقريرها أن يُقالَ : العلم قد تقرَّرَ في الشاهِدِ تعدُّدُهُ بحَسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقاتِهِ ، فلوِ اتَّحد العلمُ القديمُ مثلاً لقامَ مَقامَ علوم مختلفة بالنسبة إلينا ، والملازمة ظاهرة . أما بطلانُ التالي : فلأنَّ قيامَ العلمِ مَقامَ علوم مختلفةٍ يُوجِبُ جوازَ قيامِهِ مَقامَ سائرِ صفاتِهِ ؛ كالقدرةِ والإرادة وغيرهما ؛ بجامع أنَّ التعدُّد والاختلاف لتلك الصفات قد تقرَّرَ وجوبُهُ لجميعها في الشاهد ، فإذا لم يُعتمد عليهِ في بعضها بالنسبة إلى الغائبِ .. وجبَ ألا يُعتمد عليهِ بالنسبة إليه في سائرها ، بل إذا لم يُوثَق بما تقرَّرَ وجوبُهُ مِنْ ذلكَ في الشاهد .. لزمَ أنْ يجوز قيامُ الذاتِ العليَّةِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ باطل بإجماع المسلمين . أجابَ عن هذه الشبهةِ : بأنَّ العلوم الحادثةَ مثلاً وإن اختلفت فليس اختلافُها في نفس حقيقة العلم ، بل اختلافها إنَّما هو باختلافِ مُتعلَّقاتِها لما تعدَّدَتْ آحادُ العلم الشخصيَّةُ ، فحيثُ فُرِضَ علمٌ واحدٌ بالشخص يعمُّ جميعَ المتعلقاتِ.. زالَ ذلكَ الاختلاف ؛ ضرورةً
This is a doubt by way of counterargument against the proof of unity. Its formulation is that it is said: Knowledge has been established in the witnessed world as multiple according to the multiplicity of its objects. So if the eternal knowledge, for example, were one, it would stand in place of different sciences relative to us, and the implication is apparent. As for the invalidity of the consequent: it is because the standing of knowledge in place of different sciences necessitates the permissibility of its standing in place of the rest of His attributes, such as power, will, and others, by the commonality that multiplicity and difference for those attributes have been established as necessary for all of them in the witnessed world. So if it is not relied upon in some of them with respect to the unseen, then it must not be relied upon with respect to it in the rest of them. Rather, if one does not trust what has been established as necessary from that in the witnessed world, then it becomes necessary to allow the standing of the Supreme Essence in place of all attributes, and that is false by the consensus of Muslims. He answered this doubt: That the originated sciences, for example, although they differ, their difference is not in the very reality of knowledge; rather, their difference is only due to the difference of their objects, since the individual instances of knowledge become multiple. So where a single individual knowledge that encompasses all objects is assumed, that difference disappears, necessarily.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is an objection (شبهة) raised against the proof of the unity of God's knowledge, followed by its answer. The objector argues by analogy from the witnessed world (الشاهد) to the unseen (الغائب). In the witnessed world, knowledge is multiple because its objects are multiple. Therefore, if God's knowledge is one, it would have to stand in place of many different sciences. But then, by the same reasoning, that one knowledge could also stand in place of other attributes like power and will, since those are also multiple in the witnessed world. If we deny that, consistency demands that we allow the divine Essence itself to stand in place of all attributes, which is absurd and rejected by all Muslims. Thus, the objector concludes that the unity of knowledge is false. Sanusi answers by clarifying the nature of the multiplicity of created sciences. The difference among created sciences is not essential (ليس اختلافها في نفس حقيقة العلم); rather, it is merely due to the distinct objects they are attached to, resulting in multiple individual instances of the same essential type of knowledge. If we imagine a single comprehensive knowledge that includes all objects, the numerical multiplicity disappears. In other words, the difference is accidental, not essential. Therefore, the analogy between knowledge and other attributes (like power and will) fails, because the latter differ in essence (genus) from knowledge, not merely in objects. Hence, the objection does not undermine the unity of God's knowledge.

Key Terms

دليل الوحدة
الشاهد والغائب
العلم القديم
العلوم الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unity of God's knowledge would allow a single attribute to replace all other attributes, ignoring that essential differences prevent such substitution.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the multiplicity of created sciences is only due to their objects, not their essence. A single comprehensive knowledge would remove that multiplicity, but it does not follow that it can replace attributes of different essential categories like power or will.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's answer, why does the shubha about the unity of knowledge fail?
A) Because God's knowledge is not like human knowledge at all.
B) Because the multiplicity of created sciences is not in their essence but only in their objects; a single comprehensive knowledge would remove that multiplicity.
C) Because the objector failed to prove that knowledge is multiple in the eternal realm.
D) Because the shubha contradicts the consensus of Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's answer shows that the multiplicity of created sciences is accidental (due to objects), not essential, so divine knowledge can be one without conflicting with other distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that God's attributes must be like our own limited attributes, and how does that assumption affect your trust in His perfect knowledge of every detail of your life? Will you today consciously affirm that God's knowledge encompasses all your sins and struggles without the limitations of human understanding?
?
The objector's error was treating accidental multiplicity in our knowledge as essential. In what area of your life do you confuse the accidental with the essential, causing you to misunderstand God? Commit to examining one such assumption today.
?
If you truly believed that God's knowledge is one and perfectly comprehensive, would you still hide some deeds from Him or despair that He doesn't fully understand your situation? What small step can you take to live in the reality of His encompassing knowledge?
