The Doubt Regarding the Unity of Knowledge and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents a doubt and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the doubt raised against the unity of God's knowledge and how it is refuted by distinguishing between the essence of knowledge and its objects.

Core Idea
Multiplicity comes from objects, not essence; one knowledge can encompass all.

This lesson presents a key challenge to the proof that God's knowledge is one. The opponent argues that in our experience knowledge is multiple because it has many objects, so God's knowledge should also be multiple. Sanusi answers by showing that the multiplicity arises from the objects, not from the essence of knowledge itself. Thus, a single divine knowledge can cover all objects without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt formulation
Read the opening lines where the doubt is presented as an opposition to the proof of unity. Notice how it uses the witnessed world to argue for multiplicity.
The doubt claims that one knowledge cannot stand in place of many different sciences.
2
Understand the answer: difference in objects vs essence
Sanusi's answer distinguishes between the essence of knowledge and its objects. The multiplicity in temporal sciences is due to their objects, not the essence of knowledge itself.
When one knowledge encompasses all objects, the difference disappears.
3
Grasp the key distinction: same type vs different type
The summary clarifies that standing in place of many is permissible when the type is unified, but impossible when types differ, as it would invert realities and gather opposites.
Example: knowledge cannot stand in place of power because they differ in type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه شبهة على سبيل المعارضة لدليل الوحدة . وتقريرها أنْ يُقالَ : العلمُ (۱) قد تقرَّرَ في الشاهِدِ تعدُّدُهُ بحَسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقاتِهِ ، فلوِ اتَّحد العلمُ القديمُ مثلاً لقامَ مَقامَ علوم مختلفة بالنسبة إلينا ، والملازمة ظاهرة . أما بطلانُ التالي : فلأنَّ قيامَ العلمِ مَقامَ علوم مختلفةٍ يُوجِبُ جوازَ قيامِهِ مَقامَ سائرِ صفاتِهِ ؛ كالقدرةِ والإرادة وغيرهما ؛ بجامع أنَّ التعدُّد والاختلاف لتلك الصفات قد تقرَّرَ وجوبُهُ لجميعها في الشاهد ، فإذا لم يُعتمد عليهِ في بعضها بالنسبة إلى الغائبِ .. وجبَ ألا يُعتمد عليهِ بالنسبة إليه في سائرها ، بل إذا لم يُوثق بما تقرَّرَ وجوبُهُ مِنْ ذلكَ في الشاهد .. لزمَ أنْ يجوز قيامُ الذاتِ العليَّةِ مَقامَ الصفاتِ كلها ، وذلكَ باطل بإجماع المسلمين . أجابَ عن هذه الشبهةِ : بأنَّ العلوم الحادثة مثلاً وإن اختلفت فليس اختلافُها في نفس حقيقة العلم ، بل اختلافها إنَّما هو باختلافِ مُتعلَّقاتِها لما تعدَّدَتْ آحادُ العلم الشخصيَّةُ ، فحيثُ فُرِضَ علمٌ واحدٌ بالشخص يعمُّ جميعَ المتعلقاتِ.. زالَ ذلكَ الاختلاف ؛ ضرورةً الوجودين فأكثر وجوداً واحداً [؟] ، مثلاً : القدرة تضاد العجز في نفسها ، ومن حيث قيامها مقام غيرها من الصفات لا تضاده ، ثم كذلك في سائرها . عكاري » ( ق ۲۲۷ ) • (۱) أي : مثلاً ؛ بدليل السياق بعد ، فهو من باب الحذف من الأوائل لدلالة الأواخر . « عكاري » ( ق ۲۲۷ ) توقفِهِ على تعدُّدِ آحادِ العلم بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ أَحادِ المعلومِ ، وقد زالَ ذلكَ بفَرْض الوَحدة . والحاصلُ : أَنَّ قيامَ الواحدِ مَقامَ العددِ عند اتحادِ النوع جائز ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُوجِبُ قَلْبَ حقيقةٍ ، بخلافِ قيامِهِ مَقامَ العددِ عندَ الاختلافِ في النوع ؛ كالقدرةِ والعلم مثلاً ؛ فإنَّهُ لا يمكن أن تقوم صفةٌ واحدةٌ مَقامَهما ؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ قلب الأجناس واختلاف الحقائقِ واجتماع متضاداتٍ في شيءٍ واحدٍ (۱) ، كما تقدَّمَ في وَجْهِ امتناع أنْ يكونَ الشيء الواحد سواداً حلاوة . (۱) إذ لو قام العلم مثلاً مقام القدرة لوجب أن يؤثر وألا يؤثر ، وأن يتعلق بالواجب وألا يتعلق ، وأن ينكشف به المعلوم وألا ينكشف به ، بخلاف ما لو قام مقام علم آخر ؛ فإن التعلق واحد ، وأحكامهما متساوية ، والحقيقة واحدة ، فلا يلزم قلب حقيقة ، ولا اجتماع متضادات . حامدي » ( ص ٢٩٥ ) ، وفي أصل ( ب ) : ( واختلال ) بدل ( واختلاف ) ، و ( المتضادات ) بدل ( متضادات ) .
This is a doubt by way of opposing the proof of unity. Its formulation is that it is said: Knowledge (1) has been established in the witnessed world to be multiple according to its objects; so if the eternal knowledge were one, it would stand in place of different sciences with respect to us, and the entailment is apparent. As for the falsity of the consequent: because the standing of knowledge in place of different sciences necessitates the permissibility of its standing in place of its other attributes, such as power, will, and others; by the common factor that multiplicity and difference for those attributes have been established as necessary for all of them in the witnessed world. So if it is not relied upon concerning some of them with respect to the unseen, then it is necessary that it not be relied upon concerning the rest of them. Rather, if one does not trust what has been established as necessary from that in the witnessed world, it would necessitate permitting the standing of the Supreme Essence in place of all attributes, and that is false by consensus of the Muslims. He answered this doubt: that the temporal sciences, for example, even if they differ, their difference is not in the essence of the reality of knowledge; rather their difference is only by the difference of their objects, because the individuals of personal knowledge become multiple. So when one knowledge in person is assumed to encompass all objects, that difference disappears; necessarily the [؟] two existences [؟] one existence. For example: power opposes weakness in itself, but from the perspective of its standing in place of other attributes it does not oppose it, and likewise in the rest of them. 'Ukari (Q 227). (1) i.e.: for example; by evidence of the context after, so it is of the type of omission from the beginnings by indication of the ends. 'Ukari (Q 227). Its dependence on the multiplicity of individuals of knowledge according to the multiplicity of individuals of the known, and that has disappeared by the assumption of unity. The summary: that the standing of one in place of many when the type is unified is permissible because it does not necessitate the inversion of a reality, contrary to its standing in place of many when there is difference in type, like power and knowledge for example; for it is impossible for one attribute to stand in place of both, because it necessitates the inversion of genera, the difference of realities, and the gathering of opposites in one thing (1), as has preceded in the face of the impossibility of one thing being blackness and sweetness. (1) For if knowledge, for example, stood in place of power, it would necessitate that it be effective and not effective, and that it be related to the Necessary and not related, and that the known be disclosed by it and not disclosed, unlike if it stood in place of another knowledge; for the relation is one, and their rulings are equal, and the reality is one, so neither inversion of reality nor gathering of opposites occurs. Hamidi (p. 295). And in source (B): 'ikhtilal' instead of 'ikhtilaf', and 'al-mutadaddat' instead of 'mutadaddat'.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt (shubha) against the proof of God's unity with respect to His attributes, and then provides an answer. The doubt is formulated as an opposition: in the witnessed world, knowledge is multiple according to its objects; if God's knowledge were one, it would have to act as many different sciences, entailing that one attribute could stand in for all attributes, even to the point of the Supreme Essence replacing all attributes, which is false by consensus. The answer clarifies that temporal sciences differ only in their objects, not in the reality of knowledge. When one knowledge encompasses all objects, the difference disappears because multiplicity is due to objects, not essence. The summary distinguishes between standing in place of many when the type is the same (permissible) and when types differ (impossible, as it would invert realities and gather opposites). A footnote illustrates with the example of knowledge standing in place of power leading to contradictions, unlike standing in place of another knowledge.

Critique of the Answer Based on the Unity of Speech
Sanusi critiques the previous answer using the Sunni doctrine of unity of speech despite type differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni critique that the unity of God's speech, despite including different types, undermines the previous answer's claim that different types cannot be united in one attribute.

Core Idea
Unity of speech is not broken by its types—commands and news are one attribute.

Sanusi presents a subtle but powerful critique. The previous answer relied on the principle that different types cannot be united in one attribute. However, the Sunni Imams affirm that God's speech is one reality even though it includes commands, prohibitions, and news. This passage shows how this doctrine forces a rethinking of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a new section heading (matalib) followed by a critique.
The word مطلب indicates the start of a new topic.
2
Identify the Sunni claim
Find where Sanusi states the claim of the Imams of the Sunnah: that speech is one despite difference in type. Note that the type of request (talab) is different from the type of news (khabar).
This claim is the basis of the critique.
3
Understand the consequence
Reflect on the footnote's implication: if speech is one reality, then command would occur with news, which seems problematic. This undermines the previous answer.
The problematic consequence shows the force of the critique.

مطلب : في تحقيق وحدة الكلام ، وما ذهب إليهِ عبد الله بن سعيد بن كُلاب في المسألة ] وهذا الجواب حسن ، لكنَّهُ يُعكِّرُ عليهِ ادّعاءُ أَئِمَّةِ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ عنهم وحدةَ الكلام مع اختلافِهِ بالنوع (۲) ؛ فإنَّ نوع الطلب ليس نوع الخبر ! أمَّا الأمر والنهي فيندرجانِ في حقيقة الطلب ، فالاختلافُ فيهما (۲) وعليه : يقع الأمر بالخبر ، والنهي بالحصّ [؟] ، إذ الفرض أنه حقيقة واحدة !
Request: Regarding the verification of the unity of speech, and what Abdullah bin Saeed bin Kullab held on the matter. This answer is good, but it is disturbed by the claim of the Imams of the Sunnah, may God be pleased with them, of the unity of speech despite its difference in type (2); for the type of request is not the type of news! As for command and prohibition, they are subsumed under the reality of request, so the difference is in them. (2) And based on it: command occurs with news, and prohibition with ... [؟], since it is assumed to be one reality!
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage opens a new section (matalib) on the unity of speech (wahdat al-kalam) and critiques the previous answer. The critique states that the answer is good, but it is disturbed by the Sunni doctrine that God's speech is one in essence even though it includes different types, such as command and news. The type of request (talab) is not the type of news (khabar), yet the Imams of the Sunnah affirm that speech is one. The footnote adds that if speech is one reality, then command would occur with news and prohibition with something else, which is problematic. This critique undermines the principle used in the answer that different types cannot be united in one attribute.

The Unity of Divine Speech and Its Types
The author presents the Sunni doctrine of unity of speech and the difficulty posed by different types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni imams affirmed the unity of divine speech despite its diversity into types such as command and report.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one in essence yet diverse in type.

The unity of divine speech is a core Sunni doctrine, but it raises a puzzle: how can speech be one if it includes commands, prohibitions, and reports? This lesson explores the problem and the initial response from Sunni scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how the author states the Sunni doctrine and the problem.
The passage is from a theological investigation.
2
Identify the two main types
The passage mentions 'request' (talab) and 'report' (khabar) as distinct types. Command and prohibition fall under request.
3
Understand the logical difficulty
If speech is one, then a command would be no different from a report, leading to absurdity (command would be a report, prohibition would be a...).
This is the crux of the problem.
4
Reflect on the Sunni position
The Sunni imams held unity despite type-difference. How can that be? The lesson sets the stage for further discussion.
The answer is not fully given here; it continues in the next lesson.

مطلب : في تحقيق وحدة الكلام ، وما ذهب إليهِ عبد الله بن سعيد بن كُلاب في المسألة ] وهذا الجواب حسن ، لكنَّهُ يُعكِّرُ عليهِ ادّعاءُ أَئِمَّةِ السنَّةِ رضيَ اللهُ عنهم وحدةَ الكلام مع اختلافِهِ بالنوع (۲) ؛ فإنَّ نوع الطلب ليس نوع الخبر ! أمَّا الأمر والنهي فيندرجانِ في حقيقة الطلب ، فالاختلافُ فيهما (1) إذ لو قام العلم مثلاً مقام القدرة لوجب أن يؤثر وألا يؤثر ، وأن يتعلق بالواجب وألا يتعلق ، وأن ينكشف به المعلوم وألا ينكشف به ، بخلاف ما لو قام مقام علم آخر ؛ فإن التعلق واحد ، وأحكامهما متساوية ، والحقيقة واحدة ، فلا يلزم قلب حقيقة ، ولا اجتماع متضادات . حامدي » ( ص ٢٩٥ ) ، وفي أصل ( ب ) : ( واختلال ) بدل ( واختلاف ) ، و ( المتضادات ) بدل ( متضادات ) . (۲) وعليه : يقع الأمر بالخبر ، والنهي بالحصّ ، إذ الفرض أنه حقيقة واحدة ! تحته وانتصربعض الشيوخ لمذهب الشيخ والجمهور ؛ فقال : لم تنحصر أقسام الكلام فيما ذُكِرَ ؛ فكما جازَ ردُّ الأقسام إلى القسمين جازَ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ قسم آخرُ نسبتُهُ إلى القسمين في الاندراج كنسبة الأقسام إلى القسمين ، فقيل له : وكذا الخصم يدَّعي أنَّه لم تنحصر أيضاً أقسام المعاني فيما ذكر منها ، فيجوزُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ ثَمَّ معنى نسبتُهُ إلى العلم والقدرةِ كنسبة العلم إلى سائر العلوم . فإن قيل : يلزمُ ثَمَّ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ . قيل : وذلك لازم أيضاً هنا ؛ فإنَّ الخبرَ لا يُضادُّ النهي ، والأمر يُضادُّه ، فلو كان معنى واحدٌ خبراً طلباً .. لضاد ولم يُضادَّ ، وذلك هو المحالُ الذي ذكرتُم مِنْ حيث المعقولُ ، ولأجل استقامة الجري على هذا المسلكِ العقلي صارَ قومٌ إلى التعدُّدِ في الكلام ؛ هرباً مِنْ لزومِ هذا المحال وقد نُقِلَ ذلكَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدِ بنِ كُلَّابِ ، قال : إِنَّ الكلام اسم لسبع صفات : الأمرُ ، والنهي ، والخبر ، والاستخبار ، والوعد ، والوعيد ، والنداء (۲) ، والكلُّ قديم عندَهُ ، ونُقِلَ عنه أيضاً (۱) أي : من أن الكلام صفة واحدة . حامدي » ( ص ٢٩٦ ) . (۲) في ( شرح معالم أصول الدين » ( ص ٣٦٦ ) عدَّها خمسة ، ولم يذكر الوعد والوعيد
Section: On investigating the unity of speech, and what ‘Abdullah ibn Sa‘id ibn Kullab held on the issue. This answer is good, but it is troubled by the claim of the imams of the Sunna, may God be pleased with them, of the unity of speech despite its difference in type; for the type of request is not the type of report! As for command and prohibition, they are included in the reality of request, so the difference between them is only in respect of the object [of the command]. (1) For if knowledge, for example, stood in the place of power, it would be necessary that it affect and not affect, and that it relate to the necessary [existent] and not relate, and that what is known be disclosed by it and not disclosed, unlike if it stood in the place of another knowledge; for the relation is one, its judgments are equal, and the reality is one, so no change of reality nor gathering of opposites occurs. [From] Hamidi (p. 295), and in copy (b): (disruption) instead of (difference), and (the opposites) instead of (opposites). (2) Upon this: the command would occur as a report, and the prohibition as a [؟], since it is assumed to be one reality! Below it: And some elders defended the doctrine of the Shaykh and the majority; they said: The divisions of speech are not limited to what was mentioned; just as it is permissible to reduce the divisions to two, so it is permissible in reason that there be another division whose relation to the two in inclusion is like the relation of the divisions to the two. So it was said to him: Likewise, the opponent claims that the divisions of meanings are also not limited to what was mentioned of them, so it is permissible in reason that there be a meaning whose relation to knowledge and power is like the relation of knowledge to the other sciences. If it is said: Then it would be necessary that it be opposite and not opposite. It is said: That is also necessary here; for the report is not opposite to prohibition, while command is opposite to it. So if one meaning were both report and request, it would be opposite and not opposite, and that is the impossibility which you mentioned from the rational point of view. And for the sake of staying on this rational path, a group resorted to multiplicity in speech, fleeing from the necessity of this impossibility. And that has been transmitted from ‘Abdullah ibn Sa‘id ibn Kullab: He said: Speech is a name for seven attributes: command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise, threat, and call (2), and all are eternal with him. And it is also transmitted from him (1) i.e., that speech is one attribute. [From] Hamidi (p. 296). (2) In ‘Sharh Ma‘alim Usul al-Din’ (p. 366) he counted them as five, and did not mention promise and threat.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a theological investigation into the nature of divine speech, specifically addressing whether it is a single eternal attribute or multiple attributes. The author begins by stating that while the answer previously given (likely from a prior discussion) is good, it is troubled by the claim of the Sunni imams that speech is one despite having different types (e.g., command and report are different types). The problem is that if speech is one, then a command (which is a request) would be identical to a report (which is an informative statement), leading to absurdities—e.g., a command would be a report, and a prohibition would be a [something else]. The text then presents a defense by some scholars: they argue that the divisions of speech are not limited to the mentioned types; rather, they can be reduced to two fundamental types: request (which includes command and prohibition) and report (which includes interrogation, promise, threat, etc.). Within each type, differences are only in specific aspects, not in essence. The opponent then raises a parallel objection: if such reduction is allowed, then the opponent could similarly claim that all meanings (like knowledge and power) are one in some sense. The response is that this leads to logical contradiction: a single meaning would have to be both opposite (like command opposes prohibition) and not opposite (like report does not oppose prohibition), which is impossible. To avoid this impossibility, some scholars (notably Ibn Kullab) posited that speech actually consists of seven distinct eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise, threat, and call. The passage ends by noting a variant transmission that Ibn Kullab only counted five attributes (omitting promise and threat). This shows that the Sunni majority maintained the unity of speech, while Ibn Kullab's position was a minority attempt to resolve the logical difficulties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the unity of divine speech means it cannot have any internal distinction; thus command and report must be identical.
Correction: The Sunni position holds that speech is one in essence but admits of different types (e.g., request and report) without compromising its unity. The differences are in the aspect of the object, not in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with the unity of speech?
A) It would make speech divisible into parts
B) A command would be no different from a report
C) Speech would be eternal and created
D) There would be no difference between request and report

Key Takeaway
The Sunni doctrine of the unity of divine speech must account for the apparent diversity of command and report without reducing them to identical realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you more often treat it as a report of facts or as a command demanding obedience? Which one do you tend to ignore, and why?
?
The dilemma posed in this lesson shows that assuming God's speech is simple can lead to contradictions. Where in your own understanding of God do you simplify Him to the point of error?
?
If divine speech is one yet diverse, how should that shape your expectation of God's communication to you today—do you expect only commands, only promises, or both? What does your answer reveal about your heart?

Divine Attributes
advanced
6 min
Ibn Kullab's Seven Eternal Attributes of Speech
The author presents the dialectical argument leading to Ibn Kullab's view of seven eternal speech attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Kullab's proposal that divine speech consists of seven distinct eternal attributes to resolve the logical difficulties of unity.

Core Idea
Ibn Kullab posited seven eternal speech attributes to avoid the impossibility of a single essence containing opposites.

To avoid the logical contradiction arising from the unity of speech (where one essence would have to be both opposite and not opposite), some scholars proposed that speech is not one but multiple attributes. Ibn Kullab identified seven eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise, threat, and call. This lesson examines the argument leading to this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the defense by some elders
Some scholars attempted to defend the unity by reducing all types to two fundamental kinds: request and report. Understand their argument.
2
Follow the opponent's objection
The opponent counters that if reduction is allowed for speech, it could also be applied to other attributes like knowledge and power, leading to a similar problem.
This forces the defense to confront a parallel contradiction.
3
Identify the impossibility
A single meaning that is both report (non-opposite) and request (which includes opposite such as command vs prohibition) would have to be both opposite and not opposite, which is impossible.
This is the key impossibility that motivates Ibn Kullab's position.
4
Learn Ibn Kullab's solution
Ibn Kullab said speech is a name for seven distinct eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise, threat, and call. Each is a distinct attribute, avoiding contradiction.
Note: a variant count of five attributes is also mentioned.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a theological investigation into the nature of divine speech, specifically addressing whether it is a single eternal attribute or multiple attributes. The author begins by stating that while the answer previously given (likely from a prior discussion) is good, it is troubled by the claim of the Sunni imams that speech is one despite having different types (e.g., command and report are different types). The problem is that if speech is one, then a command (which is a request) would be identical to a report (which is an informative statement), leading to absurdities—e.g., a command would be a report, and a prohibition would be a [something else]. The text then presents a defense by some scholars: they argue that the divisions of speech are not limited to the mentioned types; rather, they can be reduced to two fundamental types: request (which includes command and prohibition) and report (which includes interrogation, promise, threat, etc.). Within each type, differences are only in specific aspects, not in essence. The opponent then raises a parallel objection: if such reduction is allowed, then the opponent could similarly claim that all meanings (like knowledge and power) are one in some sense. The response is that this leads to logical contradiction: a single meaning would have to be both opposite (like command opposes prohibition) and not opposite (like report does not oppose prohibition), which is impossible. To avoid this impossibility, some scholars (notably Ibn Kullab) posited that speech actually consists of seven distinct eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise, threat, and call. The passage ends by noting a variant transmission that Ibn Kullab only counted five attributes (omitting promise and threat). This shows that the Sunni majority maintained the unity of speech, while Ibn Kullab's position was a minority attempt to resolve the logical difficulties.

What would it mean to trust that God's revelation has intellectually robust answers?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Ibn Kullab's Seven Speech Attributes and the Refutations
Ibn Kullab's seven attributes, two refutations, and a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Kullab's claim that divine speech comprises seven eternal attributes, and the two main refutations of that view along with a defense.

Core Idea
Seven eternal types of speech? Refuters say: a genus cannot exist without its species.

Ibn Kullab proposed that divine speech consists of seven distinct eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, inquiry, promise, threat, and call. This raised logical and theological problems. Refuters argued that speech as a genus cannot exist pre-eternally without any of its species, and that some of these types reduce to report. A response attempted to defend Ibn Kullab by reinterpreting his intention. This lesson unpacks the doctrine, the refutations, and the partial reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the full argument.
Notice the two transmissions of Ibn Kullab's view.
2
Identify the doctrine and first refutation
Note the logical argument about genus and species: speech cannot exist without any of its seven types.
3
Examine the second refutation
Understand how istikhbar, promise, and threat are reduced to report.
Consider the theological implications of God seeking information.
4
Analyze the response
Consider the defense that Ibn Kullab did not mean the species are actualized eternally, only that the names apply when conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نُقِلَ ذلكَ عن عبدِ اللهِ بنِ سعيدِ بنِ كُلَّابِ، قال: إِنَّ الكلام اسم لسبع صفات: الأمرُ، والنهي، والخبر، والاستخبار، والوعد، والوعيد، والنداء (۲)، والكلُّ قديم عندَهُ، ونُقِلَ عنه أيضاً (1) قدم الكلام فقط، وأنَّ هذه السبعَ مِنْ صفاتِ الأفعالِ إِنَّما تثبتُ للكلامِ فيما لا يزال . ورُدَّ عليهِ: بأنَّ تعقل وجودِ الكلام أزلاً بدونِ واحدٍ مِنْ هذه السبع محال، وهو ظاهر؛ إذ وجودُ الجنس خارجاً في غير نوع مِنْ أنواعِهِ ممَّا لا يمكنُ، وأيضاً: فالاستخبار والوعد والوعيد آيلة إلى الخبر، فلا يحسنُ جعلُها قسيمةٌ لهُ؛ فإنَّ الاستخبار: إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ مِنَ اللهِ تقريراً فهو خبرٌ، والاستفهام على حكم الاستعلام .. لا يليقُ بعلام الغيوب، وإنْ أُرِيدَ بهِ طلب الإخبارِ رجع إلى الأمرِ، والوعد خبر عن الثواب، والوعيد خبرٌ عن العقاب، واختلافُ المخبَراتِ لا يُغيِّرُ حقيقة الخبرِ. وأجابَ بعضُ المُحققين عنِ الردَّ الأَوَّلِ: بأَنَّ عبدَ اللهِ بنَ سَعِيدِ إِنَّما أرادَ أَنَّ الكلامَ لا يُسمَّى أمراً ولا نهياً إلا عند وجودِ المأمور والمنهي لأنَّ الكلام لا يتعلَّقُ بهما إلا عند وجودهما؛ فَإِنَّهُ أَجِلُّ مِنْ أَنْ يعتقد مثل هذا
And that was transmitted from Abdullah ibn Sa'id ibn Kullab: he said, 'Kalam is a name for seven attributes: command, prohibition, report, inquiry, promise, threat, and call, and all are eternal according to him.' And it was also transmitted from him that he affirmed the eternity of speech only, and that these seven are from the attributes of acts and are established for speech in what is not yet (fīmā lā yazāl). It was refuted: by saying that the conceptualization of speech existing eternally without any one of these seven is impossible, and it is obvious; for the existence of a genus outside of any of its species is impossible. Also: Istikhbar, promise, and threat revert to report; so it is not proper to make them coordinate with it. For istikhbar: if it is from God as a confirmation (taqrīran), it is a report; and seeking information (istifhām) in the manner of inquiry is not fitting for the Knower of the Unseen; and if by it request for information is meant, it reverts to command. Promise is a report about reward, and threat is a report about punishment; and the diversity of what is reported does not change the reality of the report. And some researchers answered the first refutation: that Abdullah ibn Sa'id only meant that speech is not called 'command' or 'prohibition' except when the commanded and the prohibited exist, because speech is not related to them except when they exist; for he is too exalted to believe such a thing.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Kullab's view and a refutation with a partial response. Ibn Kullab held that kalam (divine speech) is a name for seven eternal attributes: command, prohibition, report, inquiry, promise, threat, and call. According to one transmission, he only affirmed the eternity of speech itself, while these seven are actually attributes of acts (ṣifāt afʿāl) that become attributed to speech in time (fīmā lā yazāl). The first refutation argues that it is impossible for speech (as a genus) to exist eternally without any of its species (these seven types). This is a logical point: a genus cannot exist without some species. The second refutation argues that istikhbar, promise, and threat all reduce to khabar (report), so they should not be considered separate types. Istikhbar from God is either a confirmation (which is a report) or a request for information, which is unfitting for God; if it means requesting a report, it becomes a command. Promise and threat are simply reports about reward and punishment, and different subjects do not change the nature of report. Then a response by some researchers addresses only the first refutation: they claim that Ibn Kullab did not mean that these seven are actualized eternally, but rather that speech is only called 'command' or 'prohibition' when the commanded or prohibited exists, since speech relates to them only at that time; Ibn Kullab is too exalted to believe that speech is not command or prohibition until the moment of address. This response attempts to defend Ibn Kullab by clarifying that his view does not require the species to be actualized in pre-eternity.

Key Terms
كلام
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار
وعد
وعيد
نداء
قديم
صفات الأفعال
جنس
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Kullab held that the seven types of speech are all equally eternal and actualized in pre-eternity.
Correction: The response shows that Ibn Kullab likely meant that speech is not called 'command' or 'prohibition' except when the commanded or prohibited exists, implying the species are not actualized in pre-eternity but rather are attributes of acts that manifest in time.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation against Ibn Kullab's view?
A) Istikhbar and promise are not eternal.
B) It is impossible for a genus (speech) to exist without any of its species (types) in pre-eternity.
C) The seven attributes are not all distinct.
D) Ibn Kullab contradicted himself.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Kullab's seven-attribute doctrine faces logical objections regarding genus-species necessity and reducibility, but a defense reinterprets his intent to avoid the problem of pre-eternal actualization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about Ibn Kullab’s seven attributes, do you feel a desire to categorize God’s speech into neat boxes? How does that tendency reflect a human need for control over divine mysteries? Commit to approaching God’s attributes with humility, not as a system to be mastered.
?
The refutation argues that some of these attributes reduce to report. Are there ways you reduce God’s communication to mere information, ignoring its commanding and prohibiting force? How can you listen to God’s speech this week not just as news but as a call to action?
?
Ibn Kullab’s response suggests that God’s speech is not called 'command' until there is someone to command. Do you assume that God’s attributes are only relevant when they apply to you? Where in your life do you ignore parts of God’s speech because they don’t seem immediately applicable? What will you do about it today?

Abu Ishaq's Solution: All Types Reduce to Report
Abu Ishaq's reduction of all speech types to report to maintain unity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini resolves the problem of multiple speech attributes by reducing command and prohibition to types of report, preserving divine unity.

Core Idea
All speech is really report — even command and prohibition are reports about inevitability.

In the previous lesson, you saw a refutation that types like inquiry and promise reduce to report. Now Abu Ishaq takes the next step: he argues that all divisions of speech, including command and prohibition, are fundamentally reports. This clever move preserves the unity of the divine attribute of speech. Let's read his concise solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Abu Ishaq's claim. Notice the concise style and the key terms.
2
Understand the problem
Why is it necessary to reduce all types to report? The goal is to maintain the unity (al-waḥda) of the divine attribute of speech. If speech includes multiple types, it risks multiplicity in God's essence.
The problem arises from the Ash'ari principle that God's attributes are eternal and simple.
3
Analyse the redefinition
How does Abu Ishaq redefine command and prohibition? Command (amr) becomes a report about the inevitability (taḥaqqum) of the act; prohibition (nahy) becomes a report about the inevitability (taḥattum) of avoidance.
This redefinition allows both to be subsumed under 'report' (khabar) without contradiction.
4
Consider the implication
Does this mean commands are not real? No, Abu Ishaq does not deny their reality; he reinterprets their nature to preserve divine simplicity and logical consistency.
This is a common point of confusion — see the misconception below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتزم الأستاذ أبو إسحاق ردَّ جميع أقسام الكلام إلى الخبر؛ لينتظمَ القولُ بالوَحدةِ، فقال: الأمرُ خبر عن تحقُّمِ الفعل، والنهي خبر عن تحتُّم التركِ
And the master Abu Ishaq (al-Isfarayini) committed to reducing all divisions of kalam to report (khabar), in order to make the doctrine of unity consistent. He said: 'Command is a report about the inevitability of the act, and prohibition is a report about the inevitability of avoidance.'
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini's solution to the problem of multiple speech attributes. Faced with the refutation that types like istikhbar, promise, and threat reduce to report (khabar), Abu Ishaq takes a radical step: he reduces all divisions of kalam – including command and prohibition – to report. He thereby maintains the unity (al-waḥda) of the attribute of speech. Specifically, he reinterprets command as a report about the necessity or inevitability (taḥaqqum) of the act, and prohibition as a report about the inevitability (taḥattum) of avoidance. This approach avoids the logical difficulty of a single attribute being both command and prohibition, since both are now types of report with different content. The passage is a concise statement of a theological position that prioritizes logical consistency in divine attributes.

Key Terms
خبر
أمر
نهي
وحدة
تحقق الفعل
تحتم الترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Ishaq denies that command and prohibition are real or that they have any force.
Correction: He does not deny their reality; rather, he reinterprets them as reports about inevitability to preserve divine simplicity and the unity of the attribute of speech.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq, what is a command (amr)?
A) A request for an action.
B) A report about the inevitability of the act.
C) A type of divine will.
D) An eternal attribute separate from report.

Key Takeaway
Abu Ishaq reduces all speech types to report (khabar) to maintain the unity of Allah's speech: command reports the inevitability of action, prohibition reports the inevitability of avoidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you treat it as an inevitable report about what must happen in your life, or as a mere suggestion? How does Abu Ishaq's redefinition change the urgency you feel when reading the Quran's commands?
?

?
How does this understanding of divine speech affect your relationship with the Quran? Does it make you more attentive to the 'report' nature of its verses, or does it risk making you passive? What will you do today to live out this truth?

Objection and Fakhr al-Razi's Reduction
Objection, Fakhr al-Razi's reduction, and refutation via forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to al-Ustadh's definition of speech, Fakhr al-Razi's alternative reduction of command to news of punishment, and the refutation based on God's forgiveness.

Core Idea
Commands for recommended acts lack threat and can't be mere news of punishment.

The passage begins with an objection: al-Ustadh's definition of speech as khabar (news) cannot cover recommendatory commands and prohibitory disapprovals, which carry no determination. Imam Fakhr al-Razi offered a different reduction, claiming command and prohibition are really reports about impending punishment. This lesson unpacks that move and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and al-Fakhr's view
Read the Arabic passage from the start to the refutation. Identify the objection: nadb and karaha have no tahattum (obligation). Then note Fakhr al-Razi's claim: command and prohibition are reports of punishment.
The refutation follows immediately after.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation uses the example of forgiveness for a non-believer despite the presence of command and prohibition. Consider why this undermines Fakhr al-Razi's reduction.
3
Connect to Mu'tazilite definition
The passage ends by noting that this argument also invalidates the Mu'tazilite definition of wajib. Try to see how the same reasoning applies.
This ties back to earlier discussions about obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ أيضاً على الأستاذ : بأنَّ مِنْ أقسام الأمر والنهي الندبَ والكراهة ، وليس فيهما تحتُّم ، فقد خرجا عن الكلام بتفسيره . وذهبَ الإمامُ الفخرُ إلى مثل ما ذهب إليه الأستاذ مِنْ ردَّ أنواع الكلام كلها إلى الخبر ، إلا أنَّه ردّ الأمر والنهي إلى الإخبار بحلول العقاب . ورُدَّ عليهِ : بأنَّ العَفْوَ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى مأمول في حق غير الكافرِ معَ تحقق الأمر والنهي ، وبهذا بطل على المعتزلة حد الواجب بذلك . والقاضي يقولُ : لو قُدِّرَ ورود الأمرِ الجازم بدونِ الوعدِ والوعيد.. لتحقق الأمرُ ، وخالفَهُ الإمامُ والغزالي ، وما صار إليهِ القاضي هو الجاري على قواعدِ أهل السنَّةِ ؛ فإِنَّ الثوابَ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى عندَهم مُجرَّدُ فضْلٍ ، والعقابَ مُجرَّدُ عَدْلٍ ، وتعلقهما بالأمر والنهي بإخبارِ اللهِ تعالى ، لا أنَّهما لازمان لهما عقلاً
And it was also objected to the teacher: That among the divisions of command and prohibition are recommendation (nadb) and disapproval (karaha), and there is no determination in them, so they fall outside speech according to his interpretation. And Imam al-Fakhr went to the same as the teacher in reducing all types of speech to news, except that he reduced command and prohibition to informing about the occurrence of punishment. And it was refuted against him: That forgiveness from God Almighty is hoped for regarding other than the disbeliever despite the realization of command and prohibition, and with this the Mu'tazilite definition of obligatoriness (wajib) is invalidated. And the Qadi says: If it were supposed that a decisive command comes without promise and threat... the command would be realized. And the Imam and al-Ghazali disagreed with him. And what the Qadi adopted is the one running on the foundations of Ahl al-Sunnah; for indeed reward from God Almighty with them is mere grace (fadl), and punishment is mere justice ('adl), and their attachment to command and prohibition is by God's information, not that they are necessarily entailed by them rationally.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents several intertwined arguments. First, an objection is raised against the teacher (al-Ustadh) that his definition of speech as khabar excludes commands like recommendation and disapproval. Then Imam Fakhr al-Razi's view is mentioned: he also reduces all speech to khabar, but specifically reduces command and prohibition to informing about punishment. This is refuted by noting that forgiveness is hoped for non-disbelievers, so punishment is not necessarily tied to command. Then the Qadi (al-Baqillani) offers an alternative: a decisive command itself necessitates obedience even without promise or threat. The author notes that the Imam (likely al-Ash'ari) and al-Ghazali disagreed with al-Baqillani, and that the correct Sunni view is that reward and punishment are not logically necessary consequences of command, but are attached by God's sheer grace and justice, as He informs.

كراهة

خبر
وعد
وعيد
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command and prohibition must entail punishment, otherwise they are not real commands.
Correction: The passage shows that forgiveness is hoped for despite command/prohibition, so punishment is not necessarily entailed. In Sunni theology, reward and punishment are based on God's information, not rational necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation of Fakhr al-Razi mention that forgiveness is hoped for regarding non-disbelievers?
A) Because non-disbelievers always obey commands
B) Because forgiveness shows punishment is not necessarily tied to command
C) Because the Mu'tazilites deny forgiveness
D) Because nadb and karaha are always forgivable

Key Takeaway
Fakhr al-Razi's attempt to reduce command to news of punishment fails because forgiveness can occur despite the presence of command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Qadi's View and the Sunni Position
Qadi's view, disagreement, and Sunni position on reward/punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadi's argument that a decisive command itself necessitates obedience, the disagreement with al-Ash'ari and al-Ghazali, and the Sunni doctrine that reward and punishment are attached by God's information, not rationally.

Core Idea
A decisive command carries obligation even without promise or threat.

After the refutation of Fakhr al-Razi, the passage presents the Qadi's (al-Baqillani's) view: a decisive command (amr jazim) would still be a true command even if no promise of reward or threat of punishment were attached. Al-Ash'ari and al-Ghazali disagreed. The author then endorses the Qadi's position as consistent with Sunni foundations, explaining that reward is pure grace and punishment pure justice, both attached by God's information.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadi's view
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'Wal-Qadi yaqulu'. The Qadi says: 'If it were supposed that a decisive command comes without promise and threat... the command would be realized.' This emphasizes that the binding force of command is inherent.
2
Note the disagreement
Al-Ash'ari (al-Imam) and al-Ghazali disagreed with the Qadi. The author sides with the Qadi. Reflect on why this matters for understanding obligation.
The disagreement centers on whether reward/threat are essential to command.
3
Understand the Sunni foundation
The author explains that in Sunni theology reward is pure grace (fadl) and punishment is pure justice ('adl). They are not rationally necessary consequences but are attached by God's information (bi-ikhbar Allah).
This distinguishes Sunni from Mu'tazilite rationalist necessity.

Concluding Warning on Divine Attributes
Sanusi concludes with a warning about the difficulty and risk of error in discussing divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the study of divine attributes is inherently difficult and requires divine guidance to avoid error.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are a slippery ground; only God's guidance keeps feet steady.

Sanusi closes his discussion of divine attributes with a solemn reminder. The preceding investigations have been deep, but many difficulties remain untouched. He warns that the path of theological inquiry is treacherous, and slips are common without divine assistance. This passage serves as a caution to the student of kalam to approach these topics with humility and reliance on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the tone: the author is concluding and issuing a warning.
2
Identify the warning
Locate the phrase 'مزال الأقدام' (places where feet slip). What does the author say about the investigations of the attributes? How does he describe their scope?
The author uses this metaphor to indicate high risk of error.
3
Reflect on the concluding prayer
The passage ends with a prayer: asking God to make us know Him and not to test us in our religion. Why is this prayer significant after a discussion of divine attributes?
It shows that correct knowledge is a gift from God, not mere intellectual effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ مسألةَ الوَحدةِ في الصفاتِ تتعلَّقُ بها أبحاث قويَّةٌ وإشكالات صعبةٌ يضيقُ مجال النظر فيها إلا أَنْ يُوفِّقَ اللهُ تعالى ، وقد تركنا التعرُّضَ لكثير منها خشية السآمةِ ، وفيما ذكرناهُ مِنْ ذلك كفاية . وبالجملة : فمباحثُ الصفاتِ المعنوية والمعاني متسعة جداً ، وهي مِنْ مزال الأقدام ، إلا أن يثبت الله سبحانَهُ ، نسألُهُ جَلَّ وعلا أَنْ يُعرفنا به ، ولا يَفتَنَّا في ديننا بفضلِهِ
And know: That the issue of oneness in the attributes involves strong investigations and difficult problems which narrow the field of examination except that God Almighty grants success. And we have left off addressing many of them for fear of boredom, and in what we have mentioned of that is sufficient. And in summary: The investigations of the essential attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) and the meanings (al-ma'ani) are very extensive, and they are places where feet slip, except that God, glorified be He, establishes. We ask Him, may He be exalted and high, to make us know Him, and not to test us in our religion by His grace.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark in which the author reflects on the depth of the preceding discussions on divine attributes. He states that the issue of oneness in attributes involves rigorous investigations and difficult difficulties. He admits that he has omitted many of these to avoid weariness. He then summarizes that the investigations of the essential attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) and the meanings (al-ma'ani) are very extensive and are 'places where feet slip' – i.e., areas of potential error. He ends with a prayer asking God for true knowledge of Him and for protection from trial in religion.

The Positive Meaning of God's Oneness
Sanusi defines God's oneness as two negations and three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's oneness means the negation of division and the negation of any equal or partner, and that He is one in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
God's oneness is not a number but the negation of all division and partnership.

Sanusi now applies the previous classification of unity to God. He explains that calling God 'one' is not a numerical statement but a radical negation: God has no parts, no equal, no partner. This lesson establishes the foundational meaning of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the two negations mentioned at the beginning: negation of division and negation of an equal.
2
Identify the three aspects of oneness
The passage states that God is one in essence, one in attributes, and one in actions. Find each statement and note what it negates.
These three aspects cover all possible relations: what God is, what He has, and what He does.
3
Reflect on the terms 'continuous quantity' and 'discrete quantity'
Sanusi uses philosophical terms to summarise the two negations. 'Continuous quantity' refers to internal division (parts), 'discrete quantity' refers to external multiplication (partners).
These terms come from Aristotelian categories, but Sanusi adapts them to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّ المرادَ مِنْ كونه جل وعلا واحداً نفي قبولِهِ الانقسام، ونفي نظير له في الألوهية . وحاصله : نفي الكميَّةِ المتصلة، والكمية المنفصلة . وفي معنى نفي نظير له تعالى في الألوهِيَّةِ : نفْيُ شريك معَهُ في الممكناتِ، فلا مُؤثَّرَ في جميعها سواه، فهو الواحد في ذاتِهِ؛ أي : غيرُ مُؤلَّفٍ مِنْ جزأين فأكثرَ، والواحد في صفاتِهِ، فلا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير، والواحد في أفعالِهِ، فلا شريكَ لهُ فيها ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير وليسَتِ الوَحدةُ الثابتة لذاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى تناهيه في الدقة والصغرِ إلى حد لا ينقسمُ ! وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ جوهراً فرداً، ولا بمعنى أنَّهُ معنى مِنَ المعاني؛ لأنَّ المعاني لا تقبلُ الانقسام، وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ صفةً غير قائم بنفسه، بل محتاجاً إلى محل يقوم به، وقد سبق استحاله ذلك في حقَّهِ تعالى
And if you know this, then know that the intended meaning of His being, may He be exalted and glorified, one (wāḥid) is the negation of His accepting division, and the negation of an equal to Him in divinity. Its summary: the negation of continuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttaṣila) and discrete quantity (al-kammiyya al-munfaṣila). And in the meaning of negation of an equal to Him, exalted, in divinity: negation of a partner with Him in the possible things (al-mumkināt), so there is no effecter (muʾaththir) in all of them other than Him. So He is the one in His essence—i.e., not composed of two or more parts—the one in His attributes, so there is no likeness to Him nor equal, and the one in His actions, so there is no partner with Him in them, nor opponent (ḍidd) nor minister (wazīr). And the unity (al-waḥda) established for His essence, exalted, is not in the sense of His finitude in fineness and smallness to a degree where He is not divisible—otherwise it would necessitate that He be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Nor in the sense that He is a meaning (maʿnā) among meanings, because meanings do not accept division—otherwise it would necessitate that He be an attribute not subsisting by itself, but in need of a substrate (maḥall) to subsist by it, and the impossibility of that with respect to Him, exalted, has preceded.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage defines what it means for God to be 'one' (wāḥid) in Islamic theology, building on the previous classification of unity. The author states that God's oneness signifies two negations: negation of division (refuting composition) and negation of an equal in divinity (refuting polytheism). The summary is the negation of both continuous quantity (extension, parts) and discrete quantity (multiplicity of individuals). The negation of an equal also negates any partner in the realm of created possibles (al-mumkināt), asserting that only God is the true effecter (muʾaththir) in all things. Then the author elaborates that God is one in His essence (not composed of parts), one in His attributes (no like or equal), and one in His actions (no partner, opponent, or minister). Importantly, the author clarifies what this unity does NOT mean: it is not the indivisibility of a very small atom (jawhar fard), which would imply finitude and materiality. Nor is it the indivisibility of an abstract meaning (maʿnā), which would make God an attribute dependent on a substrate—a previous argument established that God is not an accident or attribute needing a bearer. Thus, God's oneness is unique and incomparable, transcending both material and abstract notions of indivisibility.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
نفي الانقسام
نفي النظير
الكمية المتصلة
الكمية المنفصلة
نفي الشريك
الواحد في الذات
الواحد في الصفات
الواحد في الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is summarized by the negation of continuous and discrete quantity?
A) God's oneness means He is an individual among many.
B) God's oneness negates internal division and external equality.
C) God's oneness means He is very small and indivisible.
D) God's oneness means He has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's oneness means He has no parts, no equal, and no partner in any way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God alone is the only effecter (المؤثّر) of all things, then why do you still feel more fear of people's disapproval than fear of Allah? Are you giving someone else a share of your heart's submission that belongs only to Him?
?
When you accomplish something praiseworthy, do you secretly attribute it to your own intelligence or effort, forgetting that God is the sole creator of the action? How can you make the belief 'لا مُؤثَّرَ في جميعها سواه' a reality in your daily life?
?
The oneness of God's essence means He has no parts. Yet, do you sometimes try to 'divide' your worship by giving one part of your life to Him and another part to your desires, as if He does not own every aspect of your existence? What would it take for you to live as though His oneness leaves no room for divided loyalties?

What God's Oneness Does Not Mean
Sanusi refutes two false understandings of God's oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's oneness is not like the indivisibility of an atom nor like the simplicity of an abstract meaning, because both would imply imperfection.

Core Idea
God's oneness is not material indivisibility or abstract simplicity.

After establishing the positive meaning of oneness, Sanusi guards against two common misunderstandings. Some might think God's unity resembles the indivisibility of a tiny atom, or that He is like an abstract concept. Sanusi refutes both by showing the absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two wrong views
The passage mentions two incorrect interpretations of oneness: (1) that God is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), and (2) that God is a 'meaning' (maʿnā) like an accident. Find each.
2
Understand the refutation of the atom view
Sanusi says that if oneness meant extreme smallness to the point of indivisibility, God would be a material atom, which contradicts His transcendence.
The argument is that such a view implies finitude and composition (since atoms are parts of bodies).
3
Understand the refutation of the 'meaning' view
If oneness meant being an abstract meaning (like whiteness), then God would be an attribute dependent on a substrate, which is impossible for the Self-Sufficient.
Sanusi refers back to a previous argument that God is not an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Divine Transcendence: What Reason and Revelation Affirm
Sanusi summarizes the essential divine attributes confirmed by reason and revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key divine attributes that are established by both rational proofs and scriptural evidence, and how they affirm God's absolute transcendence.

Core Idea
God is self-subsistent, utterly transcendent, and all contingent beings depend on Him.

This passage from Sanusi's creed summarizes the essential attributes of Allah that are confirmed by both rational arguments (ʿaql) and transmitted revelation (naql). It is a classic statement of tanzīh (divine transcendence), describing what Allah is and what He is not. Before reading the source, let's prepare to grasp its key claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the emphatic opening 'al-maqṭūʿ bihi' which signals certainty.
The author uses a very compact style; each phrase carries doctrinal weight.
2
Identify the positive affirmations
Find what the passage affirms about Allah: self-subsistent essence, described with attributes of majesty and beauty, omnipotent, etc.
3
Identify the negations
Note the series of 'lā' statements: not an attribute, not a substance, not subject to time, place, change, composition, size, or comparison. This is the core of tanzīh.
These negations prevent anthropomorphism.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how both rational proofs and transmitted revelation agree on these points. The phrase 'wa ṭābaqa fīhi al-maʿqūl al-manqūl' emphasizes their harmony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالمقطوع به بشهادة البراهين العقلية والقواطع السمعيَّةِ : أَنَّهُ ‎جلَّ وعلا ذات قائم بنفسِهِ ؛ أي : مستغن عن المحل والمؤثر ؛ لوجوب وجودِهِ ، موصوف بما لا يُحاطُ بِهِ مِنْ صفاتِ الجلال والجمال ، ليسَ بصفةٍ مِنَ الصفاتِ ، ولا جرماً تجري عليهِ الحوادث والتغيراتُ ، ولا تمرُّ عليه الأزمنة ولا يتخصَّص بالجهات . لا يقبلُ اجتماعاً ولا افتراقاً ، ولا صِغَراً ولا كِبَراً ، لا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير ، ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير ، كلُّ الممكناتِ مُفتقرةٌ إِليه ؛ وهو الغني عن جميعها في الأزلِ وفيما لا يزالُ ، وهو على كلِّ شيءٍ قدير . كلُّ ذلك شهدَتْ بهِ البراهين المنتهية إلى ضرورات العقول وطابق فيها المعقول المنقولُ
And in summary: what is definitively established by the testimony of rational proofs and decisive scriptural evidences is that He, may He be exalted and high, is an essence self-subsistent (dhāt qā'im bi-nafsihi); that is, independent of substrate and efficient cause due to the necessity of His existence, described with what cannot be encompassed of the attributes of majesty and beauty, not an attribute among attributes, nor a substance upon which accidents and changes occur, nor do times pass over Him, nor is He specified by directions. He does not admit conjunction or separation, nor smallness or largeness, no like or similar, no opposite or vizier. All possible things are in need of Him; He is independent of all of them in pre-eternity and forever, and He is over everything powerful. All of this is witnessed by the proofs that lead back to the necessities of intellects, and the transmitted (naql) agrees with the rational (ma'qūl).
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a concise creedal summary of divine attributes as established by both rational (ʿaqlī) and scriptural (samʿī) proofs. The author begins by asserting that God is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt qā'im bi-nafsihi), meaning He needs no place or cause for His existence. He is described with attributes of majesty and beauty that cannot be fully grasped. Then follows a series of negations: He is not an attribute, not a substance subject to change, not temporal, not spatial. He does not admit composition (conjunction/separation), size, or comparison. He has no peer, opponent, or minister. All contingent things (mumkināt) depend on Him, while He is eternally self-sufficient and omnipotent. The author concludes that both rational proofs and transmitted revelation agree on these points. The tone is definitive, using 'al-maqṭūʿ bihi' (what is conclusively established). This lesson grounds the student in the basic language of tanzīh (transcendence) that pervades Sunni kalam.

Key Terms
براهين عقلية
قواطع سمعية
ذات قائمة بنفسها
صفات الجلال والجمال
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine attributes imply anthropomorphism or that God is like created beings.
Correction: The passage emphasizes tanzīh: God is not a substance, not subject to change or limitation, and has no like or similar, thus rejecting any anthropomorphic understanding.

Checkpoint

A) He is composed of parts.
B) He is independent of any substrate or cause.
C) He changes over time.
D) He is limited by space.

Key Takeaway
Allah is a self-subsistent essence, utterly transcendent, and all contingent beings depend on Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that every possible thing is in need of Allah, and He is independent of all. Is there any area of your life where you are living as though you are independent of Him? What is one decision today where you will consciously depend on Him?

The passage affirms that Allah is described with attributes of majesty and beauty that cannot be fully encompassed. Have you ever limited your conception of Allah to something manageable or familiar? Will you commit to seeking knowledge of His names and attributes with humility, knowing you will never fully grasp them?
?
The author states that rational proofs and scripture agree on these truths. Do you truly trust that your intellect, when guided by revelation, can lead you to certainty about Allah? Where do you let doubt or philosophical objections shake your conviction? This week, can you reflect on one rational proof for God's existence and let it strengthen your faith?

Proof of the Impossibility of Incapacity for God
Sanusi proves the impossibility of incapacity for God through a dichotomous argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that incapacity cannot be attributed to God, whether eternal or originated, and why it is a deficiency.

Core Idea
Incapacity is impossible for God because it contradicts His necessary power and perfection.

This lesson presents a core argument from Sanusi's al-ʿAqīda proving that incapacity (ʿajz) is absolutely impossible for God. The proof proceeds by dividing incapacity into two types—eternal and originated—and demonstrating that each leads to an absurdity. Additionally, incapacity is a deficiency, and God must be free from all deficiencies by both reason and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice the phrase 'ضرورة أنَّ كلَّ موجود منحصر في القسمين' as a key premise.
2
Understand the initial division
The argument assumes that every existent is either eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). Therefore, if incapacity existed in God, it must be one of these two.
This division is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Follow the proof against eternal incapacity
Eternal incapacity is impossible because it would prevent God from ever having power, leading to either the coexistence of opposites or the cessation of an eternal attribute.
Key point: power is necessary for God, so eternal incapacity would block it.
4
Follow the proof against originated incapacity
Originated incapacity is also impossible because its opposite (power) is eternal, causing a contradiction, and because all divine attributes of essence are eternal.
Also note the final argument: incapacity is a deficiency, and God cannot have deficiencies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر في العقيدة برهان استحالة العجز على الإله ، وحاصله : أنَّ الإلة لو اتّصفَ بالعجز لكانَ ذلكَ العجز إمَّا قديماً أو حادثاً ؛ ضرورة أنَّ كلَّ موجود منحصر في القسمين ، لكن كونه قديماً محال ؛ لأنَّه يؤدّي إلى استحالة اتصافِ الإلهِ بالقدرةِ ، وقد عرفت وجوبَ كونِهِ قادراً ؛ وذلك لأنَّهُ إِنِ اتَّصف بها معَ العجزِ لزم اجتماع الضدَّينِ ، وإن اتَّصف بها بعد عدم العجزِ لزمَ انعدام ما ثبت قدمُهُ ، وكذا أيضاً كونُ العجز حادثاً محال ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه ـ وهو القدرة ـ قديم ، اتّصف بالعجز مع وجودِ القدرة لزم اجتماعُ الضدَّينِ ، وإلا لزمَ عدم القديم كما سبق . فإن وأيضاً : فاتّصافُ الإلهِ بالعجز الحادثِ محال ؛ لما سبقَ مِنْ وجوب القدم لجميع صفاتِ ذاتِهِ ، واستحالة أنْ يتصف بصفةٍ حادثة • وأيضاً : يستحيلُ أنْ يتّصفَ الإلهُ بالعجز مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّهُ في حقٍّ كلِّ حي نقص ، واتصافُ الإله بالنقائص محال عقلاً ونقلاً
Then he mentioned in al-ʿAqīda the proof of the impossibility of incapacity for God, and its summary: that if God were qualified by incapacity, that incapacity would be either eternal or originated; of necessity, every existent is confined to these two divisions. But its being eternal is impossible because it would lead to the impossibility of God being qualified by power, and you have already known the necessity of His being powerful. And that is because if He were qualified by it (power) together with incapacity, the simultaneous existence of opposites would follow, and if He were qualified by it after the non-existence of incapacity, the annihilation of what is established as eternal would follow. Likewise, the incapacity being originated is impossible; because if it were originated, its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so if He were qualified by incapacity while power exists, the simultaneous existence of opposites would follow, and if not, the non-existence of the eternal would follow as before. Then also: the qualification of God by originated incapacity is impossible, due to what preceded regarding the necessity of eternity for all His attributes of essence and the impossibility of His being qualified by an originated attribute. And also: it is impossible for God to be qualified by incapacity absolutely, because it is a deficiency with respect to every living being, and the qualification of God by deficiencies is impossible both rationally and by revelation.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents the proof from al-ʿAqīda for the impossibility of incapacity (ʿajz) for God. The author summarizes the proof as follows: if God were described by incapacity, it would be either eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). Eternal incapacity is impossible because it would prevent God from ever possessing power (qudra), and we already know God must be powerful. If God acquired power while possessing eternal incapacity, opposites would coexist; if He acquired power after removing incapacity, what was eternal would cease, which is impossible. Originated incapacity is also impossible because its opposite (power) is eternal: if both coexist, opposites coexist; if not, the eternal power would cease. Moreover, originated incapacity is impossible because all divine attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) are eternal. Finally, incapacity is a deficiency (naqṣ), and God is free from all deficiencies by intellect and revelation.

Key Terms
عجز
قديم
حادث
قدرة
ضد
صفات الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity is only impossible if it is eternal; originated incapacity might be possible because it could come and go.
Correction: Originated incapacity is also impossible because it would require an eternal power to coexist with a temporal incapacity (contradiction), or the eternal power would cease. Moreover, all divine attributes of essence are eternal, so an originated attribute cannot belong to God.

Checkpoint
Why is eternal incapacity impossible for God?
A) Because it would prevent God from ever having power.
B) Because it would mean God is not eternal.
C) Because incapacity is a deficiency that could be temporary.
D) Because only created beings can have eternal attributes.

Key Takeaway
Incapacity is impossible for God because it would contradict His necessary power, the eternity of His attributes, and His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof declares that even a moment of incapacity would be impossible for God. How does this truth expose your own habit of attributing 'limitations' to God when your prayers seem unanswered? Will you repent of such thoughts and trust in His absolute power today?
?
If incapacity is a deficiency impossible for God, what does your constant experience of your own weaknesses and inabilities teach you about your utter dependence on Him? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or are you actively relying on His power in every moment?
?
The source insists that God cannot be qualified by any deficiency. How does this challenge the way you sometimes complain about God's 'inaction' or 'failure' to intervene? Will you commit to affirming His perfection even in the face of trials?

Imam al-Haramayn's Proof: Eternal Incapability is Impossible
Imam al-Haramayn's proof using eternal incapability, objection, and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's argument that eternal incapability is impossible, why a similar objection against power fails, and the key difference between power and incapability.

Core Idea
Eternal incapability is impossible because it requires a possible object in eternity.

Imam al-Haramayn presents a clever proof that God cannot be characterized by incapability. He argues that if God were incapable, the incapability would have to be eternal, but eternal incapability is impossible as it requires an object that doesn't exist in eternity. However, an astute objection points out that the same logic would seem to disprove God's power. This passage shows how the objection is answered by distinguishing power as a potentiality from incapability as actual failure.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the proof uses the impossibility of eternal incapability.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that power faces the same problem. Find that part and see why it seems compelling.
3
Understand the answer
The answer distinguishes power as potentiality from incapability as actual failure. See why incapability cannot be eternal while power can.
Key: power relates to future possibility, incapability relates to present failure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاسْتَدَلَّ إِمَامُ الْحَرَمَيْنِ وَغَيْرُهُ عَلَى اسْتِحَالَةِ اتِّصَافِ الْإِلَهِ بِالْعَجْزِ : بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ عَاجِزًا لَكَانَ عَاجِزًا بِعَجْزٍ قَدِيمٍ ; يَعْنِي : لِاسْتِحَالَةِ اتِّصَافِهِ بِالْحَوَادِثِ ، وَالْعَجْزُ الْقَدِيمُ مَحَالٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَسْتَدْعِي مَعْجُوزًا عَنْهُ ، وَالْمَعْجُوزُ عَنْهُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مُمْكِنًا ، وَلَا مُمْكِنَ فِي الْأَزَلِ ، فَلَا عَجْزَ فِي الْأَزَلِ . لَا يُقَالُ : مَا ذَكَرْتُمُوهُ لَازِمٌ عَلَيْكُمْ فِي إِثْبَاتِ قُدْرَةٍ أَوْ قَادِرِيَّةٍ أَزَلًا ; فَإِنَّ إِثْبَاتَ الْقُدْرَةِ يَسْتَدْعِي مَقْدُورًا ، وَالْمَقْدُورُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا مُمْكِنًا ، وَلَا مُمْكِنَ فِي الْأَزَلِ ، فَيَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا قُدْرَةَ وَلَا قَادِرِيَّةَ فِي الْأَزَلِ ! لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ : مَعْنَى الْقُدْرَةِ : صِفَةٌ يُتَأَتَّى بِهَا إِيقَاعُ الْفِعْلِ ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْوَصْفِ بِالْقُدْرَةِ وُجُودُ الْمَقْدُورِ بِهَا ، بَلْ يَأْتِي أَنْ يُفْعَلَ بِهَا حَيْثُ يُمْكِنُ الْفِعْلُ ، وَالْفِعْلُ أَزَلًا مَحَالٌ ، فَتُثْبَتُ الْقُدْرَةُ الْأَزَلِيَّةُ مُتَعَلِّقَةً بِصِحَّةِ الْفِعْلِ فِيمَا لَا يَزَالُ ، فَأَمَّا الْعَجْزُ : فَمَعْنَاهُ تَعَذُّرُ مَا يُحَاوِلُ إِيجَادَهُ ، فَلَا يَثْبُتُ بِمَعْنَى الصَّلَاحِيَّةِ ; لِأَنَّ الصَّالِحَ لِأَنْ يَعْجِزَ لَا يَكُونُ عَاجِزًا فِي الْحَالِ، بَلْ قَادِرًا، فَالْعَجْزُ إِذًا لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِالْفِعْلِ ، لَا بِالصَّلَاحِيَّةِ .
And Imām al-Ḥaramayn and others adduced as proof for the impossibility of God being characterized by incapability: that if He were incapable, He would be incapable by an eternal incapability; meaning: due to the impossibility of His being characterized by originated things, and eternal incapability is impossible; because it requires that which is incapable of (maʿjūz ʿanhu), and that which is incapable of is nothing but a possible thing, and there is no possible in eternity, so there is no incapability in eternity. It cannot be said: what you have mentioned is necessary upon you in affirming power or powerfulness eternally; for the affirmation of power requires an object of power (maqdūr), and the object of power is nothing but a possible thing, and there is no possible in eternity, so it would follow that there is no power and no powerfulness in eternity! For we say: the meaning of power is: an attribute by which the performance of an action is facilitated, and the description with power does not necessitate the existence of the object of power by it, rather it comes that one acts by it where action is possible, and action is impossible in eternity, so eternal power is established as related to the validity of action in the future. As for incapability: its meaning is the failure to bring about what one tries to bring about, so it is not established in the sense of potentiality (ṣalāḥiyya); because one who is suitable for being incapable is not incapable in the present, but rather powerful. Therefore incapability only exists by actuality (al-fiʿl), not by potentiality.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the impossibility of God's incapability by Imām al-Ḥaramayn, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof: If God were incapable, it would be by an eternal incapability (since originated attributes are impossible for God). But eternal incapability is impossible because it requires an object (maʿjūz ʿanhu) that is impossible to occur, which must be a possible thing (mumkin). However, in eternity (al-azal) there is no possible thing (since possible things are created in time). Therefore, no eternal incapability exists. The objection argues that the same logic applies to power: affirming eternal power also requires an object of power (maqdūr), which is a possible thing, and none exists in eternity. So power would also be impossible. The answer distinguishes power from incapability: Power is an attribute by which action is facilitated; it does not require an actual object but is related to the validity (ṣiḥḥa) of action in the future. In eternity, action is impossible, so power is established as relating to future possibility. Incapability, however, means the actual failure to produce something; it is not a mere potentiality. A being that is capable of being incapable (i.e., has the potential to fail) is actually powerful, not incapable. Thus incapability exists only in actuality (bi-l-fiʿl), not as a potentiality (bi-l-ṣalāḥiyya). Hence the objection fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the same reasoning that disproves eternal incapability also disproves eternal power.
Correction: The answer clarifies that power is potentiality related to future acts, while incapability is actual failure. Power does not require an actual object in eternity, only the validity of future action.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof of Imam al-Haramayn conclude that eternal incapability is impossible?
A) Because eternal incapability would require an object that is impossible in eternity.
B) Because eternal incapability would mean God is not perfect.
C) Because eternal incapability would make God dependent on creation.
D) Because eternal incapability would contradict divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn's proof shows that eternal incapability is impossible because it requires an impossible object, while eternal power is possible as potentiality for future acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely powerful and never incapable, what does that mean for the one area of your life where you have lost hope? Will you ask Him for what seems impossible today?
?
The proof shows that God's power is not like our power—it is eternal potential, not limited by circumstances. In what situation have you been acting as if God's power depends on your ability? How will you surrender that today?
?
Imagine you truly believed that God never fails to bring about what He wills. What would you dare to pray for that you have been too afraid to request? Will you pray it now?
