Cumulative Scriptural Evidence for the Vision of Allah
Sanusi defends certainty of vision through cumulative scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiple apparent (ẓāhir) scriptural proofs together produce certainty (qaṭʿ) about the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Many apparent proofs agreeing on one meaning yield certainty.

Sanusi addresses a key challenge: the scriptural evidences for the vision of Allah are each only apparent (ẓāhir), not explicit texts (naṣṣ). How then can they be conclusive? He argues that their abundance and unanimous agreement on the same meaning transforms them into certain proof. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how even one individual proof, like Moses' request, is nearly explicit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the initial clarification about likening vision to vision, not seen to seen.
The first sentence sets a boundary: direction and corporeality are impossible for Allah.
2
Find the core claim
Locate the sentence where Sanusi states that many apparent proofs, due to their abundance and agreement, yield certainty. This is the main argument.
It begins with 'وهذه الأدلة ونحوها...'
3
Understand the refutation of al-Rāzī
Sanusi mentions Ibn al-Tilimsānī's refutation of Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī. al-Rāzī doubted certainty because of lack of rational proof and considered scriptural proofs not explicit. See how the cumulative argument answers this.
4
Examine the strongest individual evidence
Sanusi points out that some individual proofs, like Moses' request (Quran 7:143) and the hadith 'You will see your Lord', are almost explicit texts. Reflect on how they support the cumulative case.
The hadith is widely transmitted (mustafīḍ) and accepted by the imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت الرؤية، أما الجهة والجسمية ولوازمهما فمستحيلة في حقه تعالى، وبالجملة: فالمقصود تشبيه الرؤية بالرؤية فيما ذكر، لا المرئي بالمرئي. وهذه الأدلة ونحوها من أدلة السمع وإن كان كل واحد منها ظاهراً ليس بنص... فهي لكثرتها وتواطئها على معنى واحد تفيد القطع بالرؤية، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرت بقولي: (والظواهر إذا كثرت...) إلى آخره. وقد أشار إلى هذا المعنى شرف الدين بن التلمساني رحمه الله تعالى رداً على الإمام الفخر في ميله إلى عدم القطع بجواز الرؤية لما لم يتضح له الدليل العقلي عليها (1)، والأدلة السمعية رآها ليست بنص، فرد عليه بما سبق؛ وهو ظاهر، على أن بعض تلك الأدلة على الانفراد - كسؤال موسى عليه السلام للرؤية - يكاد أن يكون نصاً في جواز الرؤية (3)، ويقرب منه حديث: «سترون ربكم، فإنه نص» (1)، وهو حديث مستفيض تلقته الأئمة بالقبول.
At the time of vision, as for direction and corporeality and their necessities, they are impossible in His right, Exalted is He. In summary: the purpose is to liken vision to vision in what was mentioned, not the seen to the seen. And these evidences and similar from the evidences of revelation, even if each one of them is apparent (ẓāhir) not an explicit text (naṣṣ)... yet because of their abundance and their agreement on one meaning, they yield certainty (qaṭʿ) regarding the vision. And to this meaning I alluded by my saying: 'And the apparent proofs when they become abundant...' to its end. And Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī, may Allah have mercy on him, alluded to this meaning in refutation of al-Imām al-Fakhr in his inclination to not being certain of the permissibility of vision since the rational proof for it was not clear to him (1), and he saw that the scriptural evidences were not explicit texts, so he refuted him with what preceded; and it is apparent. Moreover, some of those individual evidences – such as the request of Moses, peace be upon him, for vision – are almost an explicit text (naṣṣ) regarding the permissibility of vision (3), and close to it is the hadith: 'You will see your Lord, for it is an explicit text' (1), and it is a widely transmitted (mustafīḍ) hadith which the imams have accepted.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the proof for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) based on cumulative scriptural evidence. Sanusi begins by clarifying that the comparison is between the act of seeing in this world and the act of seeing in the afterlife, not between the seen objects. He then argues that although each individual proof from revelation is only apparent (zāhir) and not an explicit, unambiguous text (naṣṣ), the sheer number of such proofs and their agreement on the same meaning—namely, that believers will see Allah—produces certainty (qaṭʿ). He refers to his own earlier statement about 'al-ẓawāhir idhā kathurat' (apparent proofs when they are numerous). Next, he mentions that Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī used this same argument to refute Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī, who was reluctant to affirm certainty about the vision because he found no clear rational proof and considered the scriptural evidences not explicit. Sanusi then strengthens his case by noting that some individual proofs, such as Moses' request to see Allah (Quran 7:143), are almost explicit, and the prophetic hadith 'You will see your Lord' is explicit (naṣṣ) and widely transmitted (mustafīḍ), accepted by the scholars without objection. Thus, the multitude of proofs compensates for any individual weakness and yields definitive knowledge.

Key Terms
رؤية
ظاهر
نص
قطعي
مستفيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each scriptural proof must be an explicit text (naṣṣ) to prove the vision of Allah.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even if each individual proof is only apparent (ẓāhir), their abundance and agreement produce certainty (qaṭʿ).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why do multiple apparent (ẓāhir) scriptural proofs yield certainty about the vision of Allah?
A) Because each proof is individually explicit (naṣṣ).
B) Because they are numerous and agree on one meaning.
C) Because rational proofs are also available.
D) Because the Mu'tazila accepted them.

Key Takeaway
The abundance and agreement of apparent scriptural proofs produce certainty about the vision of Allah, even if each alone is not explicit.

Refutation of Quranic Objections to the Vision of Allah
Sanusi refutes two Quranic objections to the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes two Quranic verses used by the Mu'tazila to deny the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Quranic verses that seem to deny seeing Allah are actually limited to this world or to comprehensive grasp, not vision itself.

Sanusi now addresses two key Quranic objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing Allah. These verses appear to negate vision entirely, but careful analysis reveals that they are either speaking about a different concept or are restricted to this world. This lesson will guide you through Sanusi's precise refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two objections and Sanusi's responses.
Pay attention to the key terms like idrāk and ru'ya.
2
Understand the first refutation
Sanusi explains that ‘No vision can grasp Him’ does not negate vision because ‘grasp’ (idrāk) means comprehensive encompassing, not mere sight.
3
Understand the second refutation
The verse ‘You shall not see Me’ is a response to Moses' request in this world, so it does not apply to the afterlife.
The logical principle about negation of temporal statements is key.
4
Review the logical argument
Consider how Sanusi uses the principle from logic that the negation of a time-bound statement is understood within that time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا معارضة بقوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام: 103] ولا بقوله جل وعز: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ [الأعراف: 143]. فلا يعارضها قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ لأن الإدراك أخص لإشعاره بالإحاطة، ولا شك أنها منفية مطلقاً. سلمنا أنها الرؤية، لكن المراد في الدنيا، أو هو من باب الكل لا الكلية. ولا قوله جل وعز: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ لأن المراد في الدنيا؛ إذ هو المسؤول لموسى عليه السلام، والأصل في الجواب المطابقة، ولهذا قال: ﴿لَنْ تَرَانِي﴾ ولم يقل: لن أرى، أو: لن تمكن رؤيتي، وقد يستأنس لذلك بما تقرر في المنطق: أن نقيض الوقتية يؤخذ فيه وقتها المعين. هذا مما استدل به المعتزلة من السمع على استحالة الرؤية.
There is no opposition by His statement, Exalted is He: 'No vision can grasp Him' (Quran 6:103), nor by His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'You shall not see Me' (Quran 7:143). For His statement 'No vision can grasp Him' does not oppose them because 'comprehension' (idrāk) is more specific, indicating encompassing, and there is no doubt that it is negated absolutely. Even if we grant that it means vision, then the intended meaning is in this world, or it is a case of the whole but not the universality. And His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'You shall not see Me' does not oppose because the intended meaning is in this world, since it was the request of Moses, peace be upon him, and the principle in a response is correspondence; therefore He said 'You shall not see Me' and did not say 'I will not be seen' or 'My vision is not possible'. And one may seek support for that from what is established in logic: that the negation of a temporal statement is taken with its specific time. This is among the scriptural evidences with which the Mu'tazila argued for the impossibility of vision.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two Quranic verses that the Mu'tazila use to deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. First, Sanusi addresses 'No vision can grasp Him' (Quran 6:103). He argues that this verse does not oppose the proofs for vision because 'idrāk' (comprehensive grasp or encompassing) is more specific than mere 'ru'ya' (vision). The negation of comprehensive grasp does not negate vision, as one can see without fully encompassing. Moreover, the negation of idrāk is absolute and applies in this life and the next, but that does not preclude simple vision. Then he concedes hypothetically that 'idrāk' might mean 'ru'ya', but even then the verse refers to this world, or it is a negation of the whole category without negating every individual instance (the principle of 'al-kull lā al-kulliyya'). Second, he addresses 'You shall not see Me' (Quran 7:143), which was said to Moses. Sanusi argues that this response is specific to this world and to Moses' request at that time. The principle is that a response matches the request, and since Moses asked to see Allah in this world, the answer was 'You shall not see Me in this world'. The verse does not negate vision in the afterlife. He also invokes a logical principle: the negation of a temporal proposition is understood within that specific time. Finally, he notes that this is the scriptural argument used by the Mu'tazila, but he has refuted it by proper contextualization and distinction.

Key Terms
رؤية
إدراك
إحاطة
وقتية
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran categorically denies any possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the verses are either about comprehensive grasp (not simple vision) or are restricted to this world.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute the objection based on Quran 6:103 ('No vision can grasp Him')?
A) He accepts that the verse negates vision altogether.
B) He argues that 'idrāk' is comprehensive grasp, which is negated, not simple vision.
C) He says the verse was abrogated.
D) He claims the verse refers only to the Prophet Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes Quranic objections by showing that they either refer to comprehensive grasp (not simple vision) or are limited to this world, thus allowing the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a Quranic verse and let it shake your conviction about a core belief, without first seeking deeper understanding? How does Sanusi's careful distinction between idrak and ru'ya challenge you to be more humble and scholarly in your approach to revelation? What will you do when you next encounter a verse that seems to contradict what you know?
?
The Mu'tazila held firmly to their interpretation, yet Sanusi shows that they missed crucial nuances. Reflect on your own beliefs: is there a teaching you hold that might be based on a superficial reading of a text? What would it take for you to re-examine that belief with the same rigor Sanusi applies here?
?
When you pray for something, or when God answers a request, do you assume that the answer is absolute and everlasting? Moses asked for vision in this world, and God replied 'You shall not see Me'—limited to that moment. How might this change how you understand God's responses to your own prayers and requests? What does this teach you about God's wisdom and the importance of context?

The Verse Negates Encompassing Vision, Not All Vision
Sanusi argues that the verse must be interpreted metaphorically, negating encompassing vision, not the essence of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari interpretation of 'لا تدركه الأبصار' focuses on encompassing vision rather than all vision.

Core Idea
Negation of encompassment does not negate the essence of vision.

Sanusi begins by rejecting a literal reading that would make God's vision impossible. Instead, he argues that the verse must be understood metaphorically, drawing an analogy with how God's knowledge is described in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to get the gist of the argument.
2
Identify the key analogy
Notice how Sanusi compares vision to knowledge: just as 'لا يحيطون به علما' does not negate all knowledge, 'لا تدركه الأبصار' does not negate all vision.
3
Understand the concept of metaphorical interpretation
Sanusi says the literal meaning is 'impossible with respect to God', so we must interpret metaphorically. What is the metaphor? It is 'ra'yat ihaata' (encompassing vision).
4
Apply the principle to reconcile texts
The author states that texts indicating vision (ru'ya) must be qualified by the negation of encompassment to reconcile them.
This is the method of tawfiq bayn al-nusus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال، فيتعين حمله على مجازه؛ وهو لا يرى رؤية إحاطة، كما أخبر عن نفسه أنه لا يعلم علم إحاطة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه: 110]، ونفي الإبصار الخاص لا يوجب نفي أصل الإبصار؛ وهو الذي ندعيه، وبهذا تعرف أن النصوص الدالة على الرؤية يجب تقييدها بنفي الإحاطة؛ للتوفيق بين النصوص.

سلمنا أن الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية، لكن لا نسلم العموم في الأزمان، بل المراد نفي الرؤية في الدنيا؛ للجمع بين هذا وبين ما اقتضى الرؤية في الآخرة، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفراد، وأن المؤمنين خارجون من هذا العموم للأدلة الواردة فيهم.

أو نقول: إن لفظ الأبصار جمع محلى بالألف واللام يفيد في الثبوت العموم، فسلبه يفيد سلب العموم؛ لأن النفي تابع لما أشعر به اللفظ المثبت، وذلك لا يفيد عموم السلب؛ لأن سلب العموم لا ينافي ثبوت الحكم لبعض الأفراد، فيتحقق بنفي الحكم عن فرد من الأفراد، بخلاف عموم السلب؛ فإنه يكذب بثبوت الحكم لفرد من الأفراد، ولهذا كذب الله تعالى اليهود حيث قالوا: ﴿ إِذْ قَالُوا مَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْءٍ ﴾ بقوله: ﴿ قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ ﴾ [الأنعام: 91]، حيث ادعوا عموم السلب، والدلالة للمعتزلة بالآية تتوقف على تحقق الثاني دون الأول؛ فإن الأشعرية لا تدعي أنه يراه كل أحد، وإنما يراه المؤمنون دون الكافرين، ونقيض الموجبة الكلية التي سلبتها الآية هي السالبة الجزئية التي دلت عليها الآية، لا السالبة الكلية التي لم تدل عليها، فحينئذ نقول بموجبها؛ وهو أنه لا يراه جميع الأبصار، بل أبصار المؤمنين.

هكذا قرر الإمام الفخر هذا الجواب (۱)، وإليه أشرت بقولي: (أو هو من باب الكل لا الكلية) أي: السلب في الآية من باب السلب المعلق بالمجموع من حيث هو مجموع، لا من باب السلب المعلق بكل فرد فرد.

(۱) انظر (معالم أصول الدين) (ص 92).
That [interpretation] is impossible with respect to God, so it must be carried to its metaphorical meaning; which is that He is not seen with an encompassing vision, just as He informed about Himself that He is not known with encompassing knowledge in His statement: {And they do not encompass Him with knowledge} [Ta-Ha: 110], and the negation of specific sight does not necessitate the negation of the essence of sight; and that is what we claim. And by this you know that the texts indicating vision must be restricted by the negation of encompassment; for reconciliation between the texts.

We concede that perception means vision, but we do not concede generality across times; rather the intended meaning is the negation of vision in this world; to reconcile between this and what necessitates vision in the hereafter. Or we claim specification among individuals, and that the believers are excluded from this generality due to the proofs that have come concerning them.

Or we say: The word "absar" (sights) is a plural with the definite article (alif and lam) which in affirmative [constructions] indicates generality. So its negation indicates the negation of generality; because negation follows what the affirmative word suggests, and that does not indicate the generality of negation; because the negation of generality does not negate the establishment of the ruling for some individuals, so it is realized by negating the ruling from one individual. Unlike the generality of negation; for it is falsified by the establishment of the ruling for one individual. For this reason Allah falsified the Jews when they said {When they said: 'Allah has not sent down anything to any human being'} by His saying: {Say: 'Who sent down the Book?'} [al-An'am: 91], when they claimed the generality of negation. The argument for the Mu'tazila from the verse depends on the realization of the second [generality of negation] without the first [negation of generality]; for the Ash'aris do not claim that everyone sees Him, but rather the believers see Him, not the disbelievers. The contradictory of the universal affirmative which the verse negated is the particular negative which the verse indicated, not the universal negative which it did not indicate. Then we speak according to its requirement; which is that He is not seen by all sights, but rather by the sights of the believers.

Thus Imam al-Razi formulated this answer (1), and to it I pointed with my saying: (Or it is from the category of the whole, not the universal) i.e. the negation in the verse is of the category of negation attached to the aggregate inasmuch as it is an aggregate, not of the category of negation attached to every individual individual.

(1) See (Ma'alim Usul al-Din) (p. 92).
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Ash'ari position that the verse "لا تدركه الأبصار" (Sight does not perceive Him) does not negate the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife. The author presents multiple lines of reasoning.

First, he argues that the verse must be interpreted metaphorically (مجاز) because a literal reading that God cannot be seen with any sight is impossible (محال) with respect to God. He interprets the negation as applying to "encompassing vision" (رؤية إحاطة), similar to how God is not known with encompassing knowledge (علم إحاطة). Thus, negating a specific type of sight does not negate the essence of sight itself. This allows for the texts indicating vision of God in the afterlife to be reconciled by restricting them with the negation of encompassment.

Second, the author concessively assumes (سلمنا) that "perception" (الإدراك) means "vision" (الرؤية), but he offers two alternative responses: (a) The verse applies only to this world (الدنيا), not the afterlife; (b) The verse is specified to exclude believers, based on other proofs.

Third, the author presents a more technical logical argument: The word "الأبصار" (sights) is a definite plural, which in an affirmative statement indicates generality (العموم). When negated, it yields "negation of generality" (سلب العموم), not "generality of negation" (عموم السلب). The difference is crucial: negation of generality is true if even one individual is excluded (e.g., "not all sights see Him"), whereas generality of negation would require that no individual sees Him. The author gives the example of the Jews who falsely claimed that Allah had not sent down anything to any human (عموم السلب), and Allah refuted them by pointing to scripture sent down to prophets. For the Mu'tazila to prove their case, they would need to show that the verse implies generality of negation, not just negation of generality. The Ash'aris only claim that believers see God, not everyone, so the verse's negation of generality is sufficient and does not conflict with the ru'ya of believers.

Finally, the author cites Imam al-Razi's formulation of this answer and his own summary: the negation in the verse is like negating the aggregate (الكل) as a whole, not every individual (الكلي) individually.

Key Terms

الأبصار
الإحاطة
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse proves God cannot be seen at all, even in the afterlife.
Correction: The Ash'ari interpretation shows the verse only negates encompassing vision, not vision itself, leaving room for believers to see God in a non-encompassing way.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Sanusi use to support his metaphorical interpretation?
A) The analogy of sight and hearing
B) The analogy of encompassing knowledge in Quran
C) The analogy of creation and command
D) The analogy of worldly and afterlife vision

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is interpreted as negating encompassing vision, not the possibility of all vision of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار', what have you previously believed about the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife? Has Sanusi's argument that it only negates encompassing vision challenged your previous understanding? Are you willing to accept a metaphorical interpretation that reconciles the texts, or do you find yourself resisting it? What does that resistance reveal about your trust in the scholars and your attachment to your own interpretation?
?
Sanusi uses an analogy with knowledge: just as we can have knowledge of God without encompassing knowledge, we can have vision without encompassing vision. How does this analogy affect your longing for the vision of God? Do you desire to see God even if you cannot fully comprehend Him, or does your heart demand complete grasp? What would it mean for you to humbly accept a vision that is real but not encompassing?
?
Imagine standing before God on the Day of Judgment, and He grants you a vision of Himself. Would you be satisfied with a non-encompassing glimpse, or would you covet total comprehension? How does Sanusi's distinction between encompassing and non-encompassing vision prepare your heart for that encounter? Are you ready to submit to God's limits even in the hereafter?

Specifying the Verse to This World or Excluding Believers
Sanusi concedes the meaning but restricts the scope to this world or specifies believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative responses to the Mu'tazila claim: the verse either applies only to this world or is specified to exclude believers.

Core Idea
The verse can be understood as talking about this world, or as general with exceptions.

After the metaphorical interpretation, Sanusi offers additional lines of defense. Assuming perception means vision, he argues the verse could be restricted to this world or its generality specified to exclude believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation.
2
Understand the concession
Sanusi says 'سلمنا' (we concede) that idrak means ru'ya, but then offers two alternatives.
3
First alternative: restriction to this world
The verse negates vision in this world, but does not apply to the afterlife, where ru'ya is affirmed by other texts.
4
Second alternative: specification to individuals
The verse is general, but the believers are excluded by specific proofs that they will see God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Logic of Negation: Generality vs. Universal Denial
Sanusi presents a logical argument distinguishing negation of generality from generality of negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between negation of generality and generality of negation, and how it refutes the Mu'tazila argument.

Core Idea
Negating a universal does not prove a universal negation.

Sanusi now deploys a sophisticated logical and linguistic argument. He analyzes the grammatical form of 'لا تدركه الأبصار' to show that it only negates the generality of the subject, not that every individual sight fails to perceive God. This allows for the ru'ya of believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, paying attention to the logical terms.
2
Distinguish the two types of negation
Understand the difference between 'salb al-umum' and 'umum al-salb'. The first negates the whole, the second negates each individual.
Use the example of the Jews to cement the distinction.
3
Apply to the verse
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' means 'not all sights perceive Him' not 'no sights perceive Him'. This is the Ash'ari claim.
4
Understand the summary with al-Razi
Sanusi ends by citing Imam al-Razi's formulation: it is negation of the whole (al-kull) not the universal (al-kulliyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال، فيتعين حمله على مجازه؛ وهو لا يرى رؤية إحاطة، كما أخبر عن نفسه أنه لا يعلم علم إحاطة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه: 110]، ونفي الإبصار الخاص لا يوجب نفي أصل الإبصار؛ وهو الذي ندعيه، وبهذا تعرف أن النصوص الدالة على الرؤية يجب تقييدها بنفي الإحاطة؛ للتوفيق بين النصوص.

سلمنا أن الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية، لكن لا نسلم العموم في الأزمان، بل المراد نفي الرؤية في الدنيا؛ للجمع بين هذا وبين ما اقتضى الرؤية في الآخرة، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفراد، وأن المؤمنين خارجون من هذا العموم للأدلة الواردة فيهم.

أو نقول: إن لفظ الأبصار جمع محلى بالألف واللام يفيد في الثبوت العموم، فسلبه يفيد سلب العموم؛ لأن النفي تابع لما أشعر به اللفظ المثبت، وذلك لا يفيد عموم السلب؛ لأن سلب العموم لا ينافي ثبوت الحكم لبعض الأفراد، فيتحقق بنفي الحكم عن فرد من الأفراد، بخلاف عموم السلب؛ فإنه يكذب بثبوت الحكم لفرد من الأفراد، ولهذا كذب الله تعالى اليهود حيث قالوا: ﴿ إِذْ قَالُوا مَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْءٍ ﴾ بقوله: ﴿ قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَابَ ﴾ [الأنعام: 91]، حيث ادعوا عموم السلب، والدلالة للمعتزلة بالآية تتوقف على تحقق الثاني دون الأول؛ فإن الأشعرية لا تدعي أنه يراه كل أحد، وإنما يراه المؤمنون دون الكافرين، ونقيض الموجبة الكلية التي سلبتها الآية هي السالبة الجزئية التي دلت عليها الآية، لا السالبة الكلية التي لم تدل عليها، فحينئذ نقول بموجبها؛ وهو أنه لا يراه جميع الأبصار، بل أبصار المؤمنين.

هكذا قرر الإمام الفخر هذا الجواب (۱)، وإليه أشرت بقولي: (أو هو من باب الكل لا الكلية) أي: السلب في الآية من باب السلب المعلق بالمجموع من حيث هو مجموع، لا من باب السلب المعلق بكل فرد فرد.

(۱) انظر (معالم أصول الدين) (ص 92).
That [interpretation] is impossible with respect to God, so it must be carried to its metaphorical meaning; which is that He is not seen with an encompassing vision, just as He informed about Himself that He is not known with encompassing knowledge in His statement: {And they do not encompass Him with knowledge} [Ta-Ha: 110], and the negation of specific sight does not necessitate the negation of the essence of sight; and that is what we claim. And by this you know that the texts indicating vision must be restricted by the negation of encompassment; for reconciliation between the texts.

We concede that perception means vision, but we do not concede generality across times; rather the intended meaning is the negation of vision in this world; to reconcile between this and what necessitates vision in the hereafter. Or we claim specification among individuals, and that the believers are excluded from this generality due to the proofs that have come concerning them.

Or we say: The word "absar" (sights) is a plural with the definite article (alif and lam) which in affirmative [constructions] indicates generality. So its negation indicates the negation of generality; because negation follows what the affirmative word suggests, and that does not indicate the generality of negation; because the negation of generality does not negate the establishment of the ruling for some individuals, so it is realized by negating the ruling from one individual. Unlike the generality of negation; for it is falsified by the establishment of the ruling for one individual. For this reason Allah falsified the Jews when they said {When they said: 'Allah has not sent down anything to any human being'} by His saying: {Say: 'Who sent down the Book?'} [al-An'am: 91], when they claimed the generality of negation. The argument for the Mu'tazila from the verse depends on the realization of the second [generality of negation] without the first [negation of generality]; for the Ash'aris do not claim that everyone sees Him, but rather the believers see Him, not the disbelievers. The contradictory of the universal affirmative which the verse negated is the particular negative which the verse indicated, not the universal negative which it did not indicate. Then we speak according to its requirement; which is that He is not seen by all sights, but rather by the sights of the believers.

Thus Imam al-Razi formulated this answer (1), and to it I pointed with my saying: (Or it is from the category of the whole, not the universal) i.e. the negation in the verse is of the category of negation attached to the aggregate inasmuch as it is an aggregate, not of the category of negation attached to every individual individual.

(1) See (Ma'alim Usul al-Din) (p. 92).
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Ash'ari position that the verse "لا تدركه الأبصار" (Sight does not perceive Him) does not negate the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife. The author presents multiple lines of reasoning.

First, he argues that the verse must be interpreted metaphorically (مجاز) because a literal reading that God cannot be seen with any sight is impossible (محال) with respect to God. He interprets the negation as applying to "encompassing vision" (رؤية إحاطة), similar to how God is not known with encompassing knowledge (علم إحاطة). Thus, negating a specific type of sight does not negate the essence of sight itself. This allows for the texts indicating vision of God in the afterlife to be reconciled by restricting them with the negation of encompassment.

Second, the author concessively assumes (سلمنا) that "perception" (الإدراك) means "vision" (الرؤية), but he offers two alternative responses: (a) The verse applies only to this world (الدنيا), not the afterlife; (b) The verse is specified to exclude believers, based on other proofs.

Third, the author presents a more technical logical argument: The word "الأبصار" (sights) is a definite plural, which in an affirmative statement indicates generality (العموم). When negated, it yields "negation of generality" (سلب العموم), not "generality of negation" (عموم السلب). The difference is crucial: negation of generality is true if even one individual is excluded (e.g., "not all sights see Him"), whereas generality of negation would require that no individual sees Him. The author gives the example of the Jews who falsely claimed that Allah had not sent down anything to any human (عموم السلب), and Allah refuted them by pointing to scripture sent down to prophets. For the Mu'tazila to prove their case, they would need to show that the verse implies generality of negation, not just negation of generality. The Ash'aris only claim that believers see God, not everyone, so the verse's negation of generality is sufficient and does not conflict with the ru'ya of believers.

Finally, the author cites Imam al-Razi's formulation of this answer and his own summary: the negation in the verse is like negating the aggregate (الكل) as a whole, not every individual (الكلي) individually.

عموم السلب
الموجبة الكلية
السالبة الجزئية
الكل
الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'لا تدركه الأبصار' means no one can ever see God.
Correction: Sanusi shows it means 'not all sights perceive Him', i.e., only believers see Him in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic example of the Jews illustrate?
A) The distinction between literal and metaphorical interpretation
B) The difference between negation of generality and generality of negation
C) The importance of consensus in theology
D) The invalidity of analogical reasoning

Key Takeaway
The verse's logical form is a negation of generality, not a universal negation, allowing for believers' vision of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار', does your mind automatically leap to the conclusion that no one can ever see God, even in Paradise? Where else in your reading of scripture do you perhaps jump to a universal negative without checking the logical form? Will you pause before the next blanket statement you make about God or about others?
?
The Jews made a universal claim that Allah sent down nothing to any human, and Allah refuted it with a single counterexample. In your own life, when you say 'no one ever understands me' or 'nothing ever goes right', are you making similar universal negatives that collapse under the weight of a single exception? Will you commit today to stop using such all-or-nothing language, except when the text itself demands it?
?
Sanusi shows that a subtle logical mistake can lead to a major theological error. Are you willing to examine your own deeply held beliefs for such hidden logical missteps? What one belief are you most afraid to scrutinize with this kind of precision, and why?

Ibn al-Tilamsani's Objection: Does the Verse Imply General Negation of Sight?
Ibn al-Tilamsani challenges the Ash'ari argument using logical and pragmatic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Tilamsani's objection to al-Razi's argument and why the context of praise (قرينة التمدح) is crucial for interpreting the verse.

Core Idea
Praise requires complete negation, not partial.

In this passage, we encounter a critical objection from Ibn al-Tilamsani against Imam al-Razi's interpretation of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'. Al-Razi had argued that the verse only negates sight for some eyes, but Ibn al-Tilamsani pushes back using logical analysis and the pragmatics of praise. This lesson will walk through his objection and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the technical terms: عموم السلب and سلب العموم.
2
Identify the objection
Ibn al-Tilamsani refuses to concede that the verse does not indicate general negation. Find the exact phrase where he states this.
He says: 'لا تسلم أن هذه الآية لا تفيد عموم السلب'.
3
Understand the logical distinction
Al-Razi used the distinction between 'salb al-umum' and 'umum al-salb'. Ibn al-Tilamsani argues that these are not mutually exclusive. Look for how he uses the logical rule about universal affirmative and particular negative.
The key phrase: 'نقيض الكلية الموجبة الجزئية السالبة'.
4
Focus on the context of praise
Ibn al-Tilamsani invokes قرينة التمدح (context of praise) as evidence that general negation is intended. Reflect on why praise requires complete negation.
The example: saying 'No sight perceives Him' vs 'Some sights do not perceive Him'.
5
Synthesize the argument
Summarize Ibn al-Tilamsani's objection in your own words and note how it challenges the Ash'ari position.
Consider the footnote reference to Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din for further detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض ابن التلمساني على الإمام الفخر:
وهذا الجواب الأخير أضعف الأجوبة، ولهذا أخرته، وقد اعترضه ابن التلمساني بأن قال: (لا تسلم أن هذه الآية لا تفيد عموم السلب، ولا نسلم أنها إذا دلت على نفي العموم لا تدل على عموم السلب؛ فإنه لا ينافيه). وقوله: (إن نقيض الكلية الموجبة الجزئية السالبة) قلنا: مسلم أنه يكفي ذلك في تكذيبها؛ لأنه المحقق، لكن إذا كذبت بالسالبة الجزئية كان تكذيبها بالسالبة الكلية بطريق الأولى، والذي يدل على أن المراد به عموم السلب .. قرينة التمدح بذلك؛ فإنك إذا أردت الوصف بالاحتجاب عن الأبصار، التمدح بقولك: لا يدركه بصر ما ألبتة، لا بقولك: بعض الأبصار لا يدركه، فالاعتماد على الجواب الثاني ((۲)؛ يعني: للإمام.

(۲) انظر شرح معالم أصول الدين (ص 403).
Objection of Ibn al-Tilamsani to Imam al-Razi:
And this last answer is the weakest of the answers, and for this I delayed it. Ibn al-Tilamsani objected to him by saying: 'It is not conceded that this verse does not indicate the generality of negation, and it is not conceded that when it indicates the negation of generality, it does not indicate the generality of negation; for that is not incompatible with it.' And his saying: 'Indeed the contradictory of the universal affirmative is the particular negative;' we say: It is conceded that that suffices for its falsification; because it is what is actualized, but if it is falsified by the particular negative, then its falsification by the universal negative is even more primary. And what indicates that the intended meaning is the generality of negation is the context of praise (التمدح) by that; because if you intend to describe [Him] as being hidden from sights, praise is [expressed] by your saying: 'No sight whatsoever perceives Him at all,' not by your saying: 'Some sights do not perceive Him.' So reliance is on the second answer (2); meaning: [that] of the Imam.

(2) See Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din (p. 403).
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Ibn al-Tilamsani against Imam al-Razi's interpretation of the verse. The author (the commentator) also notes that he considers this answer the weakest, which is why he placed it last.

Ibn al-Tilamsani's objection: He refuses to concede that the verse does not imply general negation (عموم السلب). He argues that even if the verse indicates only negation of generality (سلب العموم), it does not preclude that it also indicates generality of negation – meaning that the two are not mutually exclusive. Specifically, he argues that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, but if the particular negative falsifies the universal, then the universal negative would also be true in the same way, and in fact is even more strongly indicated. The key point is that the context of praise (قرينة التمدح) in the verse shows that the intended meaning is a complete negation of sight; because when you want to praise God for being veiled from sight, you would say 'No sight perceives Him at all,' not 'Some sights do not perceive Him.' Therefore, the verse does indeed imply general negation, and the Ash'ari argument fails. The final line states that the reliance should be on the second answer (of Imam al-Razi?), but the author of the main text (the commentator) seems to side with Ibn al-Tilamsani's objection? Actually the last line says 'فالاعتماد على الجواب الثاني' which refers back to the second answer of Imam al-Razi mentioned earlier (the 'أو نقول' answer). So the commentator may be concluding that the best response is the second answer (i.e., the logical one), despite the objection, or perhaps that the objection itself is resolved by the second answer? This is clarified in the footnote referencing 'Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din'.

The passage ends with the footnote directing to the commentary for further details.

Correction: Ibn al-Tilamsani demonstrates that the logical distinction does not prevent general negation, and the context of praise requires it.

B) The context of praise (قرينة التمدح) in the verse
C) The verse about Jewish denial of revelation
D) The consensus of the Salaf

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilamsani's objection shows that the context of praise in the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' overrides logical distinctions and demands general negation of sight.

Negated Plural and Its Logical Import
Tilmisani scholar explains the linguistic rule of negated plurals and defends the apparent meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'negation of universality' (نفي العموم) and 'universality of negation' (عموم السلب) and why the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' does not prove that God cannot be seen at all.

Core Idea
A negated plural does not deny every instance; it only denies that all instances share the predicate.

In response to Ibn Zikri’s claim that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not perceive Him) proves vision is impossible, a Tilmisani scholar begins by examining the linguistic form. He argues that the negated plural construction does not express universal negation (عموم السلب) but only negation of universality (نفي العموم), as demonstrated by everyday Arabic usage. This lesson unpacks that key distinction and its implications for the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the respondent's opening argument
Read the Arabic passage where the Tilmisani scholar rejects Ibn Zikri’s claim that the verse conveys full negation. Then read the translation to grasp his reasoning.
Focus on the example 'لا رجال في الدار' to internalize the logic.
2
Understand the two logical concepts
Identify the two terms: 'عموم السلب' (universality of negation) and 'نفي العموم' (negation of universality). Note how they are opposites: the former denies every single individual; the latter merely denies that all individuals share the property.
This is a foundational distinction in later kalam and usul al-fiqh.
3
Apply the distinction to the verse
Ask: If 'لا تدركه الأبصار' means negation of universality, what does it actually assert? It says 'It is not the case that every sight perceives Him,' which leaves open the possibility that some sights (e.g., believers in the afterlife) may perceive Him.
This directly undermines Ibn Zikri’s objection.
4
Reflect on the second point: apparent meaning vs. remote possibility
The respondent then addresses a counter‑argument: even if the verse only negates universality, it does not contradict universal negation. The respondent replies that we do not abandon the apparent meaning for a remote, unsupported possibility. Consider why this principle is important in interpretation.
This prepares for later discussions on contextual indications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنهُ بعضُ المعاصرينَ مِنَ التَّلمسانيين في شرح على « عقيدة ابن الحاجب » فقال : ( أما قوله : « لا نُسلّمُ أَنَّ هَذهِ الآية لا تفيد عموم السلبِ » .. فمنع لا يصح بشهادة علماء المعاني ؛ فإنَّهم نضُوا على أنَّ الجمعَ المنفي معرَّفاً أو منكراً لا يفيد عموم النفي ، وإنَّما يفيد نفي العموم ؛ بدليل صدق قولنا : لا رجال في الدار ، أو لم يقم الرجالُ ؛ إذا كانَ فيها أوِ القائم رجلٌ أو رجلانِ . وأما قوله : ( لا نُسلّمُ أنَّها إذا دلَّت على نفي العموم لا تدلُّ على عموم السلبِ ؛ فإنَّه لا ينافيه » . . فنقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيهِ ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ولو سُلَّمَ فلا يُترَكُ الظاهر للمحتمل المرجوح وأما قوله : « إذا كُذِّبَتْ بالسالبة . . . » إلى آخره .. فهذا مُسلَّم بعد تكذيب السالبة الكليَّةِ ، فتستلزم الجزئية ؛ لأنَّ الكليَّةَ أَخصُّ مِنَ الجزئية ، فإذا كَذَّبَ الأخصُ مِنَ النقيض كَذَّبَ النقيض ، وإن لم يقم دليل على تكذيب السالبة . فلا يصح أخذُها كليَّةً بعد أخذ الموجبة كليَّةً ، وإلا أدَّى إلى تناقض الكليتين ، وهو باطل وأما قوله : « الذي يدلُّ ... » إلى آخره .. فنقولُ : تلكَ القرينة حالية لا لفظيَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلولُ اللفظ لأجلها ، سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ، لكنْ لا نُسلّم عمومها في الأزمانِ ؛ لأنَّ صيغة العموم مطلقة فيها ، فتُقيَّدُ بالدنيا ، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفراد ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَبَّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة : ۲۲ - ۲۳] . أو نقولُ : هب أن جميع الأبصار لا تدركه ، لكن لم قلتُم : لا يدركه المبصرون ؟! أو ندعي التقييد في الإدراك بالإحاطة ؛ فإنَّا نراه على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة ، كما يُعلَمُ على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة أو نقولُ : هذهِ الآيةُ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدح كما ذكرتُم ، لكن لمَ قلتُم : إنَّها تدلُّ على نفي الرؤيةِ ونفي الرؤية مطلقاً لا تمدح فيها ، بلِ التمدُّحُ في اقتدارِهِ على منع الرؤيةِ مَنْ يشاءُ إذا يشاءُ ويخلقُها لَمَنْ يشاءُ ، وهو أليق بالمدح ) انتهى . [ الرد على ابن زكري ] قلتُ : ولا يخفى عليك فساد هذا الرد وما احتوى عليهِ مِنْ أنواع الاختلال :
And a contemporary from the Tilmisani scholars answered him in a commentary on the 'Aqida of Ibn al-Hajib, saying: (As for his statement: 'We do not concede that this verse does not convey universality of negation'... this prevention is not valid according to the testimony of scholars of semantics; for they have established that the negated plural, whether definite or indefinite, does not convey universality of negation, but rather conveys negation of universality; evidence: the truth of our saying: 'No men are in the house' or 'The men did not stand' when there is in it one man or two men. And as for his statement: 'We do not concede that if it indicates negation of universality it does not indicate universality of negation, for that does not contradict it'... we say: suppose it does not contradict it, then where is what necessitates it?! And even if conceded, the apparent meaning is not abandoned for a remote possibility. And as for his statement: 'If it is falsified by the negative universal...' etc. this is conceded after the falsification of the negative universal, then it entails the particular; because the universal is more specific than the particular, and if the more specific of the contradictory falsifies, the contradictory falsifies, even if no evidence exists for the falsification of the negative. So it is not valid to take it as universal after taking the affirmative as universal, otherwise it would lead to contradiction of the two universals, which is false. And as for his statement: 'What indicates...' etc. we say: that contextual indication is a circumstantial, not verbal, indication, so the denotation of the word is not abandoned for its sake. We concede the form indicates universality by this contextual indication, but we do not concede its universality across times; because the form of universality is absolute in it, so it is restricted to this world, or we claim specification of individuals due to His statement: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [75:22-23]. Or we say: suppose that all sights do not perceive Him, but why did you say: the seers do not see Him?! Or we claim restriction in perception to encompassing; for we see Him as He is without encompassing, just as He is known as He is without encompassing. Or we say: this verse came in the context of praise as you mentioned, but why did you say: it indicates negation of vision, and negation of vision absolutely does not contain praise; rather the praise is in His power to prevent vision from whomever He wills whenever He wills and to create it for whomever He wills, and that is more fitting for praise.) End. [The Refutation of Ibn Zikri] I say: The corruption of this response and what it contains of types of disorder is not hidden from you.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, a contemporary Tilmisani scholar responds point by point to Ibn Zikri's objections against the Ash'ari position that God can be seen. Ibn Zikri likely argued that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not perceive Him) proves vision is impossible. The respondent argues: (1) The negated plural 'الأبصار' does not imply universal negation (عموم السلب) but only negation of universality (نفي العموم), as when we say 'لا رجال في الدار' (No men are in the house) which can be true even if one man is present. (2) Even if it only negates universality, that does not contradict the possibility of particular vision; the apparent meaning is not abandoned for a remote possibility. (3) Regarding the logical point about contradictory propositions: if the negative universal is falsified, it entails the particular negative is true; but one cannot assume the negative universal without evidence. (4) The contextual indication (قرينة) is circumstantial, not verbal; it cannot override the literal meaning. Even if it indicates universality, it does not cover all times; it can be restricted to this world, or the individuals can be specified by the verse about the believers seeing their Lord in the afterlife. (5) Perception might be restricted to encompassing perception (إدراك بالإحاطة), while non-encompassing vision is possible, as with knowledge. (6) The verse's context of praise does not require absolute negation of vision; rather, the praise lies in God's power to grant or withhold vision as He wills. The second part is the author's sharp refutation: he declares the entire response corrupt and full of disorder, implying it is invalid.

Key Terms
سالبة كلية
سالبة جزئية
عموم السلب
نفي العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a negated plural like 'لا تدركه الأبصار' means absolutely no sight ever perceives God (عموم السلب).
Correction: Arabic linguistic rules show that such a construction only negates the universality of the predicate (نفي العموم), allowing for some sights to perceive Him.

Checkpoint
According to the Tilmisani respondent's argument, what does the negated plural 'لا تدركه الأبصار' actually imply?
A) That no sight at all can perceive God.
B) That it is not the case that every sight perceives God.
C) That God is invisible by essence.
D) That only some sights can perceive God.

Key Takeaway
The verse لا تدركه الأبصار only negates that all sights perceive God, not that any sight ever does; thus it does not disprove the possibility of seeing Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a surface-level understanding of a Quranic verse because it felt comfortable, only to later discover a deeper meaning that challenged your assumptions? Will you commit to re-examining your interpretations of divine attributes with humility, even when it unsettles you?
?
When you encounter verses like 'لا تدركه الأبصار', do you tend to assume you know exactly what they mean, or do you pause to consider the linguistic nuances that could change their import? What is one verse about God that you might have misunderstood due to haste?
?
The scholar argues that we should not abandon the apparent meaning without evidence. In your own life, what beliefs about God have you accepted on weak evidence or authority? Are you willing to test them against the primary sources with the same rigor shown here?

Logical Consequences of Falsifying the Universal
Respondent clarifies the logical entailment from falsifying a universal to affirming the particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between universal and particular propositions, and why the respondent argues that falsifying the universal negative entails the particular negative.

Core Idea
If the universal negative is false, the particular negative must be true.

Ibn Zikri had raised a logical objection: if someone claims that the verse falsifies the universal negative proposition 'No sight perceives God,' then that would imply the opposite universal affirmative, leading to contradiction. The Tilmisani respondent clarifies the logic: falsifying the universal negative (سالبة كلية) automatically entails the particular negative (سالبة جزئية) because the universal is more specific. This lesson explores that logical move and its defense against Ibn Zikri.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the logical point
Read the Arabic where the respondent discusses 'إذا كُذِّبَتْ بالسالبة ...' and the translation. Focus on the syllogistic reasoning.
Look for the relationship between 'السّالبة الكلية' and 'السّالبة الجزئية'.
2
Understand the logical principle
The respondent states: if the universal negative (e.g., 'No S is P') is falsified, then the particular negative (e.g., 'Some S is not P') is necessarily true. This is because the universal implies the particular, so if the universal is false, its opposite (the particular) is true.
This is basic Aristotelian logic: from 'All S is P' we get 'Some S is P'; similarly, from 'No S is P' we get 'Some S is not P' if falsified.
3
Apply to the verse
Ibn Zikri likely argued that taking the verse as a universal negative leads to a contradiction if one also accepts the universal affirmative (that believers see God). The respondent replies that one cannot assume the universal negative without evidence; if it is falsified, the particular negative follows, and there is no contradiction.
The respondent's point is that the verse gives only a particular negative, not a universal one, so no contradiction arises.
4
Reflect on the warning against assuming universals
The passage ends with a caution: 'It is not valid to take it as universal after taking the affirmative as universal, else it would lead to contradiction of the two universals, which is false.' Understand why assuming both universals leads to inconsistency.
This ties back to the earlier linguistic argument: the verse cannot give a universal negative, so the problem does not arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أجاب عنهُ بعضُ المعاصرينَ مِنَ التَّلمسانيين في شرح على « عقيدة ابن الحاجب » فقال : ( أما قوله : « لا نُسلّمُ أَنَّ هَذهِ الآية لا تفيد عموم السلبِ » .. فمنع لا يصح بشهادة علماء المعاني ؛ فإنَّهم نضُوا على أنَّ الجمعَ المنفي معرَّفاً أو منكراً لا يفيد عموم النفي ، وإنَّما يفيد نفي العموم ؛ بدليل صدق قولنا : لا رجال في الدار ، أو لم يقم الرجالُ ؛ إذا كانَ فيها أوِ القائم رجلٌ أو رجلانِ . وأما قوله : ( لا نُسلّمُ أنَّها إذا دلَّت على نفي العموم لا تدلُّ على عموم السلبِ ؛ فإنَّه لا ينافيه » . . فنقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيهِ ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ولو سُلَّمَ فلا يُترَكُ الظاهر للمحتمل المرجوح وأما قوله : « إذا كُذِّبَتْ بالسالبة . . . » إلى آخره .. فهذا مُسلَّم بعد تكذيب السالبة الكليَّةِ ، فتستلزم الجزئية ؛ لأنَّ الكليَّةَ أَخصُّ مِنَ الجزئية ، فإذا كَذَّبَ الأخصُ مِنَ النقيض كَذَّبَ النقيض ، وإن لم يقم دليل على تكذيب السالبة . فلا يصح أخذُها كليَّةً بعد أخذ الموجبة كليَّةً ، وإلا أدَّى إلى تناقض الكليتين ، وهو باطل وأما قوله : « الذي يدلُّ ... » إلى آخره .. فنقولُ : تلكَ القرينة حالية لا لفظيَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلولُ اللفظ لأجلها ، سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ، لكنْ لا نُسلّم عمومها في الأزمانِ ؛ لأنَّ صيغة العموم مطلقة فيها ، فتُقيَّدُ بالدنيا ، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفراد ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَبَّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة : ۲۲ - ۲۳] . أو نقولُ : هب أن جميع الأبصار لا تدركه ، لكن لم قلتُم : لا يدركه المبصرون ؟! أو ندعي التقييد في الإدراك بالإحاطة ؛ فإنَّا نراه على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة ، كما يُعلَمُ على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة أو نقولُ : هذهِ الآيةُ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدح كما ذكرتُم ، لكن لمَ قلتُم : إنَّها تدلُّ على نفي الرؤيةِ ونفي الرؤية مطلقاً لا تمدح فيها ، بلِ التمدُّحُ في اقتدارِهِ على منع الرؤيةِ مَنْ يشاءُ إذا يشاءُ ويخلقُها لَمَنْ يشاءُ ، وهو أليق بالمدح ) انتهى . [ الرد على ابن زكري ] قلتُ : ولا يخفى عليك فساد هذا الرد وما احتوى عليهِ مِنْ أنواع الاختلال :
And a contemporary from the Tilmisani scholars answered him in a commentary on the 'Aqida of Ibn al-Hajib, saying: (As for his statement: 'We do not concede that this verse does not convey universality of negation'... this prevention is not valid according to the testimony of scholars of semantics; for they have established that the negated plural, whether definite or indefinite, does not convey universality of negation, but rather conveys negation of universality; evidence: the truth of our saying: 'No men are in the house' or 'The men did not stand' when there is in it one man or two men. And as for his statement: 'We do not concede that if it indicates negation of universality it does not indicate universality of negation, for that does not contradict it'... we say: suppose it does not contradict it, then where is what necessitates it?! And even if conceded, the apparent meaning is not abandoned for a remote possibility. And as for his statement: 'If it is falsified by the negative universal...' etc. this is conceded after the falsification of the negative universal, then it entails the particular; because the universal is more specific than the particular, and if the more specific of the contradictory falsifies, the contradictory falsifies, even if no evidence exists for the falsification of the negative. So it is not valid to take it as universal after taking the affirmative as universal, otherwise it would lead to contradiction of the two universals, which is false. And as for his statement: 'What indicates...' etc. we say: that contextual indication is a circumstantial, not verbal, indication, so the denotation of the word is not abandoned for its sake. We concede the form indicates universality by this contextual indication, but we do not concede its universality across times; because the form of universality is absolute in it, so it is restricted to this world, or we claim specification of individuals due to His statement: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [75:22-23]. Or we say: suppose that all sights do not perceive Him, but why did you say: the seers do not see Him?! Or we claim restriction in perception to encompassing; for we see Him as He is without encompassing, just as He is known as He is without encompassing. Or we say: this verse came in the context of praise as you mentioned, but why did you say: it indicates negation of vision, and negation of vision absolutely does not contain praise; rather the praise is in His power to prevent vision from whomever He wills whenever He wills and to create it for whomever He wills, and that is more fitting for praise.) End. [The Refutation of Ibn Zikri] I say: The corruption of this response and what it contains of types of disorder is not hidden from you.

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, a contemporary Tilmisani scholar responds point by point to Ibn Zikri's objections against the Ash'ari position that God can be seen. Ibn Zikri likely argued that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not perceive Him) proves vision is impossible. The respondent argues: (1) The negated plural 'الأبصار' does not imply universal negation (عموم السلب) but only negation of universality (نفي العموم), as when we say 'لا رجال في الدار' (No men are in the house) which can be true even if one man is present. (2) Even if it only negates universality, that does not contradict the possibility of particular vision; the apparent meaning is not abandoned for a remote possibility. (3) Regarding the logical point about contradictory propositions: if the negative universal is falsified, it entails the particular negative is true; but one cannot assume the negative universal without evidence. (4) The contextual indication (قرينة) is circumstantial, not verbal; it cannot override the literal meaning. Even if it indicates universality, it does not cover all times; it can be restricted to this world, or the individuals can be specified by the verse about the believers seeing their Lord in the afterlife. (5) Perception might be restricted to encompassing perception (إدراك بالإحاطة), while non-encompassing vision is possible, as with knowledge. (6) The verse's context of praise does not require absolute negation of vision; rather, the praise lies in God's power to grant or withhold vision as He wills. The second part is the author's sharp refutation: he declares the entire response corrupt and full of disorder, implying it is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Falsifying a universal negative only yields a particular negative, not a universal affirmative, so the logical objection based on contradiction is unfounded.

?

Contextual Indications and Multiple Defenses
Respondent offers multiple interpretations of the verse; author rejects them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the respondent's alternative interpretations of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' that preserve the possibility of seeing God, including restriction to this world, specification to non-believers, encompassing perception, and the context of praise.

Core Idea
Even if the verse implied universal negation, it can be restricted by context, time, individuals, or mode of perception.

The Tilmisani respondent offers a series of alternative explanations for the verse, assuming for the sake of argument that it does indicate universality. He lists several possibilities: the verse may be restricted to this world, specified to the disbelievers, or refer to encompassing perception (إدراك بالإحاطة). He also addresses the context of praise, arguing that praising God for preventing vision is more fitting than praising Him for being invisible. The lesson concludes with the author's sharp rejection of the entire response.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the respondent's reply
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما قوله: الذي يدلّ ...' through the end of his quote. Then read the translation. Note the multiple 'أو نقول' (or we say) constructions.
Each 'أو' introduces an alternative defense; they are not necessarily all true but show the flexibility of interpretation.
2
Examine the first two defenses: restriction to time and specification to individuals
First, the verse's generality can be restricted to this world (الدنيا) based on other verses. Second, it can be specified to certain individuals because Allah will make believers see Him in the afterlife (referencing Qur'an 75:22-23).
These are standard usul al-fiqh techniques of takhsis and taqyid.
3
Understand the defense about encompassing perception
The respondent suggests that the verse may deny encompassing perception (إدراك بالإحاطة), not vision per se. He analogizes to knowledge: we know God without encompassing Him fully.
This distinction is important in Ash'ari theology: vision of God is possible but not comprehensive.
4
Analyze the argument from praise
Finally, even if the verse is in a context of praise, praising God for His power to grant or withhold vision is more fitting than praising Him for being absolutely invisible.
This reframes the verse from a proof of impossibility to a proof of divine power.
5
Read the author's refutation
The author (Sanusi) dismisses the entire response as corrupt and disordered. Reflect on why he might reject all these alternatives.
This reminds us that the debate is not settled by this response; the author likely has deeper objections.

If you were faced with a person who uses this verse to deny the vision of God, would you be able to respond with the same depth as the respondent? What does this teach you about the importance of studying theology deeply?

The Logical Principle and the Nature of Contextual Clues
Sanusi states a logical principle and rejects a contextual clue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that prevents taking a negative universal as absolute when evidence supports the affirmative, and how a situational contextual clue does not override the literal meaning of a verse.

Core Idea
A negative universal cannot stand against affirmative evidence; a situational clue does not negate the literal meaning.

Sanusi opens with a logical rule: if evidence exists against a negative universal claim, it cannot be maintained once the affirmative universal is established. He then addresses the opponent's reliance on a contextual clue, arguing that the clue is situational, not verbal, and thus does not override the literal meaning of the verse. This sets the stage for the subsequent alternative interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical principle stated at the beginning.
The passage uses technical terms like 'saliba' (negative universal) and 'mūjiba' (affirmative universal).
2
Identify the logical principle
The author states: if a negative universal is contradicted by evidence, it cannot be taken as universal after the affirmative is taken as universal. Why would this lead to contradiction?
The principle prevents two contradictory universals from coexisting.
3
Analyze the qarīna argument
The opponent used a contextual clue (qarīna) to claim generality. Sanusi distinguishes between situational (ḥāliyya) and verbal (lafẓiyya) clues. A situational clue does not override the literal meaning.
This rejection is key to preserving the literal meaning of the verse about seeing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقم دليل على تكذيب السالبة . فلا يصح أخذُها كليَّةً بعد أخذ الموجبة كليَّةً ، وإلا أدَّى إلى تناقض الكليتين ، وهو باطل وأما قوله : « الذي يدلُّ ... » إلى آخره .. فنقولُ : تلكَ القرينة حالية لا لفظيَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلولُ اللفظ لأجلها ، سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ، لكنْ لا نُسلّم عمومها في الأزمانِ ؛ لأنَّ صيغة العموم مطلقة فيها ، فتُقيَّدُ بالدنيا ، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفراد ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَبَّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة : ۲۲ - ۲۳] . أو نقولُ : هب أن جميع الأبصار لا تدركه ، لكن لم قلتُم : لا يدركه المبصرون ؟! أو ندعي التقييد في الإدراك بالإحاطة ؛ فإنَّا نراه على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة ، كما يُعلَمُ على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ غير إحاطة أو نقولُ : هذهِ الآيةُ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدح كما ذكرتُم ، لكن لمَ قلتُم : إنَّها تدلُّ على نفي الرؤيةِ ونفي الرؤية مطلقاً لا تمدح فيها ، بلِ التمدُّحُ في اقتدارِهِ على منع الرؤيةِ مَنْ يشاءُ إذا يشاءُ ويخلقُها لَمَنْ يشاءُ ، وهو أليق بالمدح ) انتهى
If evidence exists to belie the negative universal, then taking it as universal after taking the affirmative as universal is not valid, otherwise it would lead to contradiction of the two universals, which is false. As for his statement: 'that which indicates ...' to the end, we say: that contextual clue (qarīna) is a situational one, not verbal, so the signification of the utterance is not abandoned because of it. Granted that the form indicates generality by this contextual clue, but we do not concede its generality over times, because the form of generality is unrestricted with respect to them, so it is restricted to this world. Or we claim specification (takhṣīṣ) with respect to individuals, by His statement: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (Qiyāma 75:22-23). Or we say: suppose that all sights do not perceive Him, but why did you say that 'those who see' do not perceive Him? Or we claim restriction in perception (idrāk) to encompassing, for we see Him as He is without encompassing, just as He is known as He is without encompassing. Or we say: this verse came in the context of praise as you mentioned, but why did you say that it indicates negation of vision (ru'ya) — and negation of vision absolutely does not contain praise in it? Rather, the praise is in His power to prevent vision from whomever He wills whenever He wills, and to create it for whomever He wills, and that is more fitting for praise. End.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and responses defending the Sunni position that believers will see God in the afterlife, contra those who interpret verses like 'Sights do not perceive Him' (6:103) as an absolute negation. The author begins by stating a logical principle: if a negative universal is contradicted by evidence, it cannot be taken as universal after the affirmative universal is taken as universal, as that would lead to contradiction. The opponent's argument relied on a contextual clue (qarīna) that supposedly indicates generality; the author refutes that the clue is situational, not verbal, so it does not override the literal meaning. Even if the form indicated generality, it is restricted to this world (i.e., in the present life). Then he offers alternative specifications: the verse about faces looking at their Lord (75:22-23) shows that the righteous will see God, so the negation in 'sights do not perceive Him' is specified to disbelievers or to encompassing perception. He further argues that even if all sights do not perceive God, it does not mean that the seeing persons (the Prophet, believers) do not perceive Him; and that 'perception' (idrāk) means encompassing, which is denied, but vision without encompassing is affirmed. Finally, he reinterprets the verse in the context of praise: the negation of absolute vision is not praiseworthy, but rather the power to grant or withhold vision as God wills. Thus the verse actually implies that God can give vision to whom He wills.

Key Terms
عموم
نفي
القرينة
حالية
لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any contextual clue (qarīna) automatically overrides the literal meaning of a verse.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes between situational and verbal clues; only verbal clues can override the literal meaning, not situational ones.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what type of clue is the qarīna in the opponent's argument?
A) A verbal clue (lafẓiyya)
B) A situational clue (ḥāliyya)
C) A logical clue (ʿaqliyya)
D) An ambiguous clue (mujmala)

Key Takeaway
A negative universal cannot be absolute when evidence supports the affirmative, and a situational contextual clue does not nullify the literal meaning of a verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems to negate something about Allah, do you immediately take it as universally and absolutely true, or do you consider other evidences that affirm His attributes? How has this lesson's principle changed your approach to understanding such texts?
?
Have you ever abandoned the literal meaning of a verse because of a contextual clue without examining whether that clue is situational or verbal? What is at stake in that decision, and how will you handle it differently now?
?
In your own life, do you tend to let situational circumstances override clear statements from revelation? Think of one specific area where you have done this—what would it look like to submit to the literal meaning even when the situation seems to suggest otherwise?

Alternative Interpretations Preserving Vision of God
Sanusi presents four alternative interpretations to defend the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple alternative interpretations that allow for seeing God in the afterlife despite the apparent negation in the verse, including temporal restriction, specification to individuals, distinction of perception from vision, and the praise context.

Core Idea
The verse's negation can be restricted to this world, to disbelievers, or to encompassing perception, and it actually praises God's power over vision.

After rejecting the opponent's initial argument, Sanusi offers several alternative ways to interpret the verse 'Sights do not perceive Him'. He suggests it may only apply to this world, or to disbelievers, or that 'perception' means encompassing, not simple vision. He also reinterprets the verse's context of praise as actually affirming God's power to grant vision. Each alternative preserves the Sunni belief that believers will see God in Paradise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and note the sequence of alternatives introduced by 'أو' (or). There are four distinct arguments.
The passage uses repetition of 'أو نقول' to signal new points.
2
List the four alternatives
Identify each alternative: temporal restriction to this world, specification to individuals using the verse about faces looking at their Lord, the distinction between 'sights' and 'those who see', and the restriction of 'perception' to encompassing.
The last point about praise is also an alternative.
3
Understand each alternative's rationale
For each, explain how it allows for vision of God in the afterlife. For example, temporal restriction limits the negation to the present life; specification exempts believers; perception as encompassing means simple vision is still possible.
The praise argument turns the verse into evidence for God's power to grant vision.

The Mu'tazila Argument from 'Lan Tarani' and Its Refutation
The Mu'tazila's proof from 'lan tarani' and a brief refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's use of the verse 'lan tarani' to deny the vision of Allah, and how the Ash'ari response turns their own proof on its head.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's proof backfires: 'lan tarani' actually proves Allah can be seen.

The Mu'tazila famously argued that the Qur'anic verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) proves that Allah cannot be seen, even in the afterlife. They claimed the word 'lan' indicates eternal negation. In this passage, the author presents their proof and begins to show how the same verse actually supports the Ash'ari position that Allah is possible of vision (jā'iz al-ru'ya).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the author's contextual remark about shifting answers in debate.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's reasoning
Find the three premises: (1) 'lan' indicates perpetuity, (2) evidence from another verse, (3) metaphor is not default. Conclude: Moses will never see Allah, so no one will.
3
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
The author states that this verse actually indicates that Allah is jā'iz al-ru'ya (possible of vision). Though cut off, see how the Mu'tazila's own evidence is turned around.
The full refutation continues after the passage ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم لو كان الكلام مع المعتزلي المستدل بالآية على مذهبه الفاسد لحسن أن ينتقل معه من جواب يعترضه إلى جواب آخر يسلم من الاعتراض لأن مقصوده هو بالاعتراض تصحيح مذهبه والدفع عنه لا خصوصية ذلك الجواب الذي يعترضه. الكلام على قوله تعالى: {لَن تراني}. ومما تمسكت به المعتزلة: قوله تعالى: {لَن تراني} [الأعراف: 143]، و (لن) تفيد التأبيد؛ بدليل قوله تعالى: {قُل لَّن تَتَّبِعُونَا} [الفتح: 15]، والمراد هنا: التأبيد، والمجاز والنقل على خلاف الأصل، فوجب أن يقال: إن موسى عليه السلام لن يرى الله البتة، وكل من قال: إن موسى لن يرى الله البتة... قال: إن غيره لا يراه. والجواب: أن هذا يدل على كونه تعالى جائز الرؤية؛ لأنه لو
Yes, if the speech were with the Mu‘tazilī who uses the verse as evidence for his corrupt doctrine, then it would be good to move with him from an answer he objects to another answer free from objection, because his purpose in objecting is to correct his doctrine and defend it, not the specificity of that answer he objects to. The discussion on His statement, exalted is He: {You will not see Me}. And among what the Mu‘tazila clung to is His statement, exalted is He: {You will not see Me} [al-A‘rāf: 143], and 'lan' denotes perpetuity; evidenced by His statement, exalted is He: {Say: You will not follow us} [al-Fatḥ: 15], and the intended meaning here is perpetuity, and metaphor and transposition are contrary to the original principle, so it must be said that Moses, peace be upon him, will never see Allah at all, and whoever says that Moses will never see Allah at all... he says that others do not see Him. The answer: that this indicates that He, exalted is He, is possible of vision (jā'iz al-ru'ya); because if
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage begins with a transitional remark: the author notes that if one were arguing against a Mu‘tazilī who actually uses the verse to support his doctrine, it would be appropriate to shift to alternative answers if one is objected to. Then the author presents the Mu‘tazila's proof from the verse 'lan tarānī' (You will not see Me) from Sūrat al-A‘rāf. The Mu‘tazila argue that 'lan' indicates eternal negation (ta’bīd), citing another verse 'lan yattabi‘ūnī' (they will not follow me) where it means forever. They claim that metaphor (majāz) and transposition (naql) are contrary to the original principle, so the literal meaning must be taken: Moses will never see Allah, and by extension no one else will see Him. The author then gives a brief answer: this verse actually proves that Allah is possible of vision (jā’iz al-ru’ya). The argument is cut off, but it likely continues that the use of 'lan' does not necessarily negate possibility forever in this context, or that the negation is specific to Moses in this world. The key point is that the Mu‘tazila's own proof backfires.

تأبيد
جائز الرؤية
مجاز
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'lan' always indicates absolute eternal negation without any possibility of exception.
Correction: While 'lan' often indicates perpetuity, it can be context-dependent. In the verse 'lan tarani', the negation is specific to Moses in this world and does not preclude vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's argument in this passage, what does the word 'لن' indicate?
A) A weak negation that may be broken.
B) Perpetuity (تأبيد), i.e., eternal negation.
C) A possibility of future change.
D) A metaphorical meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's argument from 'lan tarani' is refuted by showing the verse itself indicates the possibility of seeing Allah, turning their own proof against them.

The Rational Proof for Seeing God
Sanusi presents and critiques the rational proof for ru'yat Allah using existence as the enabling factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and critique of the rational proof for the permissibility of seeing God based on existence.

Core Idea
Existence is proposed as the enabling factor for vision, but the proof requires careful logical analysis.

Sanusi presents the famous rational proof for ru'yat Allah, showing both its formulation and its weakness. He then provides a refined version by Ibn al-Tilmisani that navigates the logical pitfalls. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the initial criticism before the refined proof.
2
Identify the claim and objection
Find the statement of the famous proof and Sanusi's initial objection. Why does he say it is weak?
3
Trace the logical deduction
Follow the elimination process in the major premise. Understand why the enabling factor must be existence itself.
This is the core of the argument.
