⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الدليل العقلي لجواز رؤيته تعالى . وأمَّا إثباتُها بالدليل العقلي المشهورِ؛ وهو أَنَّ مُصحح الرؤية الوجود . . فضعيف؛ لأنَّ الوجودَ عين الموجودِ ، فلا يصلح علة . تقرير الاستدلال بالوجودِ على ما حرَّرَهُ ابنُ التِّلِمْسانِي أَنْ يُقالَ : البارى تعالى موجود ، وكلُّ موجود يصح أن يُرى ؛ ينتج : فالبارى يصح أن يُرى . ودليل الصغرى ظاهر ، وأمَّا الكبرى - وهي : أَنَّ كلَّ موجود يصحُ أَنْ يُرى - فلأن صحة الرؤية موقوفة على مُصحح ، وإلا لصح تعلّقها بالمعدوم كالعلم ، والرؤية تتعلَّق بالمختلفاتِ ، بدليل تعلقها بالجوهر والعرض ، وهما مختلفان؛ فالمصحّحُ لرؤيتهما إذاً لا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يكون ما به الافتراقُ ، أو ما به الاشتراك . لا جائز أن يكونَ ما به الافتراقُ ، وإلا لزم تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بالنوع بالعلل المختلفة ، وأنَّهُ محال ، تعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ المصحح أمراً وقع فيه الاشتراك؛ وذلك المشتركُ لا يخلو : أن يكون أمراً ثبوتياً ، أو عدميّاً . لا جائزَ أن يكون أمراً عدميّاً ، وإلا لصح رؤية المعدوم ، وامتنع رؤية الموجود ، ولأن العدمَ لا يصلحُ أن يكون علة للأمر الثبوتي ، تعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ أمراً ثبوتيّاً ، والأمرُ الثبوتي لا يخلو : إمَّا أن يتقيَّد بالوجودِ أو لا؛ فإن لم يُقيَّد بالوجودِ امتنع رؤية الموجودِ ، وإنْ تقيَّد بالوجودِ فلا يخلو : أنْ يتقيَّد بكونه صفةً أو موصوفاً . لا جائزَ أنْ يتقيَّد بأحدهما ، وإلا لما رئي الآخرُ ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّهُ إِنَّما صح رؤيته لكونه موجوداً ، والبارى تعالى موجود ، فصح أن يُرى .
Discussion on the rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Him, Exalted. As for establishing it by the famous rational proof, which is that the enabling factor (musahhih) of vision is existence... it is weak because existence is identical to the existent, so it is not suitable as a cause. The formulation of the proof by existence as refined by Ibn al-Tilmisani is that it is said: The Creator, Exalted, exists, and every existent is valid to be seen; conclusion: the Creator is valid to be seen. The proof of the minor premise is clear. As for the major premise – which is that every existent is valid to be seen – it is because the validity of vision depends on an enabling factor, otherwise it would be valid to attach it to the non-existent like knowledge, but vision attaches to different things, as evidenced by its attachment to substance and accident, which are different. So the enabling factor for seeing them is necessarily either that by which they differ or that by which they share. It is not possible that it is that by which they differ, otherwise it would necessitate explaining judgments equal in type by different causes, which is impossible. So it is determined that the enabling factor is something in which they share. And that shared thing is necessarily either a positive matter (amr thubuti) or a negative one. It is not possible that it is a negative matter, otherwise the non-existent would be valid to be seen and the existent would be impossible to be seen, and because non-existence is not suitable as a cause for a positive matter. So it is determined that it is a positive matter. And the positive matter is necessarily either qualified by existence or not. If it is not qualified by existence, then seeing the existent is impossible. If it is qualified by existence, then it is necessarily either qualified as an attribute or as something described. It is not possible that it is qualified by one of them, otherwise the other would not be seen. So it is determined that it is valid to be seen only because it is existent, and the Creator, Exalted, is existent, so it is valid to be seen.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage presents and analyses the famous rational proof for the permissibility of seeing God (ru'yat Allah). The author begins by stating the proof's weakness: it claims existence is the enabling factor (musahhih) for vision, but existence is identical to the existent and thus cannot serve as a cause. He then provides the precise formulation by Ibn al-Tilmisani: God exists; every existent can be seen; therefore God can be seen. The minor premise is obvious. The defence of the major premise involves a detailed analysis: vision requires an enabling factor; otherwise it would attach to non-existents like knowledge does. Since vision applies to different kinds of things (substances and accidents), the enabling factor must be something common to them. It cannot be what differentiates them, as that would require different causes for the same type of judgment. The common factor is either a positive attribute (thubuti) or a privative one (adami). It cannot be privative, because then non-existents would be visible and existents not, and privation cannot cause a positive state. Thus it must be positive. The positive factor must be qualified by existence, otherwise the existent would not be seen. It cannot be restricted to either attribute or subject alone, as then the other would not be seen. So only unrestricted existence remains as the enabling factor. Therefore, every existent can be seen, and since God exists, He can be seen.

Key Terms
musahhih
thubuti
adami
ru'yat Allah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it uses existence to prove visibility.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it argues that existence is the enabling factor after eliminating other possibilities.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can every existent be seen?
A) Because existence is the enabling factor for vision.
B) Because every existent has a color.
C) Because vision is like knowledge in attaching to anything.
D) Because the enabling factor must be what differentiates substances from accidents.

Key Takeaway
The rational proof for ru'yat Allah hinges on establishing existence as the common enabling factor for vision after a detailed process of elimination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson critiques a rational proof for seeing God. How does your heart respond to the idea that even logical arguments can be weak? Are you more inclined to trust reason or revelation when they seem to conflict?
?
If the existence of God alone does not provide a strong proof for seeing Him, what does that mean for your desire to see God? Does it increase your longing or create doubt?
?
The author engages deeply with a proof he ultimately considers weak. Are you willing to study and understand arguments you may not fully accept? Is your faith strong enough to examine even its own rational foundations?

Al-Razi's Objection from Createdness
Al-Razi's first objection from createdness and Sanusi's evaluation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection that the existence proof for ru'yat Allah would also imply that God could be created, and how Sanusi evaluates this objection.

Core Idea
If the existence proof is valid, then God could be created — an impossible conclusion.

Al-Razi, in his work al-Ma'alim, critiques the logical structure of the proof for the vision of God. He constructs a parallel argument using the concept of 'createdness' to show that the proof's reasoning leads to an absurdity. Sanusi acknowledges the strength of this objection and considers a potential response.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Read the passage carefully to understand the analogy between substance/accident and the shared judgment of createdness.
Notice how al-Razi applies the same logic used in the existence proof.
2
Identify the shared cause
Al-Razi argues that the shared judgment requires a shared cause, which he identifies as either originatedness (huduth) or existence. He eliminates originatedness.
This mirrors the structure of the original proof.
3
Understand the absurd conclusion
Since existence is the only remaining cause, it would apply to God, making it permissible for Him to be created.
This is the absurdity that refutes the proof.
4
Consider Sanusi's evaluation
Sanusi calls this objection strong and examines a possible answer: that the cause is possibility (imkan), not existence. He shows that this answer would undermine the original proof for vision.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمامُ في ( المعالم ) : ( وهذا عندي ضعيفٌ؛ لأنَّهُ يُقالُ : الجوهر والعرضُ مخلوقانِ ، فصحَّةُ المخلوقية فيهما حكم مشترك بينهما، فلا بدَّ لهُ مِنْ علَّةٍ مشتركة ، والمشترك إمَّا الحدوث أو الوجود، والحدوث باطل؛ لما ذكر تُموه ، تعيَّن الوجود ، فوجبَ كونه تعالى يصح أن يكون مخلوقاً ! وكما أنَّ هذا باطل فكذلك ما ذكر تُموه . وأيضاً : فإنَّا ندرك باللمس الطويل والعريض ، وندرك الحرارة والبرودة ، فصحَّةُ الملموسيَّةِ حكم مشترك ، ونسوقُ الكلام إلى آخره حتى يلزم صحَّةُ كونِهِ تعالى ملموساً ، والتزامه مدفوع ببديهة العقل ((١) . والأوَّلُ قويٌّ ، فإنْ أُجيب عنه بأنَّ صحة المخلوقيَّةِ مُعلَّلةٌ بالإمكان ، والبارى واجبٌ .. لزم مثله في صحة الرؤية . والثاني أيضاً قوي ، وجواب الأستاذ عنه بالفرق بينَ اللمس .
Al-Imam said in 'al-Ma'alim': 'This is weak in my view, because it is said: substance and accident are created, so the permissibility of createdness in both is a shared judgment between them, so it must have a shared cause. The shared cause is either originatedness (huduth) or existence. Originatedness is invalid for the reason you mentioned, so existence is determined, and so it is necessary that He, Exalted, is valid to be created! And just as this is false, so is what you mentioned. Also, we perceive by touch length and width, and we perceive heat and cold, so the permissibility of being tangible is a shared judgment, and we continue the argument to its end until it follows the permissibility of His being tangible, and accepting that is repudiated by the intuitive intellect.' And the first [objection] is strong. For if it is answered that the permissibility of createdness is caused by possibility (imkan), and the Creator is necessary... then the same would follow for the permissibility of seeing. And the second [objection] is also strong, and the answer of the teacher about it is by differentiating between touch...
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage presents al-Imam al-Razi's objection from his book al-Ma'alim against the existence proof for ru'yat Allah. Al-Razi argues that the same reasoning can be applied to createdness (makhluqiyya): substance and accident are both created, so the permissibility of being created is a shared judgment that requires a shared cause. That cause could be originatedness (huduth) or existence. Originatedness is invalid for the same reason it was rejected in the proof (presumably because it is not common to all existents). Therefore, existence would be the cause, leading to the absurd conclusion that God, being existent, could be created. Similarly, by touch we perceive length, width, heat, and cold, so the permissibility of being tangible is a shared judgment; following the same reasoning leads to the absurdity that God could be tangible, which is impossible by intuitive intellect. After presenting the objection, the author (Sanusi) comments that the first objection (about createdness) is strong. If one answers that the cause for permissibility of createdness is possibility (imkan) and God is necessary, then the same answer would apply to the permissibility of seeing, undermining the proof. The second objection (about tangibility) is also strong, and the teacher (perhaps Sanusi himself) answers it by distinguishing between touch and sight. The text cuts off before completing the response.

Key Terms
makhluqiyya
huduth
imkan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof for ru'yat Allah is sound and al-Razi's objection is merely rhetorical.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the proof's own logical structure leads to an absurdity, which means the proof is invalid as it stands. However, this does not deny the doctrine of ru'yat, only the specific rational argument.

Checkpoint
What shared cause does al-Razi identify for the permissibility of createdness?
A) Originatedness
B) Existence
C) Possibility
D) Both originatedness and existence

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection from createdness shows that the existence proof leads to the absurd conclusion that God could be created, thereby refuting the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a rational proof you've relied on for a core belief is shown to have a logical flaw, do you tend to abandon the belief or search for stronger evidence? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundations of your own convictions?
?
Sanusi acknowledges the strength of al-Razi's objection but does not conclude that ru'yat Allah is impossible. Are you willing to hold a theological position even when a particular argument for it is refuted, or do you feel the need to have a perfect proof for every belief?
?
The objection forces you to reflect on the limits of analogical reasoning in matters of the divine. In your own spiritual life, when do you rely on rational arguments versus revealed texts or direct experience? Are you comfortable with the tension between reason and revelation when they seem to conflict?

Al-Razi's Objection from Tangibility
Al-Razi's second objection from tangibility and Sanusi's evaluation.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
If the existence proof is valid, then God could be tangible — an impossibility that refutes the proof.

Al-Razi presents a second parallel argument: using the sense of touch, he shows that the same reasoning would imply that God is tangible, which is clearly false. Sanusi again affirms the strength of this objection and notes that the teacher responds by distinguishing touch from sight, though the text cuts off.

The text is incomplete, but the direction of the answer is indicated.

Refuting the Argument Against Seeing God
Sanusi refutes an argument against vision of God and discusses Imam al-Haramayn's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that vision requires physical influence is weak and how Imam al-Haramayn affirmed the possibility of perceiving God without customary causes.

Core Idea
Connection is customary, not rational — God can be seen without modality.

Sanusi addresses a common objection to the vision of God (ru'ya), which claims that vision involves influence and being influenced, impossible for God. He declares this argument weak and explains that the connection is based on custom, not rational necessity. He then presents the position of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who affirmed that all five perceptions can relate to God without their usual physical causes, a view also attributed to al-Ash'ari. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and clarifies the theological stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces the objection and refutes it.
Focus on the distinction between custom and rational necessity.
2
Identify the weak argument
The argument claims vision is impossible because it involves influence/influence. Sanusi says it's weak because the connection with touch is customary, not rational. Why does this matter?
Custom can be overridden by divine power.
3
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's view
He affirms all five perceptions can relate to God without customary causes. This contrasts with Abd Allah ibn Sa'id and al-Qalanisi, who denied that other perceptions (besides sight) can relate to God.
This supports the possibility of seeing God without physical modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرؤية ؛ لوجود التأثير والتأثرِ في الأَوَّلِ بخلاف الثاني . . ضعيف (۱) ؛ فإنَّ الاتصال الثابتَ معَ اللمس عادي لا عقلي ، فلم لا يجوز أن يتعلَّق هذا الإدراك بهِ تعالى مِنْ غير اتصال ولا تكييف ؟! وإمام الحرمين قد التزم هذا (۲)، وصحح تعلُّق الإدراكاتِ الخمسةِ بهِ تعالى مِنْ غيرِ أنْ تقارنها الأسباب المتصلة بها عادةً ، ونسب هذا أيضاً للشيخ الأشعري (۳) ، خلاف ما ذهَبَ إِليهِ عبدُ اللهِ بنُ سعيد والقلانسي مِنْ منع تعلَّقِ باقي الإدراكاتِ بهِ تعالى
And vision [is claimed impossible] due to the presence of influence and being influenced in the first unlike the second... weak (1). For the connection established with touch is customary, not rational. So why should it not be permissible for this perception to be related to Him - exalted is He - without connection and without modality?! And Imam al-Haramayn (2) committed to this, and affirmed the relation of the five perceptions to Him - exalted is He - without the customary causes accompanying them, and he attributed this also to Shaykh al-Ash'ari (3), contrary to what 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id and al-Qalanisi held of prohibiting the relation of the remaining perceptions to Him - exalted is He.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument against the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The argument presumably claimed that vision requires a connection involving influence and being influenced, which is impossible for God. The author declares this argument weak. He explains that the connection associated with touch is based on custom, not rational necessity, so it is not impossible for God to be perceived without such connection and without modality. Then he mentions that Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) adopted this position, affirming that all five senses can relate to God without their usual accompanying causes, and he attributed this view to al-Ash'ari as well. This contrasts with the view of Abd Allah ibn Sa'id and al-Qalanisi, who denied that the remaining perceptions (other than sight) can relate to God. So the author is supporting the possibility of God being perceived by senses other than sight, contrary to those who restrict it.

Key Terms
الرؤية
التأثير
التأثر
الاتصال
الإدراكات الخمسة
إمام الحرمين
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that perceiving God requires physical interaction or connection, making it impossible.
Correction: The connection involved in perception is customary, not rational, so God can be perceived without physical connection or modality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the argument against ru'ya weak?
A) Because vision is impossible for God.
B) Because the connection with touch is customary, not rational.
C) Because Imam al-Haramayn rejected it.
D) Because the five perceptions cannot relate to God.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God does not require physical connection; it is a matter of divine power and custom can be set aside.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can be perceived without customary means, how does that challenge your reliance on physical evidence for your faith? In what area of your life are you limiting God by assuming He must work through 'normal' causes?
?
The author says connection is customary, not rational. Is your relationship with God based on what you consider 'normal' and expected, or on submission to His absolute power? What would change in your dua and worship if you truly believed God could act without any customary intermediaries?
?
Imam al-Haramayn affirmed that all five senses can relate to God. Have you ever considered experiencing God beyond just 'seeing' Him? How would your desire for divine closeness increase if you believed you could 'hear' His speech directly or 'taste' His sweetness? Will you ask Him tonight to grant you even a glimpse of that reality?

Seventh Objection: Existence as Common Attribute for Vision and Its Answer
Sanusi presents the seventh objection to the proof of vision and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection to the proof of vision—that existence is not a univocal attribute—and Sanusi's answer defending univocal predication of existence.

Core Idea
Existence is univocal but not a genus; it does not make God composite.

The seventh objection attacks the proof of God's visibility by denying that existence is a common attribute in the same sense for God and creatures. If existence were univocal, it would make God composite, which is impossible. Sanusi offers a nuanced answer, showing that univocal predication does not entail genus or composition. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection noting that the objector grants the need for a common cause but denies existence is univocally predicated.
The objector appeals to al-Ash'ari's doctrine of existence being identical with essence.
2
Understand the consequence of univocal existence
If existence were univocal, it would be a genus of God, requiring a differentia and leading to composition—this is why the objector rejects univocity.
3
Examine the answer's strategy
Sanusi's answer commits to existence as additional to quiddity and univocal, but it is not an essential attribute, so it does not make God composite.
The proof: existence is divided into necessary and possible, so it must be common; but it is not essential because essences are understood without it.
4
Connect to different theological positions
Note that the answer works for al-Ghazali's view but not for those who say existence is identical with the existent (like al-Qadi and Imām al-Ḥaramayn).
This shows how the viability of the proof depends on one's metaphysics of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : سلَّمنا أنَّ المشترك لا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ علَّةٍ مشتركة لا تُسلَّمُ أنَّ الوجود مقول على الواجب والممكن بالاشتراك المعنوي ، بل بالاشتراك اللفظي ، وإلا لكانَ جنساً للواجب ، فيحتاج إلى فصل ، ويلزم التركيب في ذاتِ واجب الوجودِ جلَّ وعلا. كيف ومذهب الشيخ الأشعري أنَّهُ مشترك بالاشتراك اللفظي ، وأنَّ وجودَ كلِّ شيء هو عين ذاتِهِ ؟! وعلى هذا فلا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِ وجودِنا علَّةً لصحة رؤيتنا أن يكون وجود الباري تعالى علَّةً لصحة رؤيتِهِ. والجواب عسير على مذهب الشيخ ، وجوابه على الجملة : التزامُ أنَّ الوجود زائد على ماهيّة الموجودِ وإِنْ كانَ لا يفارقها ، وأَنَّهُ مقول على الموجوداتِ بالاشتراكِ المعنوي ؛ بدليل صحة انقسامِهِ إلى الواجب والممكن ، ومورد التقسيم لا بد وأن يكون مشتركاً ، ولا يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ جنساً إلا لو كان مشتركاً ذاتياً، وهو ممنوع ؛ بدليل عدم توقف فهم الذاتِ على فهمِهِ ، وهذا يتجه على اختيار الإمام في الوجود ، ولا يتجه على رأي مَنْ يقولُ : الوجود نفس الموجود ، وإن لم يكن تمام ماهيَّتِهِ ؛ كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين
Seventh: We grant that the common [attribute] necessarily has a common cause, but it is not granted that existence is predicated of the Necessary and the possible by univocal predication; rather, by equivocal predication. Otherwise it would be a genus for the Necessary, thus requiring a differentia, and composition in the essence of the Necessary of existence, exalted and high, would follow. How [can that be] when the doctrine of al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari is that it is equivocally predicated, and that the existence of every thing is identical with its essence?! On this basis, it does not follow from our existence being a cause for the validity of our vision that the existence of the Exalted Creator is a cause for the validity of His vision. The answer is difficult according to the doctrine of al-Shaykh, and his answer in summary is: the commitment that existence is additional to the quiddity of the existent, even if it does not separate from it, and that it is predicated of existents by univocal predication, evidenced by the validity of its division into the Necessary and the possible; and the subject of division must be common, and it does not necessitate being a genus unless it were an essential commonality, which is denied; evidenced by the fact that understanding the essence does not depend on understanding it. This is consistent with the choice of the Imām concerning existence, and is not consistent with the view of those who say existence is identical with the existent, even if it is not the entirety of its quiddity, like al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection to the proof that God can be seen because existence is a common attribute between God and contingent beings, and that common attribute causes visibility. The objector argues that existence is not a univocal common attribute (i.e., not predicated in the same sense) but only equivocal, because if it were univocal it would make existence a genus of God, which would imply composition in God's essence. Moreover, al-Ash'ari's own position is that existence is identical with each thing's essence, so there is no common attribute. Thus the proof fails. The answer (jawāb) is difficult on al-Ash'ari's view, but a summary answer is given: one can instead commit to the view that existence is a real attribute superadded to quiddity (though inseparable), and that it is predicated univocally across all existents. This is proven by the fact that existence can be divided into necessary and possible – the subject of such a division must be common. However, this does not make it a genus unless it were an essential commonality (mushtarak dhātī), which is not the case because understanding an essence does not depend on understanding existence. This answer works for the Imām (al-Ghazālī?) but not for those like al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn who say existence is identical with the existent but not the entirety of its quiddity. The commentary helps the reader see that the viability of the proof depends on one's metaphysics of existence.

Key Terms
الاشتراك المعنوي
الاشتراك اللفظي
الجنس
الفصل
التركيب
الوجود
الماهية
وجوب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A univocal common attribute necessarily makes God a composite being.
Correction: Univocal existence does not imply composition because it is not an essential attribute; essences are understood independently of existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector say existence cannot be univocal?
A) Because univocal predication would make existence a genus of God
B) Because existence is an accident
C) Because God's existence is unknowable
D) Because al-Ash'ari denies existence is an attribute

Key Takeaway
Existence is univocally predicated of God and creatures, but it is not an essential genus, so it does not imply composition in God.

The Nature of Temporal Origination and the Answer
Objection and answer about temporal origination as a mental attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal origination is a mental attribute, not a real one, and how this avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Temporal origination is a mental relation, not a real attribute.

This lesson addresses a key objection in kalam: is temporal origination (ḥadath) a real attribute that would apply even to God? The objector argues it is, but the response explains it is a purely mental consideration. Understanding this clarifies the Ashʿarī stance on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the objector defines ḥadath and claims its attribute is constant.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has an objection and a response. Find the boundary and see how each part builds.
3
Understand the response's logic
The answer argues that if ḥadath were real, it would itself be temporal, causing infinite regress. This proves it is a mental attribute.
This is a classic proof by contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask: Why does this matter for divine attributes? The answer prevents attributing temporality to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحدوث هو الوجود المقيَّدُ بمسبوقيَّةِ العدم ، والمسبوقية أمر مقارن للوجودِ وكيفية له ، وصفة الثابتِ ثابتة . وجوابه : أنَّ الحدوث صفةٌ اعتباريَّةٌ ، لا حقيقة ثابتة ، وإلا لكانَتْ حادثة أيضاً ، ولزم التسلسل
Temporal origination is existence restricted by being preceded by nonexistence; being preceded is something accompanying existence and a modality for it, and the attribute of what is constant is constant. Its answer: that temporal origination is a relational attribute, not a fixed reality; otherwise it would itself be temporal, and infinite regress would be necessary.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector defines hadath (temporal origination) as existence qualified by prior nonexistence; the attribute of being preceded (mashuqiyya) accompanies existence and is a quality of it. The objector then asserts that the attribute of what is constant (al-thabit) is constant—implying that if existence is constant (as in God's existence), then the attribute of being preceded would also be constant, which is problematic. The answer refutes this by explaining that hadath is a purely mental/relational attribute (ṣifa iʿtibāriyya), not a real attribute. If it were real, it would itself be subject to coming into existence (ḥāditha), leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus, the objection is invalid. The argument follows the standard Ashʿarī position that attributes of relation are not real entities.

Key Terms
الحدوث
المسبوقية
صفة اعتبارية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That temporal origination is a real, fixed attribute that would apply to God's existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ḥadath is a mental/relational attribute, not a real attribute, avoiding contradictions in divine simplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is ḥadath not a real attribute?
A) Because it is a fixed reality
B) Because it would lead to infinite regress if real
C) Because it is a necessary attribute of God
D) Because it is a sensation

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination is not a real attribute but a mental consideration, preventing infinite regress and preserving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat concepts like 'luck' or 'fate' as if they were real, independent entities? How does this lesson's distinction between real and mental attributes challenge you to rethink the way you view such concepts?
?
This passage shows that treating ḥadath as a real attribute leads to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, are there any beliefs or justifications that, if pursued endlessly, would trap you in a regress? What step can you take today to ground yourself in the transcendent reality of God, not in endless mental constructs?
?
When you think about God's attributes (e.g., His power, His knowledge), do you ever imagine them as something added to His essence or as temporal? How can you train your heart to see them as mere relations that protect His absolute oneness and permanence, rather than as entities that need to be defined or justified?

Existence as a Cause of Visibility: Objection and Answer
Objection that existence causes visibility only for us, answered by the invariability of rational causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ashʿarīs argue that a rational cause cannot be subject-relative and how they defend God's absolute power.

Core Idea
A rational cause's effect is invariable; it cannot depend on the subject.

The objector suggests that existence might only cause visibility when found in a suitable subject, just as the ability to create only applies to God. This would allow that God is visible to Himself but not to us. The answer firmly rejects this, arguing that rational causes are universal in their effect. This passage is a key defense of the Ashʿarī view that God's power applies equally to all possible things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim: existence could be a cause of visibility only for a suitable subject, like how creation is only valid from God.
Notice the analogy with creation—a key parallel.
2
Identify the analogy used
The objector says that just as the validity of creating substances depends on possibility relative to God, so visibility might depend on existence relative to a suitable subject.
This analogy is meant to show that causes can be subject-specific.
3
Examine the answer's key claim
The answer asserts that a rational cause's judgment never fails; it is invariable. Then it contrasts God's effective power with human powerlessness.
The conclusion: God's power relates equally to all possibles, so existence cannot be a relative cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحادي عشر : ما المانعُ أنْ يكونَ الوجودُ علَّةً لصحة الرؤيةِ بالنسبةِ إلينا ؟ والعلَّةُ إنَّما تقتضي حكمها إذا وُجِدَتْ في محلها ؛ فإِنَّ صحةَ خلْقِ الجواهرِ مُعلَّلُ بإمكانها بالنسبة إلى اللهِ تعالى ؛ لأنَّ الخَلْقَ إِنَّمَا يصح منه ، ولا يصح بالنسبة إلينا . وجوابه : أنَّ العلَّةَ العقليّة لا يتخلَّفُ حكمها عنها بحال ، وقدرتنا لا تُؤثر ، وقدرة الباري تعالى مُؤثرةٌ ، ونسبتها إلى سائر الممكناتِ نسبةٌ واحدةٌ ، ولذلكَ قلنا : إنَّ البارى تعالى قادر على كل الممكناتِ ومُوجِدٌ لها ، وليس للعبدِ قدرة على إيجاد ممكن ألبتة
The eleventh: What prevents existence from being a cause for the validity of vision with respect to us? The cause only necessitates its judgment when it exists in its subject; for the validity of creating substances is explained by their possibility with respect to God, because creation is only valid from Him, and it is not valid with respect to us. Its answer: that the rational cause's judgment is never separate from it, and our power has no effect, and the power of the Exalted Creator is effective, and its relation to all possible things is one relation; therefore we said: the Exalted Creator is able over all possible things and brings them into existence, and the servant has no power to bring any possible into existence at all.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This is the eleventh objection and its answer. The objector argues that existence could be a cause for the validity of vision (ru'ya) only in relation to us, not in relation to God. The cause's effect depends on being in a suitable subject. For example, the validity of creating substances is due to their possibility (imkān) in relation to God, but not in relation to us—creation is valid only from God. So, existence could be a cause for vision only when found in a subject capable of being seen. The answer asserts that a rational cause's judgment never fails; our power (qudra) has no effect, while God's power is effective and relates equally to all possible things. Thus, God is able to bring all possibles into existence, and humans have no power to create. Hence, existence as a cause of visibility cannot be subject-relative in the way claimed; it must be universal if it is a rational cause. The argument defends the Ashʿarī position that God's power is absolute and that vision of God is possible because existence is a sufficient cause.

Key Terms
صحة الرؤية (validity of vision)
العلة العقلية (rational cause)
القدرة (power)
الإمكان (possibility)
خلق الجواهر (creation of substances)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a rational cause's effect can depend on the subject it is in.
Correction: The passage argues that a rational cause's judgment never fails; it is invariable.

Checkpoint
What does the response say about the relation of God's power to possible things?
A) It varies depending on the kind of possible
B) It is a single relation to all possibles
C) It is stronger for some than others
D) It does not extend to created substances

Key Takeaway
A rational cause cannot have a variable effect; therefore existence, if it is a rational cause of visibility, must apply equally to all subjects, including God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you still attribute real causal power to yourself or to other humans, as if your effort could bring about existence independent of God's will? Will you repent and affirm that only God's power is truly effective?
?
When you think about seeing God in the afterlife, do you believe that your own existence as a subject enables that vision? Or do you submit to the fact that God's power is the sole cause of visibility, regardless of the subject? How does your belief shape your longing for the Beatific Vision?

The Twelfth Objection: Vision and Discernment
Objection and answer: vision, existence, and discernment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that vision of existence would prevent discerning differences, distinguishing between rational necessity and customary association.

Core Idea
Vision of existence is not a barrier to discernment; discernment follows by custom, not necessity.

The objection that vision attached to existence would make all things appear identical seems compelling. But Sanusi shows that this confuses a natural custom with a rational necessity. He further refutes Abu Hashim's alternative view by exposing an internal inconsistency in the Mu'tazili theory of states.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and question
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts: the objection from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and the additional question from the Bahshamiyya.
The Bahshami question is about discerning differences among objects if vision attaches to existence.
2
Understand Sanusi's distinction
Sanusi responds that when we see something, we first know its existence, and knowledge of its distinction follows. He clarifies that this is not an intellectual necessity but a customary matter.
This distinction is crucial: the connection is based on custom (ʿādah), not rational necessity.
3
Examine the refutation of Abu Hashim
Sanusi criticizes Abu Hashim's claim that vision attaches to the specific and then yields knowledge of the general existence. He points out an inconsistency: Abu Hashim holds that the most specific description is a psychological state, but vision cannot perceive a psychological state directly.
This refutation turns the Mu'tazili theory of states against itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني عشر : أنَّ هذهِ الحجَّةَ تنتقصُ بالوجهين اللذين ذكرهما الإمامُ الفخرُ ، وقد تقدَّما وزادَتِ البهشميَّةُ سؤالاً ؛ وهو أنَّ الرؤية لو تعلَّقَتْ بالوجودِ لَمَا أدركنا اختلاف الأشياء . وجوابه : أنا إذا شاهدنا شيئاً علمنا وجودَه ، وتبعه العلم بتمييزه . وقال أبو هاشم : الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ويتبعه العلم بالوجودِ الأعم . قال : وما ذكرناه أدخلُ في قضية العقل ؛ فإنَّ العلم بالأخص يستلزم العلم بالأعم ، ولا ينعكس . قلنا : نحنُ لا ندَّعي أنَّ ذلكَ لازم للعقلاء إلا عادةً ، بل نقولُ : إِنْ عُلِمَ ذلكَ في بعض الأشياء فهو قضية عادية . وقولُ أبي هاشم : ( إنَّ الرؤية تتعلق بالأخص ، ثم يتبعها العلم بالوجودِ ) كيف يصح منهُ معَ زعمِهِ أنَّ أخص وصف الشيء حال نفسية
The twelfth: That this argument is undermined by the two points mentioned by Imam Fakhr, which have preceded. And the Bahshamiyya added a question: that if vision attached to existence, we would not perceive differences among things. Its answer: when we witness something, we know its existence, and knowledge of its distinction follows it. Abu Hashim said: vision attaches to the specific, and knowledge of the general existence follows it. He said: what we mentioned is more relevant to the rational judgment; for knowledge of the specific entails knowledge of the general, but not vice versa. We said: we do not claim that is necessary for rational beings except as a custom; rather we say: if that is known in some things, it is a customary matter. And the statement of Abu Hashim: (vision attaches to the specific, then knowledge of existence follows it) — how can this be valid from him given his claim that the most specific description of a thing is a psychological state?
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is the twelfth objection and its extended answer. The objection states that the proof (for the possibility of seeing God) is flawed by two arguments from Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi; these are referenced as having preceded. Then the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim al-Jubbai) add a question: if vision (ru'ya) attaches to existence (al-wujud), we would not perceive differences among objects (since existence is common). The answer (by Sanusi): when we see a thing, we first know its existence, and knowledge of its distinction (tamyīz) follows. Abu Hashim is then cited: vision attaches to the specific (al-akhass) and knowledge of the more general existence follows. He argues that this is more consistent with reason because knowledge of the specific entails knowledge of the general, but not the reverse. Sanusi responds: we do not claim this is an intellectual necessity (lāzim li-l-ʿuqalāʾ) except by custom (ʿādatan); rather, if it is known in some cases, it is only a customary matter, not a rational one. Finally, he refutes Abu Hashim's claim: how can he say vision attaches to the specific and then yields knowledge of existence, when he also holds that the most specific description of a thing is a psychological state (ḥāl nafsiyya)? The point is that if the specific is a psychological state, it cannot be the direct object of vision (since vision perceives external objects). This refutation leverages the Mu'tazili notion of 'states' (aḥwal). The passage shows Sanusi defending the Ashʿarī position that vision of God is possible by a direct perception of existence, and discernment of differences is a secondary effect.

Key Terms
الرؤية (vision)
الوجود (existence)
الأخص (the specific)
الأعم (the general)
قضية عادية (customary matter)
حال نفسية (psychological state)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That vision of existence would prevent discerning differences between objects.
Correction: Sanusi argues that discernment follows vision as a customary consequence, not as an intellectual necessity. Moreover, the refutation of Abu Hashim shows that the proposed alternative is flawed.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does knowledge of distinction follow vision of existence?
A) By intellectual necessity
B) By customary association
C) By inference from the specific
D) It does not follow at all

Key Takeaway
Vision of existence does not preclude discernment; the connection is customary, not rationally necessary, and Abu Hashim's alternative is internally inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at something, do you first recognize its existence or its specific qualities? Reflect on how your everyday perception might be more about custom than logical necessity. How does this affect your confidence in what you see?
?
Sanusi insists that the link between vision and discernment is not rationally necessary but customary. In your own life, what habits of thought have you assumed were necessary when they are merely customary? Are you willing to question them?
?
Abu Hashim's view collapsed because he held an inconsistent position. Is there an inconsistency in your own beliefs about Allah's attributes that you have been avoiding? What would it cost you to resolve it?

The Ash'arite Definition of Perception and the Possibility of Seeing God
Sanusi contrasts two theories of vision and defines perception as a created meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite definition of perception as a meaning created by God in the perceiver, and how this definition allows for the possibility of seeing God without needing a body or direction.

Core Idea
Perception is a meaning God creates directly, not dependent on rays or direction.

Sanusi contrasts two theories of vision: the Mu'tazilite ray theory, which leads to denying the possibility of seeing God, and the Ash'arite view that perception is a direct creation of God. This lesson unpacks the Ash'arite definition and its implications for the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from describing the opponents' theory to presenting the Ash'arite view.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
Find the conditions for vision: being opposite, not too close/far, and the ray mechanism. Note why they conclude God cannot be seen (not a body, no direction).
The ray base and emitter are key terms.
3
Understand the Ash'arite definition of perception
Perception is a meaning (ma‘nā) created by God in the perceiver. Note how it is named differently depending on the organ. This is the core of the Ash'arite position.
Contrast this with the ray theory.
4
Grasp the role of 'āda and kharq al-‘āda
Normal conditions for perception are by habit, not necessity. God can break habit (kharq al-‘āda) to create perception of what is extremely near/far or without direction.
This is how seeing God becomes possible.
5
Reflect on the outcome
The Ash'arite definition removes the physical restrictions, making the vision of God possible. Connect this to the broader Aqeedah of the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيرى ; بشرط أنْ يكونَ في مقابلة الرائي ، وبشرط انتفاء القربِ والبعدِ المفرطينِ ، وإِنَّما تقعُ الرؤيةُ عندَهم بالطَّرْفِ بِطَرَفِ تلكَ الأشعةِ المتصل بالمرئي، ويُسمُّونَهُ : قاعدة الشعاع ، ويُسمُّونَ المتصل منها بالناظرِ (۱) : منبعثَ الشعاع ، وقالوا : إنَّ قاعدة الشعاع إذا لاقت جسماً صقيلاً لا تضرُّس فيه كالمرآة . . لم تتشبَّتْ بهِ ، بل تنعكس إلى الرائي فيرى نفْسَهُ . قالوا : وإِنَّما لم يرَ داخلَ الجفن للقرب المفرط ، فلهذا قالوا : لا يصحُ أن يُرى جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ لاستحالةِ اتصال الأشعةِ بهِ ؛ لأنَّها إِنَّما تتصل بالأجسام ، ولاستدعائها جهةً تنبعث إليها ، والله جل وعلا ليسَ بجرم ولا في جهة . وأهل الحقِّ رضيَ الله تعالى عنهم يقولون : الإدراك معنى يخلقه الله تعالى في المدرِكِ ؛ فإِنْ خُلِقَ في جزءٍ مِنَ العينِ سُمِّيَ إبصاراً ، وفي جزءٍ مِنَ القلبِ سُمِّيَ عِلْماً ، وفي جزء مِنَ الأُذُنِ سُمِّيَ سمعاً ، وفي اللسانِ سُمِّيَ ذوقاً ، وفي كلِّ الجسدِ سُمِّيَ حِسّاً ، واختصاص خلْقِهِ بهذهِ المَحال إنَّما هو بحكم العادة ، وكذا اختصاص بعضها ؛ بأنْ يكونَ المُدرَكُ في جهةٍ وغير قريب جداً ولا بعيد جداً . . إنَّما هو بحكمِ العادة ، ويجوز أن تنخرقَ العادةُ ، فيتعلَّق بما هو قريب جداً أو بعيد جداً ، بل وبما ليس في جهةٍ كما جرت العادة بذلك في العلم(٢)
Thus one sees, on condition that it be opposite the viewer, and on condition that extreme closeness and extreme distance be absent. And vision, according to them, only occurs through the ray (al-ṭarf) by the tip of those rays connected to the visible object, and they call it: the base of the ray (qā‘idat al-shu‘ā‘), and they call the part connected to the eye: the emitter of the ray (mumbai‘ith al-shu‘ā‘). And they said: When the base of the ray meets a polished body without roughness, like a mirror, it does not attach to it, but rather reflects to the viewer, and he sees himself. They said: He does not see the inside of the eyelid because of extreme closeness; therefore they said: It is not valid that the Mighty and Majestic be seen, due to the impossibility of the rays connecting to Him, because they only connect to bodies, and because they require a direction from which they emanate, and God, Mighty and Majestic, is neither a body nor in a direction. And the People of Truth, may God be pleased with them, say: Perception (al-idrāk) is a meaning (ma‘nā) that God creates in the perceiver. If it is created in a part of the eye, it is called sight; in a part of the heart, it is called knowledge; in a part of the ear, it is called hearing; in the tongue, it is called taste; in the whole body, it is called touch. And the specification of its creation in these places is only by the rule of habit (al-‘āda), and likewise the specification that the perceived object be in a direction and not extremely near nor extremely far is only by the rule of habit. And it is permissible for habit to be broken, so that it attaches to what is extremely near or extremely far, indeed even to what is not in a direction, just as habit has established that in the case of knowledge.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage presents two opposing theories of vision and then defines perception in Ash'arite terms. The passage begins by describing the Mu'tazilite (or generally the opponents') theory of vision through emission of rays. According to this theory, vision requires the object to be in front of the viewer, not too close or too far, and occurs when rays from the eye connect to the object. They call the ray base 'qā‘idat al-shu‘ā‘' and the part that leaves the eye 'mumbai‘ith al-shu‘ā‘'. They also claim that when the ray meets a polished surface like a mirror, it reflects back so that the viewer sees himself. Because of this theory, they deny the possibility of seeing God, since rays can only connect to bodies and require a direction, and God is neither a body nor in a direction. The Ash'arites (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) reply by defining perception differently: perception is a meaning (ma‘nā) created by God in the perceiving organ. Depending on which organ it is created in, it is given different names: in the eye it is sight, in the heart knowledge, etc. The fact that perception usually occurs in a particular organ, and that the perceived object is normally in a specific direction and at a moderate distance, is due to the regular habit (al-‘āda) that God has established. However, this habit is not necessary; God can break it (kharq al-‘āda) and create perception of what is extremely near, extremely far, or even of what has no direction, just as He creates knowledge of Himself without any direction. Thus, the Ash'arites argue that seeing God is possible because the ray theory is false and perception is a direct act of God not dependent on physical conditions.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrāk)
الشعاع (al-shu‘ā‘)
قاعدة الشعاع (qā‘idat al-shu‘ā‘)
العادة (al-‘āda)
خرق العادة (kharq al-‘āda)
الرؤية (al-ru’ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that seeing God requires a physical body for the viewer or that God must be in a direction to be seen.
Correction: The Ash'arites clarify that perception is a meaning created by God in the perceiver, not dependent on the ray mechanism or any physical conditions. Even without direction, God can create perception (like knowledge).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arite definition of perception, what is perception?
A) A ray emitted from the eye that connects to the object.
B) A meaning created by God in the perceiving organ.
C) The reflection of light from the object.
D) The movement of atoms in the eye.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite definition of perception as a created meaning, independent of physical conditions, establishes the possibility of seeing God without needing a body or direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If perception is a meaning God creates directly in you, independent of physical conditions, then why do you still doubt that you can truly see God with your own eyes in the Hereafter, even without a body or direction? What does this reveal about your reliance on your own physical experience rather than divine power?
?
The Ash'arites teach that the regular association between your eye and sight is only a habit of God, not a necessary connection. How does this challenge your daily assumption that your senses are reliable guides? Can you really trust your own sight without acknowledging it as a direct gift from God each moment?
?
God can break His habit and create sight of what is extremely near or far, or even of Himself. Why do you still limit God's ability to make Himself known to you in ways that defy your normal expectations? What specifically are you afraid of in surrendering your concept of causality to God's direct action?

Objection Against Ray Theory and Philosophers' Response
An objection against the ray theory and the philosophers' response about images in mirrors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the ray theory of vision and the philosophers' response involving impressed images in mirrors.

Core Idea
The ray theory fails for mirrors unless we distinguish the viewer from the image.

This passage presents a classic objection to the emission theory of vision: if we see by a ray from the eye, then in a mirror we would only see ourselves, not the mirror. The philosophers respond by arguing that what is seen is an image impressed in the mirror, not the viewer. This sets up a debate about the nature of perception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ray theory objection
Read the objection carefully. The objector claims that if the ray is the basis for seeing, then in a mirror the viewer would see himself but not the mirror, which contradicts experience.
2
Examine the philosophers' response
The philosophers deny that the visible object in the mirror is the viewer himself. Instead, it is an image impressed in the mirror that faces the viewer.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this response matters for the theory of vision. Does it save the ray theory? The next lesson will show it fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة الشعاع التي باعتبارها صح إدراك المرئي لم تتحقق؛ إذ لا تشبث لها فيهما؛ لعدم التضريس كما زعموا، فيلزم على قولهم: أن يرى نفسه ولا يرى المرآة ولا الماء، وهو خلاف الحس. وأجاب الحكماء عن الإلزام باعتبار اشتراط المقابلة بأن قالوا: لا تسلم أن المرئي في المرآة والماء لم يقابل الرائي، وتوهمكم ذلك إنما جاء من اعتقادكم أن المرئي فيهما نفس الرائي، ونحن نقول: إن المرئي إنما هو صورة منطبعة فيهما موافقة لصورة الرائي، لا نفس الرائي، ولا شك حينئذ أن تلك الصورة مقابلة للرائي.
The basis of the ray by which it is correct to perceive the visible has not been realized; for there is no attachment for it in them (the mirror and water) due to the lack of roughness as they claimed. So it follows from their statement that he would see himself and not see the mirror or the water, which contradicts sensory experience. And the philosophers answered the objection by considering the condition of facing, saying: It is not conceded that the visible in the mirror and water is not facing the viewer; your misconception arises only from your belief that the visible in them is the viewer himself. But we say: The visible is only an image impressed in them conforming to the image of the viewer, not the viewer himself, and there is no doubt then that that image is facing the viewer.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the theory that vision occurs via a ray emitted from the eye. The objector argues that if the ray were the basis for seeing, then in the case of a mirror or water, the ray would have no hold (attachment) because there is no roughness as they claim. Consequently, the viewer would see himself (since the ray returns to him) but would not see the mirror or water, which is contrary to experience. The philosophers respond by saying that what is seen in the mirror is not the viewer himself but an image impressed in the mirror; thus the image is facing the viewer, satisfying the condition of facing. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
شعاع
إدراك المرئي
اشتراط المقابلة
صورة منطبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mirror objection proves the ray theory is false.
Correction: The philosophers respond that the visible in the mirror is an image, not the viewer, so the condition of facing is satisfied.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what would the ray theory imply about seeing in a mirror?
A) The viewer sees the mirror clearly
B) The viewer sees himself but not the mirror
C) The viewer sees nothing
D) The viewer sees both himself and the mirror

Key Takeaway
The mirror objection to the ray theory is answered by distinguishing the viewer from the image impressed in the mirror.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed that seeing oneself in the mirror must mean one sees oneself directly. What assumptions do you make in your own life that, like the objector's, conflate the image with the reality? How might you test those assumptions today?
?
The philosophers distinguished between the viewer and the image to resolve a contradiction. In your spiritual journey, have you ever confused your own image (self-perception) with the truth of who you are before Allah? What would it take to see yourself as the 'impressed image'—a reflection of what He wants you to be—rather than as you assume?
?
This passage shows how a plausible-seeming argument can rest on a false premise. Think of a time you were convinced of a belief about God or yourself that later proved based on a flawed foundation. What specific step can you take this week to examine one of your deeply-held assumptions?

Refuting the Image Theory: The Reflection Moves with the Viewer
Ahl al-Haqq refute the image theory using the mirror reflection movement argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq refutation of the philosophical claim that the visible in a mirror is an image impressed in the surface.

Core Idea
Reflections move with the viewer, so they cannot be fixed images on the mirror.

This passage presents a concise logical refutation by Ahl al-Ḥaqq of the philosophers' 'image theory.' The argument shows that if reflections were images stuck to the mirror surface, they would not change position when the viewer moves. But they do — proving that the visible is the viewer themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The argument uses a logical counterfactual to show absurdity.
2
Analyze the distance argument
Identify why an impressed image would not recede or approach with the viewer. Understand that its fixed position on the surface contradicts experience.
This is the first of two parallel points.
3
Analyze the movement argument
Consider the second point: the image would also not move with the viewer's movement, yet we see that it does.
This reinforces the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب أهل الحق: بأنه لو كان المرئي صورة منطبعة في جسمي المرآة والماء.. للزم ألا تبعد تلك الصورة ببعد الرائي منهما ولا تقرب بقربه؛ ضرورة قيام تلك الصورة بسطحي المرآة والماء، فوجب أن تثبت بثباتهما، فدل ذلك على أن المرئي نفس الرائي، لا شيء ينطبع في المرآة والماء، وكذلك يلزم ألا تتحرك بحركته، وهو ظاهر.
The people of truth answered: If the visible were an image impressed in the bodies of the mirror and water, it would necessarily follow that that image would not move away with the viewer's distance from them nor approach with his proximity, because necessarily that image is established on the surfaces of the mirror and water. So it would have to remain fixed with their fixation. This indicates that the visible is the viewer himself, not something impressed in the mirror and water. And likewise it would necessarily follow that it would not move with his movement, which is obvious.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is the refutation by Ahl al-Ḥaqq of the philosophers' claim that the visible in the mirror is an image impressed in the mirror surface. The refutation argues that if the image were on the surface, it would not change position relative to the viewer's distance: it would not recede when the viewer steps back nor approach when he steps forward. But in experience, the reflection does move with the viewer. Therefore, the visible object must be the viewer himself, not a separate image. Similarly, the reflection moves with the viewer's movement. So the philosophers' account fails.

Key Terms
صورة منطبعة
أهل الحق
بعد الرائي
تحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The image is a physical copy impressed on the mirror's surface.
Correction: The argument shows that such an image would not move with the viewer, but reflections do, so the visible is the viewer.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Ahl al-Haqq refutation of the image theory?
A) The image would not be facing the viewer
B) The image would not move with the viewer's distance
C) The image would be too small
D) The image would be inverted

Key Takeaway
The reflection's movement with the viewer disproves the image theory; the visible is the viewer themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the philosophers argue that the image is a fixed impression on the mirror, they treat the reflection as separate from themselves. Do you treat your own faults or sins as something fixed and separate from you, like an image stuck on a mirror, rather than a direct reflection of your inner state? How does realizing that they are inherently connected to you change your approach to repentance?
?
The reflection moves with you—it responds to your every step. Is there a part of your relationship with Allah that you treat as static, expecting Him to respond without you moving towards Him? What step can you take today to move closer to Him, knowing that your movement affects what you see?
?
The argument concludes that what you see in the mirror is yourself. When you evaluate your actions, do you see them as impersonal events disconnected from your identity, or do you recognize that they are you? If your deeds are truly you, what will you do to ensure the reflection you present to Allah is one you are willing to meet?

Refuting the Air Argument for Seeing Larger Objects
Refutation of the air argument for seeing larger objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the condition of facing (muqābala) for vision and how it is refuted by showing the absurdity of the air argument.

Core Idea
The air argument fails because it requires air to face both viewer and object simultaneously.

This passage continues the critique of the condition of facing as necessary for vision. The opponents argue that if facing is required, we could never see objects larger than ourselves. They propose that illuminated air mediates vision. Sanusi refutes this by showing it leads to an impossibility. Understanding this refutation strengthens the argument that vision does not require literal facing.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Explore the initial objection: that if facing is required, we can only see things equal to or smaller than our own size.
2
Understand the philosophical response
The philosophers suggest that the ray or illuminated air helps overcome this limitation.
3
Follow the refutation
Notice how the refutation points out that if the air faces the large object, it cannot simultaneously face the viewer without absurdity.
4
Connect to previous discussion
The passage references earlier arguments about the pupil, reinforcing the pattern of refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يلزم على اشتراط المقابلة: ألا يرى الرائي إلا قدر ذاته؛ إذ لا يقابل أكبر منها. قالوا: الشعاع أعان على ذلك. قلنا: قد تقدم جوابه (1) يعني: أنه مما يرد عليهم في اشتراطهم المقابلة: أنه يلزمهم ألا يرى الرائي من الأجسام ما هو أكبر من جسمه؛ لأنه لا يقابل أكبر منه. فإن قالوا: الهواء المضيء الذي بينه وبين ذلك الجسم الأكبر مقابل لذلك الجسم الأكبر، فأعان على رؤيته. قلنا: فيلزم أن ما قابل من الهواء ذلك الجسم العظيم يكون مقابلاً للرائي ليعين بعد رؤيته على رؤية ما قابل، وهو محال (1)، وقد تقدم مثل هذا عند ذكر جوابهم عما ألزموه من عدم رؤية الإنسان أكثر من حدقته (3).
And among what necessarily follows from the condition of facing: that the viewer would not see except the measure of his own self, since he cannot face anything larger than it. They said: The ray aided in that. We said: Its answer has already preceded (1) meaning: it is something that comes back against them in their requirement of facing: that it necessarily follows that the viewer would not see among bodies what is larger than his body, because he cannot face anything larger than it. For if they say: The illuminated air that is between him and that larger body is facing that larger body, so it aided in seeing it. We say: Then it would follow that what faces of the air that great body would be facing the viewer to aid, after its seeing, in seeing what it faced, which is impossible (1), and the like of this has already preceded when mentioning their answer to what they were obligated with regarding a person not seeing more than the size of his pupil (3).
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage presents another objection against the condition of facing (muqābala) as necessary for vision. If facing is required, the viewer can only face something equal to or smaller than his own size, so he could not see anything larger than himself. The philosophers might respond that the ray or the illuminated air helps. The refutation points out that this leads to absurdity: if the air facing the large body is considered facing the viewer to aid vision, then the air would have to be facing both simultaneously, which is impossible. The argument references a previous discussion about the pupil.

Key Terms
اشتراط المقابلة
الهواء المضيء
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The illuminated air or ray can mediate vision of larger objects without violating the facing condition.
Correction: The refutation shows that the air would need to face both the viewer and the large object simultaneously, which is impossible, thus the air argument fails.

Checkpoint
What absurdity does the refutation point to in the air argument?
A) The air cannot be illuminated
B) The air would have to face both the viewer and the large body simultaneously
C) The air is not visible
D) The ray does not exist

Key Takeaway
The condition of facing cannot be salvaged by appealing to illuminated air because that would require the air to face two opposite directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson exposes the danger of relying on 'intermediaries' that cannot do what we claim. In your spiritual life, are there rationalizations or excuses you use to avoid directly facing a truth about yourself? What would it cost you to be honest and let that truth transform you?
?
The refutation here depends on logical consistency: a single thing cannot face two opposite directions. In your own beliefs and practices, are there contradictions you tolerate because they are convenient? Are you willing to let the truth correct you, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
Sanusi's argument forces us to think: if an argument leads to an impossibility, it must be rejected. Where in your life have you accepted a flawed argument because it seemed to solve a problem, only to realize it required an impossibility? How can you cultivate the courage to reject such arguments and seek the truth directly?

God's Sight and Prophet's Vision Refute the Ray Theory
Sanusi presents two counterexamples refuting the philosophers' theory of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of God's sight and the Prophet's vision of Paradise directly demolish the philosophical theory that vision requires rays, direction, and facing.

Core Idea
Vision does not require rays or facing; God's sight and the Prophet's vision prove it.

The philosophers claimed that vision requires rays from the eye, a direction, and no obstacles. This lesson presents two powerful counterexamples from Islamic theology that completely undermine that theory. First, God's sight of all things without any physical means. Second, the Prophet's vision of Paradise from his place despite immense distance and many veils.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the two examples: God's sight and the Prophet's vision.
The passage is short but packed with refutation.
2
Analyze the first refutation
Focus on the description of God's sight: without a visual structure, without a ray, not in a direction, not facing. How does this contradict the philosophers' conditions?
God sees everything, so vision can occur without those conditions.
3
Analyze the second refutation
Consider the Prophet's vision of Paradise from his place with extreme distance and many veils. Why does this prevent the philosophers' claim about rays and obstacles?
This shows that distance and veils are not absolute obstacles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة البصر له سبحانه تهدم ما أصلوه: ولو سلم ذلك كله فرؤية الله تعالى لكل موجود ولا بنية ولا شعاع وليس في جهة ولا مقابلة.. يهدم ما أصلوه. وأيضاً: فما ثبت من رؤية النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الجنة من موضعه مع غاية البعد وكثافة الحجب الكثيرة.. يمنع ما تخيلوه من الأشعة والموانع.
The attribute of sight for Him, exalted is He, demolishes what they have founded: And even if all that were conceded, the vision of God, exalted, of every existent without a structure (visual organ) and without a ray and not being in a direction or facing... demolishes what they have founded. And also: what is established of the Prophet's, peace be upon him, vision of Paradise from his place with extreme distance and the density of many veils... prevents what they imagined of rays and obstacles.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage concludes the refutation by appealing to two examples that contradict the philosophers' theory of vision requiring rays, facing, etc. First, God's sight: He sees everything without physical eye structure, ray, direction, or facing. This shows that vision can occur without those conditions. Second, the Prophet Muhammad's vision of Paradise from his place, despite the great distance and many veils, demonstrates that rays and obstacles are not necessary. Thus, the entire foundational premise of the philosophers is demolished.

Key Terms

جهة
مقابلة
حجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The examples are merely analogies or metaphors, not direct refutations.
Correction: The passage presents them as direct counterexamples that contradict the necessary conditions posited by the philosophers, thus demolishing their theory. They are not analogies but proofs.

Checkpoint

D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The attribute of God's sight and the Prophet's vision prove that vision can occur without rays, direction, or obstacles, refuting the philosophical theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If vision does not require physical conditions for God, how does that affect your understanding of His closeness to you? Are you living in a way that invites His sight with awe, or do you ignore His presence?

God's Sight Refutes Physical Conditions for Vision
Sanusi argues that God's sight, being free from physical conditions, refutes their entire framework for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's attribute of sight cannot be subject to physical conditions like eye structure, rays, or direction.

Core Idea
God's vision is free from all physical limitations.

Sanusi has been discussing the rational conditions for vision posited by some theologians. In this passage, he delivers a decisive refutation: even if those conditions were valid for humans, they cannot apply to God. By affirming that God sees every existent without any physical apparatus, Sanusi demolishes their entire framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's argument unfold naturally.
Notice that he starts with a concessive phrase 'ولو سلم ذلك كله' (even if all that is granted).
2
Identify the three conditions refuted
The passage explicitly names three conditions for vision that are impossible for God: an eye structure, emission of rays, and facing (direction). Find each one.
These correspond to hadqa, shu'aa', and muqabala.
3
Understand why each is impossible
For each condition, Sanusi gives a reason: an eye structure implies corporeality, rays are bodies and only separate from bodies, facing requires direction which is impossible for God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that all their conditions are invalid because God's sight is free from these physical limitations. This refutes their entire framework.
The key phrase is 'فبطل بهذا كل ما أصلوه في الرؤية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سُلَّمَ ذلكَ كلُّهُ فرؤيةُ اللهِ تعالى لكلِّ موجود ولا بنية ولا شعاع وليس في جهةٍ ولا مقابلة .. يهدم ما أصلُوهُ لا شكّ أنَّ ممَّا يجبُ اللهِ سبحانه وتعالى كونه بصيراً ، يتعلَّق بصرُهُ بكل موجود كما سبق (۱) ، ومعلوم استحالةُ بنية الحدقة التي جعلوها شرطاً في الرؤية عليه ، وكذا يستحيل انبعاثُ الأَشعَةِ مِنْ ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ ؛ لأنَّها أجسام لا تنفصلُ إلا مِنْ أجسام ، وكذا يستحيل أن يقابله شيء ؛ لاستحالة الجهة عليه ، فبطل بهاذا كلُّ ما أَصَلوهُ في الرؤيةِ ؛ مِنِ اشتراطِ بنية الحدقة المخصوصة ، وانبعاث الأشعة ، والمقابلة
And even if all that is granted, the vision of God most high of every existent without an eye structure (biniya), without a ray (shu'aa'), without direction (jiha), and without facing (muqabala) destroys what they established. There is no doubt that among what is necessary for God glorified and exalted is that He is seeing (basiran), His sight is related to every existent as preceded (1), and it is known the impossibility of an eye structure (hadqa) which they made a condition for vision upon Him, and likewise the emission of rays (ash'aa') from His noble essence is impossible because rays are bodies that only separate from bodies, and likewise it is impossible that anything faces Him because of the impossibility of direction (jiha) upon Him. So by this, all that they established concerning vision becomes invalid: from the condition of a specific eye structure, the emission of rays, and facing (muqabala).
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage presents a double refutation of the conditions for vision. First, Sanusi states that even if all their previous arguments were granted, the fact that God sees every existent without any eye structure, ray, direction, or facing directly undermines their entire framework. Then he elaborates: it is necessary for God to be seeing (basir) and His sight encompasses every existent. It is impossible for God to have an eye structure (hadqa) because that would imply corporeality. Also, emission of rays (ash'aa') is impossible for God because rays are bodies that separate from bodies, and God is not a body. Furthermore, facing (muqabala) is impossible because God has no direction. Therefore, all their conditions for vision—eye structure, rays, and facing—are invalid when applied to God. This establishes that vision of God does not require these physical conditions.

الحدقة (hadqa/eye)
الشعاع (shu'aa'/ray)
الجهة (jiha/direction)
المقابلة (muqabala/facing)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's vision is similar to human vision and requires physical conditions like an eye or direction.
Correction: Sanusi argues that God's sight is transcendent and not subject to any physical conditions such as eye structure, rays, or facing. These are impossible for God because they imply corporeality or direction.

Checkpoint

B) Because rays are bodies that only separate from bodies

Key Takeaway
God's vision is unconditioned by physical limitations such as eye structure, rays, or direction.

?
Sanusi proves that God's vision is unconditioned because He is not a body. Do you ever imagine God in human terms, with eyes that 'look' or a face that 'turns'? Repent from such thoughts right now and resolve to purify your concept of God from any physical limitation.
?
If God's sight is free from conditions, then nothing about you escapes His notice — not your hidden thoughts, your private sins, or your secret good deeds. Are you living as though this is true?
